GM Reference: - Setting DCs: Chapter 3 - Using clocks: Chapter 5 - Combat management: Chapter 19

Standalone Usage

This book is designed to be completely standalone:

  • For Players: All rules needed to create characters and play
  • For GMs: All mechanics needed to run sessions and resolve actions
  • Universal: Works across all genres (Cozy, Fantasy, Horror/Mystery, Modern, Sci-Fi)
  • No Dependencies: Can be used without Player’s Handbook or GM’s Toolkit (though less context)

Quick Lookup Optimized

  • Alphabetical glossaries: Tags & Conditions (Chapter 18)
  • Genre-organized: Skills and Proficiencies grouped by setting
  • Cross-genre conversion: Examples show how to adapt between genres
  • Complete index: This page for quick navigation

Cross-References to Other Books

Player’s Handbook: - Character creation process: PH Chapter 5 - PC archetypes using skills: PH Chapter 20 - PC templates with proficiencies: PH Chapter 21 - Actions and checks (overview): PH Chapter 7 - Combat basics (overview): PH Chapter 8

Tags & Conditions: Complete glossary (50+ entries)

Combat Actions: Complete reference (20+ actions)

Using This Index

Building a Character: 1. Choose genre (Chapters 7-11, 13-17) 2. Select 3-5 skills from genre chapter 3. Select 2-4 proficiencies from genre chapter 4. See Player’s Handbook for full creation process

During Play: 1. Quick lookup: Chapters 3, 18, 19 2. Skill checks: Chapter corresponding to your genre 3. Combat: Chapter 19 4. Conditions: Chapter 18

Porting Between Genres: 1. Identify character concept (core role/function) 2. Find equivalent skills in new genre chapter 3. Find equivalent proficiencies in new genre chapter 4. Swap gear for genre-appropriate items

Total: 7 sheets providing complete mechanics reference

Last Updated: November 11, 2025 Part of: 3-Book Core Structure See Also: Player’s Handbook INDEX.md, GM’s Toolkit INDEX.md

Introduction

The Mechanics Reference Guide is your comprehensive resource for the technical details of this game system. Whether you are running a cozy village festival, delving into ancient dungeons, investigating occult mysteries, navigating modern conspiracies, or exploring distant star systems, this guide provides the mechanical foundation you need.

What’s Inside

This guide organizes mechanics by genre and function, making it easy to find exactly what you need when you need it:

  • Core Mechanics (Chapters 1-5): Universal systems that apply across all genres, including DC ladders, outcome tiers, advantage and disadvantage, tags and conditions, and progress tracking through clocks.
  • Skills Reference (Chapters 6-11): Fiction-first capabilities organized by genre. Each skill package includes default actions, DC ranges, synergies, and practical usage guidance for Cozy, Fantasy, Horror/Mystery, Modern, and Sci-fi settings.
  • Proficiencies Reference (Chapters 12-17): Specialized training and mastery systems across four tiers (Novice, Trained, Expert, Master), with acquisition mechanics and downtime activities tailored to each genre.
  • Additional Reference (Chapters 18-20): Comprehensive glossaries of tags and conditions, combat mechanics, and character progression systems.

How to Use This Guide

The Mechanics Reference Guide serves two primary purposes:

Quick Reference During Play: Use the table of contents and stable anchor IDs to jump directly to specific rules. Every heading includes an anchor for deep linking, and cross-references guide you to related mechanics. Need to know the DC for a skill check? Check your genre’s skill chapter. Wondering how a condition clears? Jump to the tags and conditions glossary.

Deep Study and Preparation: Read through entire sections to understand how mechanics interconnect. The core concepts chapter explains universal principles, while genre-specific chapters show how those principles adapt to different play styles. Examples throughout demonstrate mechanics in action across varied contexts.

Integration with Other Guides

This reference works alongside three companion guides:

  • Player Guide: For player-facing procedures, character creation, and advancement from the player’s perspective.
  • GM Guide: For adjudication philosophy, scenario design, pacing tools, and running the table.
  • Storyteller Guide: For narrative techniques, genre tone, story structure, and integrating mechanics with fiction.

The Mechanics Reference Guide provides the technical backbone these guides rely on. When the Player Guide mentions skill checks, it points here for the mechanics. When the GM Guide discusses pacing, it references clocks from this guide. When the Storyteller Guide explores genre tone, it links to genre-specific skills and proficiencies.

Navigating Mechanics

The system’s mechanics interconnect in deliberate ways:

Skills define what characters can do. They are fiction-first capabilities with specific DC ranges and synergies. Your character’s skills determine which actions are possible and how difficult they are.

Proficiencies represent specialized training that enhances skills. A character with the Baker proficiency gains advantages when using Baking or related skills. Proficiencies also unlock downtime activities and narrative opportunities.

Tags describe environmental and situational factors. A Dim tag might grant advantage on Stealth checks while raising the DC for Perception. Tags shape position and effect, making the world mechanically consequential.

Conditions represent character states that persist across actions. Exhausted, Frightened, or Poisoned conditions modify what characters can effectively do until cleared through rest, treatment, or other means.

Clocks track progress toward goals or mounting threats. They turn abstract time pressure into concrete segments, letting you see how close you are to completion or catastrophe.

These elements work together to create a flexible, fiction-first system. The DC ladder and outcome tiers provide consistent resolution, while genre-specific content ensures the mechanics serve your story’s tone and priorities.

The chapters ahead unpack these systems in detail. Start with the core concepts to build your foundation, then explore genre-specific chapters as your table’s needs evolve.

Core Mechanical Concepts

These fundamental mechanics apply across all genres and provide the foundation for every check, contest, and resolution in the game. Understanding these concepts allows you to adjudicate situations confidently and consistently.

Rolling Dice

This game uses 4d6 (four six-sided dice) as its core resolution mechanic. At its heart: Roll 4 dice, add your bonus, compare to a target number (DC).

Basic Roll

  1. Roll 4d6: Roll four six-sided dice simultaneously.
  2. Sum all dice: Add all four dice together (no dropping or keeping subsets on a basic roll).
  3. Add bonus: Add your relevant attribute bonus or skill bonus.
  4. Compare to DC: Check your total against the Difficulty Class (DC).
  5. Calculate margin: Subtract DC from your total (Roll - DC = Margin).
  6. Determine outcome: Use margin to find your outcome tier (see below).

Example: You attempt to climb a rough wall (Athletics, DC 16, +3 bonus). You roll 4d6 and get 3, 5, 2, 4 = 14. Add your +3 bonus = 17 total. Margin: 17 - 16 = +1. This is a Full Success (margin ≥0).

Key Statistics: - Average roll: 14 (without bonuses) - Range: 4 to 24 (without bonuses) - Distribution: Bell curve centered on 14

Outcome Tiers

Your margin determines how well you succeeded or how badly you failed:

Margin Outcome What Happens in Story
≥ +5 Critical Success You succeed spectacularly with extra benefit. The lock opens silently and you find a hidden compartment. The crowd rallies and spreads your message beyond the square.
≥ 0 Full Success You succeed cleanly. The lock opens. The crowd rallies. The enemy falls.
-1 to -2 Partial Success You succeed, but at a cost or with complication. The lock opens but your tools break. The crowd rallies but demands a favor.
≤ -3 Failure You don’t succeed, and trouble grows. The lock stays shut and guards approach. The crowd turns hostile. The enemy escapes.
≤ -7 or all 1s Critical Failure A disaster occurs—the worst possible result. The lock jams permanently and alerts guards. The crowd riots. The enemy counterattacks viciously.

Special Rule: Rolling all 1s (1, 1, 1, 1) is always a Critical Failure regardless of bonuses or margin.

GM Guidance: Frame outcomes in narrative terms first, mechanics second. A Partial Success isn’t “you kind of succeed”—it’s “you succeed, but what’s the complication?” A Failure isn’t “you fail”—it’s “you fail, and how does the situation escalate?”

Advantage

When you have advantage, roll extra dice and keep the best 4. Favorable circumstances, clever preparation, superior positioning, or helpful tags grant advantage.

Advantage comes in two levels:

  • +1 Advantage: Roll 5d6, keep the best 4 dice.
  • +2 Advantage: Roll 6d6, keep the best 4 dice.

Example (+1 Advantage): You attempt to track footprints with Survival, DC 16, +2 bonus. You have advantage from fresh mud. Roll 5d6: 2, 4, 5, 3, 6. Keep the best 4: 4, 5, 3, 6 = 18. Add +2 bonus = 20 total. Margin: +4 (Full Success, nearly Critical).

Example (+2 Advantage): Same tracking attempt, but you also have a trained hound helping. Roll 6d6: 2, 4, 5, 3, 6, 1. Keep the best 4: 4, 5, 3, 6 = 18. Add +2 bonus = 20 total. Margin: +4 (Full Success).

Stacking Rule: Multiple sources of advantage do NOT stack beyond ±2. You either have +1 advantage, +2 advantage, or no advantage.

Disadvantage

When you have disadvantage, roll extra dice and keep the worst 4. Hostile conditions, penalties from conditions, hindering tags, or poor positioning impose disadvantage.

Disadvantage comes in two levels:

  • -1 Disadvantage: Roll 5d6, keep the worst 4 dice.
  • -2 Disadvantage: Roll 6d6, keep the worst 4 dice.

Example (-1 Disadvantage): You attempt to climb a slick wall (Athletics, DC 16, +3 bonus). The Slick tag imposes disadvantage. Roll 5d6: 2, 4, 5, 3, 6. Keep the worst 4: 2, 4, 5, 3 = 14. Add +3 bonus = 17 total. Margin: +1 (Full Success, but barely).

Example (-2 Disadvantage): Same climb, but you’re also Exhausted (imposes additional disadvantage level). Roll 6d6: 2, 4, 5, 3, 6, 1. Keep the worst 4: 2, 4, 3, 1 = 10. Add +3 bonus = 13 total. Margin: -3 (Failure).

Stacking Rule: Multiple sources of disadvantage do NOT stack beyond ±2. You either have -1 disadvantage, -2 disadvantage, or no disadvantage.

Advantage and Disadvantage Interaction

If you have both advantage and disadvantage from different sources, they cancel each other out:

  • +1 Advantage + -1 Disadvantage = Roll normally (4d6)
  • +2 Advantage + -1 Disadvantage = +1 Advantage (5d6, keep best 4)
  • +1 Advantage + -2 Disadvantage = -1 Disadvantage (5d6, keep worst 4)
  • +2 Advantage + -2 Disadvantage = Roll normally (4d6)

Example: You climb a rough wall (Elevated tag grants +1 advantage) while Exhausted (condition imposes -1 disadvantage). They cancel out, so you roll normally (4d6).

GM Guidance: Track advantage/disadvantage sources explicitly. When multiple sources apply, net them out before rolling. This keeps the math simple and the game moving quickly.

Worked Examples

Example 1: Picking a Lock (No Modifiers)

  • Skill: Thievery, DC 14, +2 bonus
  • Roll: 4d6 → 3, 3, 5, 4 = 15
  • Total: 15 + 2 = 17
  • Margin: 17 - 14 = +3 (Full Success)
  • Outcome: The lock clicks open smoothly. You slip inside undetected.

Example 2: Persuading a Hostile Merchant (Disadvantage)

  • Skill: Persuasion, DC 16, +1 bonus
  • Situation: Merchant is Suspicious (condition grants -1 disadvantage)
  • Roll: 5d6 → 2, 6, 4, 5, 3. Keep worst 4: 2, 4, 5, 3 = 14
  • Total: 14 + 1 = 15
  • Margin: 15 - 16 = -1 (Partial Success)
  • Outcome: The merchant agrees, but demands payment up front and watches you closely.

Example 3: Tracking Through a Forest (Advantage)

  • Skill: Survival, DC 18, +3 bonus
  • Situation: Fresh mud grants +1 advantage
  • Roll: 5d6 → 1, 3, 6, 4, 5. Keep best 4: 3, 6, 4, 5 = 18
  • Total: 18 + 3 = 21
  • Margin: 21 - 18 = +3 (Full Success)
  • Outcome: You follow the trail confidently, noticing details others would miss (footprint depth suggests injury, pace indicates exhaustion).

Example 4: Critical Success

  • Skill: Investigation, DC 16, +4 bonus
  • Roll: 4d6 → 6, 6, 5, 5 = 22
  • Total: 22 + 4 = 26
  • Margin: 26 - 16 = +10 (Critical Success)
  • Outcome: Not only do you find the hidden compartment, you discover a second secret passage and recognize the maker’s mark as belonging to a legendary craftsman—granting a new lead.

Example 5: Critical Failure (All 1s)

  • Skill: Stealth, DC 14, +3 bonus
  • Roll: 4d6 → 1, 1, 1, 1 = 4 (all 1s)
  • Total: 4 + 3 = 7
  • Margin: 7 - 14 = -7 (would be Failure, but all 1s = Critical Failure)
  • Outcome: You trip over a loose stone, sending it clattering down the corridor. Guards immediately converge on your position, and the alarm bell rings throughout the complex.

Summary

  • Core Roll: 4d6 + bonus vs DC
  • Average: 14 (before bonuses)
  • Advantage: Roll 5d6 or 6d6, keep best 4
  • Disadvantage: Roll 5d6 or 6d6, keep worst 4
  • Outcome Tiers: Crit Success (≥+5), Full (≥0), Partial (-1 to -2), Fail (≤-3), Crit Fail (≤-7 or all 1s)
  • Stacking: Advantage/disadvantage net out, cap at ±2 levels

DC Ladder

The Difficulty Class (DC) ladder establishes target numbers for skill checks. The ladder balances accessibility with challenge across a 12-22 range:

  • DC 12: Simple tasks with some risk of failure. Picking a common lock, tracking obvious footprints, or persuading a friendly NPC.
  • DC 14: Routine challenges requiring competence. Climbing a rough wall with handholds, identifying common poisons, or calming a spooked animal.
  • DC 16: Standard difficulty for trained individuals. Disabling a well-made trap, navigating through a storm, or negotiating a fair deal with a neutral party.
  • DC 18: Difficult tasks demanding skill and focus. Forging convincing documents, treating exotic toxins, or infiltrating a guarded facility.
  • DC 20: Very difficult challenges at the edge of normal capability. Picking an arcane lock, tracking through a blizzard, or swaying a hostile crowd.
  • DC 22: Exceptional feats requiring expertise and favorable conditions. Disarming ancient mechanisms, predicting a rare celestial event, or persuading someone to betray core beliefs.

GM Guidance: Start with DC 16 as your baseline for “this requires a roll.” Adjust up or down based on circumstances. Tags, conditions, and proficiencies modify DCs or grant advantage/disadvantage rather than stacking endless modifiers. Keep the math simple.

Example: Picking a lock starts at DC 14 for a common lock. An Ancient tag might raise it to DC 16. A Fine Tools proficiency might grant advantage instead of lowering the DC. A Dim tag might create complications without changing the target number.

Outcome Tiers

Every check resolves into outcome tiers based on your margin (Roll - DC). See Rolling Dice above for the complete outcome tier table and margin thresholds.

Critical Success (Margin ≥+5): You succeed spectacularly with extra benefit. The lock opens silently and you find a hidden compartment. The crowd rallies and spreads your message beyond the square.

Full Success (Margin ≥0): You achieve your goal cleanly. The lock opens, the crowd rallies, the enemy falls. Success means progress without compromise. The fiction moves in your favor.

Partial Success (Margin -1 to -2): You achieve your goal but with a cost, complication, or lesser effect. The lock opens but your tools break. The crowd rallies but demands a favor. The enemy falls but reinforcements heard the fight. Partials keep the story moving while maintaining tension.

Failure (Margin ≤-3): You don’t achieve your goal, and the situation complicates. The lock stays shut and the patrol rounds the corner. The crowd turns hostile. The enemy escapes and raises the alarm. Failures introduce new problems, not dead ends.

Critical Failure (Margin ≤-7 or all 1s): A disaster occurs—the worst possible result. The lock jams permanently and alerts guards. The crowd riots against you. The enemy counterattacks with devastating force.

GM Guidance: Frame outcomes in terms of goals and complications, not just success/failure. A Partial on a Persuasion check doesn’t mean “half-convinced”—it means the NPC agrees but wants something in return, or agrees publicly while planning betrayal. Make complications interesting and consequential.

Example: A character attempts to sneak past guards (Stealth, DC 16). On a Success, they pass unnoticed. On a Partial, they pass but a Suspicion clock advances by 1 segment, or they leave evidence behind. On a Failure, a guard spots them and sounds the alarm.

Advantage and Disadvantage

Advantage and disadvantage modify how you roll without changing the target DC. See Rolling Dice above for complete mechanics and examples.

Advantage: Roll 5d6 (±1) or 6d6 (±2) and keep the best 4 dice. Favorable circumstances, clever preparation, or helpful tags grant advantage.

Disadvantage: Roll 5d6 (±1) or 6d6 (±2) and keep the worst 4 dice. Hostile conditions, penalties from conditions, or hindering tags impose disadvantage.

Stacking: Multiple sources of advantage or disadvantage do not stack beyond ±2 levels. You either have +1/+2 advantage, -1/-2 disadvantage, or neither. If you have both, they net out (e.g., +1 advantage and -1 disadvantage cancel to a normal 4d6 roll).

GM Guidance: Use advantage and disadvantage for circumstantial modifiers instead of adjusting DCs. A character with the high ground gets advantage on ranged attacks rather than reducing the DC. A Frightened character has disadvantage on opposed checks rather than facing a higher DC.

Example: A character climbs a slick wall (Athletics, DC 16). The Slick tag imposes -1 disadvantage, so they roll 5d6 and keep the worst 4 dice. If they had a rope (providing +1 advantage) and the wall was still slick, the advantage and disadvantage cancel, and they roll normally (4d6).

Position and Effect

Position and effect describe risk and impact, adding texture to resolution:

Position measures what you risk:

  • High Position: Minimal risk. Failure means setbacks, not disaster. Researching in a safe library, negotiating from a position of strength.
  • Standard Position: Moderate risk. Failure brings complications. Sneaking through patrols, treating injuries in the field.
  • Low Position: Serious risk. Failure escalates dramatically. Defusing a trap under fire, fleeing a collapsing ruin.

Effect measures what you achieve:

  • Great Effect: Significant progress or impact. Clear 2 clock segments, achieve multiple goals, or create lasting change.
  • Standard Effect: Meaningful progress. Clear 1 clock segment, achieve your immediate goal.
  • Limited Effect: Minor progress. Partial clock advance, achieve part of your goal.

GM Guidance: Set position and effect before the roll based on the situation, approach, and tools available. A well-prepared infiltration has higher position than a desperate improvisation. A ritual with rare components has greater effect than one with common substitutes.

Example: A character tries to sway a neutral merchant (Persuasion, DC 16). Standard position (failure means they refuse and grow suspicious) and standard effect (success means they agree to help). If the character had leverage from a previous favor, they might have high position. If they were asking for something exceptional, it might be limited effect.

Checks and Contests

Checks resolve actions against static opposition. Roll against the DC and consult outcome tiers.

Contests resolve opposed actions. Both parties roll their relevant skill. Higher result wins. Margin of success determines degree of victory:

  • Margin 1-3: Narrow victory. You succeed but barely.
  • Margin 4-6: Clear victory. You succeed convincingly.
  • Margin 7+: Decisive victory. You dominate the contest.

Ties: In contests, ties result in a Partial outcome for both parties. Neither achieves their goal cleanly, but the situation shifts.

GM Guidance: Use checks for most situations. Reserve contests for direct opposition between characters or when both parties actively resist. A character sneaking past a guard uses a check (Stealth vs DC), not a contest, unless the guard is actively searching for them.

Example - Check: A character picks a lock (Thievery, DC 16). They roll once against the DC.

Example - Contest: Two characters race to grab an artifact. Both roll Athletics. If one rolls 18 and the other rolls 14, the first character wins with a margin of 4—a clear victory. They reach the artifact first and have time to secure it before the other arrives.

Worked Examples

Example 1: Infiltrating a Castle

Situation: A character sneaks through a castle during a festival. The halls are Crowded (tag) and Dim (tag), but guards have Alert (tag) status.

Skill: Stealth, DC 16 (base, +2 from Alert) Position: Standard (failure means confrontation) Effect: Standard (success means reaching the target undetected) Modifiers: Advantage from Dim and Crowded tags (they cancel the +2 DC from Alert, so GM rules advantage applies and DC stays 16)

Outcome - Success (18): The character weaves through festival crowds and shadows, reaching their target with guards none the wiser.

Outcome - Partial (15): The character reaches the target, but a Suspicion clock advances by 1 segment. Guards noticed something but haven’t pinpointed the threat.

Outcome - Failure (12): A guard spots suspicious movement. The character must decide: flee, fight, or attempt a fast-talk (new check, possibly at disadvantage).

Example 2: Treating Poison

Situation: A character treats a companion suffering from a Poisoned condition (disadvantage on strenuous actions).

Skill: Medicine, DC 16 Position: Standard (failure means the condition persists) Effect: Standard (success clears the condition) Modifiers: None, unless the character has First Aid proficiency (grants advantage)

Outcome - Success (17): The poison is neutralized. The Poisoned condition clears immediately.

Outcome - Partial (14): The poison’s effects lessen. The condition will clear after the next scene, or the character can try again after gathering better supplies.

Outcome - Failure (11): The poison persists. The Poisoned condition remains, and the character may need to seek professional help or rare antidotes.

Example 3: Contested Negotiation

Situation: Two merchants bid for exclusive rights to a trade route. Both use Negotiation in a contest.

Skill: Negotiation (contest) Position: High for both (failure means the other wins, but no other risk) Effect: Great (winner secures exclusive rights)

Roll: Merchant A rolls 19. Merchant B rolls 13. Margin of 6.

Outcome: Merchant A wins clearly, securing the exclusive rights and favorable terms. Merchant B walks away with minor concessions but misses the primary opportunity.

GM Usage Notes

  • Default to DC 16 for standard challenges. Adjust by ±2 for easier or harder tasks. Rarely go below 12 or above 22.
  • Use advantage and disadvantage instead of accumulating +/- modifiers. Keep the math simple and the rolls quick.
  • Set position and effect explicitly before rolling to calibrate risk and reward.
  • Frame Partials as “yes, but” and Failures as “no, and” to keep the story moving.
  • For contests, describe margin of victory in narrative terms: narrow, clear, or decisive.

These core concepts form the mechanical foundation for all resolution in the game. The following chapters build on this framework with specific applications, genre variations, and detailed reference materials.

Check Resolution and Outcomes

This chapter details the procedures for resolving checks and contests, interpreting outcomes across different genres, and handling special cases that arise during play.

Rolling Procedures

When to Roll

Call for a roll when all three conditions are true:

  1. Uncertainty: The outcome is not predetermined. A trained character performing a routine task in ideal conditions does not roll.
  2. Consequence: Failure introduces complications or costs. If failure just means “try again,” don’t roll—either the task succeeds with time, or it’s impossible.
  3. Agency: The character has meaningful influence over the outcome. Don’t roll for things completely outside the character’s control.

GM Guidance: Default to “say yes” when possible. Reserve rolls for moments of tension, risk, or meaningful choice. A baker character making simple bread does not roll Baking unless the stakes are high (festival competition, royal banquet, limited ingredients).

When Not to Roll

Skip the roll in these situations:

  • Trivial Tasks: A skilled character performing basic tasks in their specialty. A character with Thievery doesn’t roll to unlock a simple door with proper tools and time.
  • Impossible Tasks: No amount of luck makes this achievable. Don’t ask for a roll if failure is the only option—just say “no, and here’s why.”
  • No Consequences: Nothing changes whether they succeed or fail. If they can keep trying indefinitely, resolve it as a time cost instead.

Example (Cozy): A gardener character wants to plant vegetables. This is routine for them, so no roll. If they want to cure a mysterious blight threatening the harvest, that’s uncertain and consequential—roll Gardening, DC 16.

Example (Sci-fi): A starship pilot wants to dock at a station in calm conditions. Routine, no roll. If they’re docking during a debris storm while pursued, that’s uncertain and consequential—roll Starship Piloting, DC 18.

Interpreting Results by Outcome Tier

Each outcome tier should drive the fiction forward in genre-appropriate ways.

Success

You achieve your goal cleanly. No compromises, no costs. The lock opens, the crowd believes you, the ship docks safely. Success means progress.

Fantasy Example: Sneaking through a dungeon (Stealth, DC 16). Success means you reach your destination undetected. Guards remain unaware. You control the next moment.

Horror Example: Investigating a crime scene (Investigation, DC 16). Success reveals critical clues—the murder weapon, timeline inconsistencies, a hidden witness. You have actionable leads.

Modern Example: Hacking a secure system (Hacking, DC 18). Success grants access and covers your tracks. The system shows no evidence of intrusion.

Partial Success

You achieve your goal but with a cost, complication, or reduced effect. Partials keep momentum while maintaining tension. Frame them as “yes, but”:

  • Cost: You succeed but expend resources, tools, time, or goodwill.
  • Complication: You succeed but create a new problem or advance a threat clock.
  • Lesser Effect: You achieve part of your goal or temporary success.

Fantasy Example: Picking a lock (Thievery, DC 16). Partial means the lock opens, but your tools break. You’ll need new tools before attempting similar tasks.

Horror Example: Calming a panicked witness (Psychology, DC 14). Partial means they calm enough to talk, but they’re fixated on you now. They’ll seek you out repeatedly, demanding reassurance.

Modern Example: Negotiating a crisis (Negotiation, DC 18). Partial means the immediate threat de-escalates, but you made promises you’ll need to keep. Those promises will complicate future scenes.

Failure

You don’t achieve your goal, and the situation complicates. Frame failures as “no, and”:

  • No Progress: The goal remains unachieved.
  • Complication: A new problem emerges—threats advance, resources diminish, positions worsen.

Failures should never be dead ends. They introduce new choices and shift the situation.

Fantasy Example: Convincing a hostile duke (Persuasion, DC 20). Failure means he not only refuses, but now views you as a threat. Guards escort you out, and your access to the court is revoked.

Horror Example: Evading a pursuer in fog (Stealth, DC 16). Failure means they track you to your safehouse. Now they know where you’re hiding, and you must flee or fortify.

Sci-fi Example: Repairing a failing life support system (Systems Engineering, DC 18). Failure means the system continues failing. You have one more scene before the air runs out, and you’ll need a different approach or outside help.

Group Checks

When multiple characters work toward a shared goal, use group checks to streamline resolution:

Procedure: 1. Identify the leader—the character most qualified or driving the effort. 2. The leader rolls the check against the DC. 3. Each assistant can provide advantage by contributing meaningfully. Describe how they help. 4. Assistants share in the consequences of Partials and Failures.

GM Guidance: Limit assistants to those who can contribute meaningfully. Don’t let every character help with a Hacking check if only one has the skill. Assistants take on risk—if the check results in a Partial or Failure, they share the cost.

Fantasy Example: A party climbs a cliff (Athletics, DC 16). The strongest character leads. Another character with rope provides advantage. On a Partial, both characters reach the top, but the rope frays and will break if used again.

Cozy Example: Organizing a village festival (Event Planning, DC 16). The character with the skill leads. Assistants provide advantage by rallying volunteers. On a Partial, the festival happens, but one volunteer group feels overworked and harbors resentment.

Modern Example: Coordinating a rescue operation (Logistics, DC 18). The logistics expert leads. Field operatives provide advantage by scouting routes. On a Failure, the operation is delayed, and the rescue window narrows.

Contests

Contests resolve direct opposition between characters or NPCs. Both sides roll relevant skills. Higher result wins.

Procedure: 1. Identify the opposing skills. Both parties roll. 2. Compare results. Higher roll wins. 3. Calculate margin: winner’s roll minus loser’s roll. 4. Interpret margin narratively: - Margin 1-3: Narrow victory. You succeed, but barely. - Margin 4-6: Clear victory. You succeed convincingly. - Margin 7+: Decisive victory. You dominate completely.

Ties: Both achieve Partial outcomes. Neither gets what they wanted cleanly. Introduce complications for both sides or escalate the situation.

Fantasy Example: Two knights joust (Athletics contest). Knight A rolls 19, Knight B rolls 14. Margin of 5. Knight A lands a clear hit, unseating Knight B without injury. Knight A wins the round decisively.

Horror Example: A detective and killer play a game of cat-and-mouse (Investigation vs Stealth contest). Detective rolls 17, Killer rolls 18. Margin of 1. The killer evades detection by a hair, leaving behind a tantalizing clue that taunts the detective.

Sci-fi Example: Two ships race through an asteroid field (Starship Piloting contest). Pilot A rolls 16, Pilot B rolls 16. Tie. Both ships navigate successfully but dangerously close to collision. A debris cloud now separates them, forcing new tactical decisions.

Edge Cases

Critical Success

Some tables use critical success (rolling maximum on the die) for exceptional outcomes. This is optional.

If Using Criticals: A critical success achieves the goal with an additional benefit—extra effect, bonus information, or eliminating a threat. Keep it narrative and avoid power creep.

Example: A critical success on Hacking not only breaches the system but also plants evidence implicating someone else, misdirecting any investigation.

Critical Failure

Similarly, critical failure (rolling minimum on the die) can introduce dramatic complications. This is also optional.

If Using Criticals: A critical failure introduces a severe complication beyond normal failure—broken tools, injury, or exposure. Use sparingly to avoid punishing players excessively.

Example: A critical failure on a Stealth check might mean you’re not just spotted—you’re recognized by someone who has reason to hunt you.

Retries

Can a character retry a failed check? It depends:

  • New Approach: If circumstances change or the character tries a fundamentally different method, allow a new roll.
  • Same Approach: If nothing changes, retrying doesn’t make sense. The lock is still locked, the guard is still suspicious. Failure stands unless the situation evolves.

GM Guidance: Failures should complicate, not block. If a character fails to pick a lock, introduce guards, time pressure, or a need to find another route. Don’t let the game stall on one roll.

Example: A character fails to persuade a merchant (Persuasion, DC 16). They can’t just keep asking. But if they return with leverage (a letter of recommendation, evidence of profit, or a bribe), that’s a new approach—new roll.

Multiple Obstacles

When a character faces a series of related challenges (climbing multiple cliff faces, bypassing several security checkpoints), don’t call for a roll at every step:

  • Single Roll: One check represents the entire sequence. Interpret outcome tiers accordingly.
  • Clock: Use a clock to track progress through multiple obstacles. Each successful check advances the clock.

Example: Infiltrating a high-security facility involves bypassing guards, spoofing cameras, and cracking a door lock. Use a 6-segment clock. Each successful check advances the clock by 1 (or 2 on exceptional success). Partials advance but trigger complications. Failures don’t advance and add obstacles.

GM Usage Notes

  • Call for rolls only when uncertainty, consequence, and agency align.
  • Use Partials to maintain momentum while introducing tension. “Yes, but” keeps the story moving.
  • Failures complicate and shift situations. Never let a single failed roll become a dead end.
  • Group checks streamline collaborative efforts but distribute risk.
  • Contests add drama to direct opposition. Use margin to calibrate narrative impact.
  • Retries require changed circumstances. Don’t let players grind the same roll.

These resolution procedures ensure that dice inform the story without dictating it. The next chapter explores how tags and conditions add texture and consequence to these mechanics.

Tags and Conditions Overview

Tags and conditions add texture and consequence to the game world. Tags describe environmental and situational factors that shape scenes. Conditions represent character states that persist and modify capabilities. Together, they make fiction mechanically meaningful.

What Tags Do

Tags are descriptors applied to scenes, locations, objects, or situations. They modify how checks resolve by shifting DCs, granting advantage or disadvantage, or altering position and effect.

Core Function: Tags translate fiction into mechanical adjustments. A Dim room isn’t just described as dark—the Dim tag grants advantage on Stealth checks and imposes disadvantage on ranged attacks beyond short range. The world’s details shape what’s possible.

Common Applications: - Environmental Tags: Describe physical spaces (Dim, Elevated, Slick, Cramped) - Situational Tags: Describe circumstances (Crowded, Alert, Hazardous, Obscuring) - Emotional Tags: Describe social atmosphere (Tense, Festive, Solemn)

GM Guidance: Apply tags when they meaningfully affect actions. Don’t tag everything—reserve tags for details that create mechanical texture. A room is just a room until the Dim lighting or Slick floor matters to what a character attempts.

Tag Categories

Environmental Tags modify physical space:

  • Dim: Advantage on Stealth, disadvantage on Perception and ranged attacks beyond short range. Duration: scene or until light sources introduced.
  • Elevated: Advantage on ranged attacks from higher ground, disadvantage on melee attacks from lower ground. Duration: positional.
  • Slick: Disadvantage on fast movement and Athletics checks. Duration: scene or until surface dries/clears.
  • Cramped: Limits movement, may impose disadvantage on combat actions requiring space. Duration: locational.

Situational Tags modify circumstances:

  • Crowded: +2 DC to move quickly, advantage on Stealth and blending. Duration: scene or until crowd disperses.
  • Alert: NPCs actively searching, +2 DC to Stealth-based actions. Duration: scene or until threat passes.
  • Hazardous: Partials or failures may inflict conditions (Poisoned, Bleeding, Exhausted). Duration: scene or until hazard removed.
  • Obscuring: Advantage on contested checks where visibility matters, disadvantage on Perception. Duration: scene or until obscuration clears.

Interaction Tags modify how tags combine:

  • Fragile: Structures or cover break easily. Partials may destroy cover or create new hazards. Duration: until broken.
  • Sacred Ground: Advantage against undead or malevolent forces; may impose restrictions on certain actions. Duration: locational or until desecrated.

For a comprehensive tag glossary with genre-specific entries, see Chapter 18.

How Tags Affect Checks

Tags modify checks in three primary ways:

1. Advantage or Disadvantage: A Dim tag grants advantage on Stealth. A Slick tag imposes disadvantage on fast movement. When multiple tags apply, they may cancel or stack, depending on GM judgment.

2. DC Adjustments: Some tags shift DCs directly. An Alert tag might add +2 to Stealth DCs. A Helpful tag (good gossip in a cozy village) might reduce DC by 2 for gathering information.

3. Position and Effect: Tags shift risk and impact. A Hazardous tag might lower position (failure escalates to injury). A Sacred Ground tag might increase effect (rituals are more powerful here).

Example (Fantasy): A character sneaks through a castle during a storm. The storm creates Obscuring (rain and wind) and Slick (wet stones) tags. The Obscuring tag grants advantage on Stealth, while Slick imposes disadvantage on Athletics if they need to climb. The GM rules that the advantage and disadvantage cancel for Stealth checks involving movement, but pure hiding benefits from Obscuring without penalty.

Example (Horror): A detective searches a crime scene with a Dim Gaslight tag and Blood Scent tag (Hazardous). Dim grants advantage on moving unseen but imposes disadvantage on Perception checks to find small clues. The Blood Scent tag means a failed check might attract predators or scavengers, lowering position.

What Conditions Do

Conditions represent persistent character states that modify capabilities. Unlike tags, which describe environments, conditions attach to characters and follow them between scenes until cleared.

Core Function: Conditions create mechanical consequences for narrative events. A character who falls into a trap becomes Restrained. A character who witnesses horror becomes Frightened. A character poisoned by a trap becomes Poisoned. These states persist and shape subsequent actions.

Common Applications: - Physical Conditions: Poisoned, Bleeding, Exhausted, Restrained, Prone, Stunned - Mental Conditions: Frightened, Distracted, Obsessed, Shaken - Magical Conditions: Hexed, Cursed, Mana-Drained (genre-dependent)

GM Guidance: Apply conditions when fiction demands lasting impact. A scary moment might impose Frightened. A brutal fight might impose Exhausted. A trap’s poison imposes Poisoned. Conditions aren’t punishment—they’re narrative and mechanical consequences that create texture and challenge.

Common Conditions

Poisoned: Disadvantage on strenuous physical actions. Duration: until treated (Medicine DC 16) or rest. Clearing: Successful treatment, antidote, or full rest.

Exhausted: Disadvantage on complex actions requiring focus or stamina. Duration: rest. Clearing: Full rest with food and safety.

Frightened: Disadvantage on actions opposing the source of fear. Duration: scene or until source removed. Clearing: Distance from threat, calming support, or overcoming fear narratively.

Restrained: Cannot move freely; disadvantage on actions requiring mobility. Duration: until freed. Clearing: Break restraints (Athletics or Thievery check) or assistance.

Bleeding: Lose resources or effectiveness over time; risk escalation if untreated. Duration: until treated. Clearing: First aid (DC 14) or medical attention.

Weakened: Reduced effect on physical actions. Duration: rest or treatment. Clearing: Rest, medicine, or magical healing (genre-dependent).

For a comprehensive conditions glossary with genre-specific entries, see Chapter 18.

Condition Duration and Clearing

Conditions persist based on their nature and narrative context:

Scene Duration: Lasts until the current scene ends. Frightened often clears when the threat passes. Distracted clears when focus returns.

Clock Duration: Clears when a clock advances or completes. A Hex Mark might be tied to a 3-segment clock, clearing when the clock fills.

Rest Duration: Clears after a full rest. Exhausted and Poisoned typically require rest to clear.

Explicit Clear: Requires specific action. Restrained requires breaking free. Cursed requires a ritual or dispelling.

GM Guidance: Match duration to narrative weight. Minor conditions (Distracted, Irritated) clear quickly. Major conditions (Cursed, Poisoned, Exhausted) require significant effort or time. Don’t let conditions linger without purpose—they should drive decisions, not just accumulate.

Example (Cozy): A character becomes Exhausted after organizing a multi-day festival. The condition imposes disadvantage on complex tasks. It clears after a full night’s rest and a hearty meal. The condition reinforces the cost of their effort without punishing them long-term.

Example (Modern): A character exposed to Tear Gas becomes Weakened (disadvantage on precise tasks). The condition lasts for the scene and clears after moving to fresh air and rinsing their eyes. Quick resolution reflects the temporary nature of the effect.

Example (Sci-fi): A character suffers Radiation Burst exposure, becoming Weakened. The condition ties to a 3-segment clock. Each medical treatment advances the clock by 1. The condition clears when the clock fills, representing the time and care needed for recovery.

Interaction Examples

Tags and conditions interact to create layered mechanical situations:

Example 1: Fantasy Combat in a Burning Building

Tags: Hazardous (fire), Fragile (burning beams), Obscuring (smoke) Conditions: Character is Exhausted from a previous fight

Situation: The character attempts to rescue someone from the burning building.

  • Obscuring Tag: Grants advantage on avoiding enemy notice but imposes disadvantage on Perception to find the victim.
  • Hazardous Tag: Partials or failures risk adding Weakened or Bleeding conditions.
  • Fragile Tag: Partials might collapse beams, changing the environment mid-scene.
  • Exhausted Condition: Disadvantage on the strenuous Athletics check to navigate debris.

Outcome - Partial: The character finds the victim but a beam collapses, blocking the original exit. They must find a new route (new check) or break through the debris.

Example 2: Horror Investigation in Fog

Tags: Dim Gaslight, Thick Fog (Obscuring + Slick), Blood Scent (Hazardous) Conditions: Character is Frightened from a previous encounter

Situation: The character investigates a murder scene at night in heavy fog.

  • Dim + Obscuring Tags: Advantage on Stealth, disadvantage on Perception.
  • Slick Tag: Disadvantage on fast movement if pursued.
  • Hazardous Tag: Blood Scent attracts predators on failed checks.
  • Frightened Condition: Disadvantage on actions if predators appear.

Outcome - Failure: The character’s nerves get the better of them. They misread a clue and alert something lurking in the fog. Now they must flee (with Slick imposing disadvantage on movement) or confront the threat (with Frightened imposing disadvantage).

Example 3: Sci-fi EVA Repair

Tags: Hard Vacuum (Hazardous), Microgravity (Cramped) Conditions: Character is Weakened from previous radiation exposure

Situation: The character must patch a hull breach before air runs out.

  • Hard Vacuum Tag: Partials or failures risk escalating to life-threatening situations.
  • Microgravity Tag: Disadvantage on fast movement; requires careful maneuvering.
  • Weakened Condition: Reduced effect on physical actions; may take longer.

Outcome - Success: The character patches the breach cleanly despite the hostile conditions, demonstrating skill under pressure.

Outcome - Partial: The patch holds, but the character’s limited mobility (Microgravity + Weakened) means they exhausted their spare materials. Future repairs will require different solutions.

GM Usage Notes

  • Apply tags when they create meaningful mechanical texture. Not every room needs tags.
  • Use conditions to reinforce narrative consequences. Frightened after horror, Exhausted after exertion, Poisoned after traps.
  • Match condition duration to narrative weight. Minor conditions clear quickly; major conditions drive decisions.
  • Tags and conditions interact to create layered situations. Let them combine naturally to reflect complex fiction.
  • Keep the math simple: advantage/disadvantage or ±2 DC. Avoid stacking endless modifiers.

For comprehensive tag and condition lists organized by genre, see Chapter 18: Tags and Conditions Glossary. That chapter provides alphabetical entries with specific effects, durations, and clearing mechanics for quick reference during play.

Clocks and Progress Tracking

Clocks are segmented progress trackers that turn abstract goals and threats into concrete, visible progress. They answer the question “how close are we?” for long-term efforts, mounting dangers, and time-sensitive challenges.

What Clocks Are

A clock is a circle divided into segments—typically 4, 6, or 8. Each segment represents a unit of progress toward completion. When all segments fill, the clock completes, and the associated event resolves.

4-Segment Clocks: Quick progress for minor goals or imminent threats. Use for short-term projects, fast-moving dangers, or simple objectives.

6-Segment Clocks: Standard progress for moderate goals or medium-term threats. Use for investigations, crafting projects, or threats that unfold over multiple scenes.

8-Segment Clocks: Extended progress for major goals or long-term threats. Use for grand rituals, epic journeys, faction projects, or slow-building dangers.

GM Guidance: Choose clock size based on narrative weight and desired pacing. A 4-segment clock completes quickly, creating urgency. An 8-segment clock unfolds over multiple sessions, providing long-term structure. You can adjust clock sizes mid-game if pacing feels off—narrative needs trump initial choices.

When to Use Clocks

Clocks work best for situations where progress is incremental and time matters:

Long-Term Goals: Character projects that unfold over multiple scenes. Building a reputation, researching ancient lore, or repairing a damaged ship. Each successful action advances the clock.

Mounting Threats: Dangers that escalate over time. Pursuing enemies closing in, plagues spreading, or structural collapses. Clocks tick forward based on time passing or failed checks.

Ticking Dangers: Time-sensitive situations with a deadline. Rituals completing, bombs counting down, or rescue windows closing. Clocks advance on a schedule—every scene, every hour, or at narrative beats.

Faction Projects: Off-screen activities by NPCs or organizations. Rival factions building power, political movements gaining momentum, or enemies preparing attacks. Clocks represent background developments that shape the world.

Complex Tasks: Multi-step endeavors too complex for a single check. Organizing festivals, infiltrating organizations, or navigating bureaucracies. Each successful check advances the clock.

GM Guidance: Clocks make progress visible and create tension. Players see segments filling and know they’re advancing—or that danger is mounting. Use clocks when you want players to feel progress accumulating or threats building, not just abstract “someday.”

Advancing Clocks

Clocks advance based on triggers appropriate to their nature:

Success-Based Advancement: Successful checks advance the clock. Use this for player-driven goals. A successful Research check advances the Investigation clock. A successful Diplomacy check advances the Alliance clock.

Time-Based Advancement: Clocks tick forward as time passes. Use this for mounting threats and deadlines. The Ritual Completion clock advances by 1 segment per hour. The Structural Collapse clock advances every scene.

Event-Based Advancement: Clocks advance when specific narrative events occur. Use this for faction projects or background developments. The Rival’s Plot clock advances when the rivals learn key information or remove obstacles.

Partial Outcomes: On Partial successes, you might advance the clock by 1 segment but also trigger a complication. This reflects progress with cost.

Exceptional Results: On particularly good rolls or clever approaches, advance the clock by 2 segments instead of 1. Reward ingenuity and good fortune.

GM Guidance: Be transparent about what advances clocks. Players should know what actions contribute to progress and what risks advancing threat clocks. Hidden clocks create mystery but visible ones create investment.

Clock Advancement Examples

4-Segment Clock - Cozy: Organizing a last-minute bake sale to fund repairs. Each successful Baking or Event Planning check advances the clock. Completing the clock means the sale succeeds and funds are raised.

6-Segment Clock - Fantasy: Tracking through a wilderness to find a hidden grove. Each successful Survival or Wayfinding check advances the clock. Completing the clock means you find the grove.

8-Segment Clock - Horror: A dark ritual nearing completion. The clock advances every midnight. Players must disrupt the ritual before all segments fill, or the entity manifests.

Multiple Clocks

Running multiple clocks simultaneously creates dynamic tension by tracking competing pressures:

Opposing Clocks: Two clocks race toward completion. Players advance their Escape clock while the GM advances the Pursuit clock. First to complete determines the outcome.

Layered Clocks: Multiple clocks track different aspects of a situation. A heist might track Infiltration (player clock), Security Response (threat clock), and Time Until Shift Change (deadline clock). Players must balance advancing their goal while managing threats.

Cascading Clocks: Completing one clock triggers or accelerates another. Completing the Gather Allies clock might unlock the Assault the Stronghold clock. Completing the Ritual Preparation clock might start the Perform Ritual clock.

GM Guidance: Don’t overwhelm the table with too many clocks. Two to three active clocks create dynamic tension. More than that can feel chaotic. Retire completed or irrelevant clocks to keep focus sharp.

Multiple Clock Example (Modern)

Situation: Detectives investigate a serial killer while the killer prepares their next attack.

Investigation Clock (6 segments, player-driven): Advances with successful Investigation, Forensics, or Interrogation checks. Completing reveals the killer’s identity and location.

Next Victim Clock (4 segments, time-based): Advances every session or at narrative beats. Completing means the killer strikes again, adding urgency.

Pressure: Players feel the tension between thorough investigation (which takes time) and racing the clock (which risks missing clues). Partials advance the Investigation clock but might also advance the Next Victim clock, as time passes during failed leads.

Examples by Genre

Cozy: Harvest Festival Preparation

Clock: 6-segment, player-driven Goal: Organize the annual harvest festival successfully

Advancement: Each successful Event Planning, Baking, or Negotiation check advances the clock by 1. Partials advance the clock but create minor complications (volunteer shortages, ingredient costs, rival towns’ jealousy).

Completion: The festival succeeds. The town’s reputation grows, and characters earn community goodwill.

Complication: A rival town runs a concurrent event. The GM introduces a second clock (Rival Festival, 4 segments, time-based) that advances every scene. If the rival clock completes first, some vendors and attendees go to the rival festival instead, reducing the player festival’s success.

Fantasy: Ritual Completion

Clock: 8-segment, time-based Goal: Disrupt an enemy ritual before it summons a demon

Advancement: The ritual clock advances by 1 segment every midnight. Players must infiltrate the enemy’s sanctum, disrupt preparations, or eliminate the ritualists before all 8 segments fill.

Completion: If the clock completes, the demon manifests and becomes a major threat. If players disrupt the ritual (by reducing the ritualists’ numbers, destroying components, or desecrating the site), the clock stops advancing or even loses segments.

Pressure: Players balance preparation (which takes time) against urgency (midnight approaches). Each day spent gathering allies or researching weaknesses is a day the clock advances.

Horror: Sanity Degradation

Clock: 6-segment, event-based Goal: Track a character’s mental stability as they uncover occult truths

Advancement: The clock advances when the character witnesses disturbing events, reads forbidden texts, or experiences trauma. Each segment represents a step toward breaking.

Completion: When the clock completes, the character gains a permanent mental condition (Obsessed, Paranoid, or worse) or experiences a psychotic break that requires narrative resolution.

Management: Characters can reduce segments through therapy (Psychology skill), rest in safe environments, or confronting fears narratively. The clock creates tension between pursuing dangerous truths and maintaining sanity.

Modern: Criminal Investigation

Clock: 6-segment, player-driven Goal: Gather enough evidence to arrest a corrupt official

Advancement: Successful Investigation, OSINT, or Interrogation checks advance the clock by 1. Exceptional success advances by 2. Partials advance the clock but trigger complications (evidence is circumstantial, witnesses recant, media leaks).

Completion: Players have enough evidence for an arrest. The case moves to trial or resolution.

Opposing Clock: The official’s Cover-Up clock (4 segments, event-based) advances when players make mistakes, leave traces, or tip off the official. If the Cover-Up clock completes first, the official flees, destroys evidence, or leverages political power to quash the investigation.

Sci-fi: Starship Repairs

Clock: 8-segment, player-driven Goal: Repair critical ship systems before life support fails

Advancement: Successful Systems Engineering or EVA Repair checks advance the clock by 1. Clever jury-rigging or exceptional rolls advance by 2. Partials advance but consume resources (materials, air, time).

Completion: The ship is functional. Life support stabilizes, and the crew can navigate to safety.

Deadline Clock: Life Support Failure (4 segments, time-based) advances every scene or hour. If it completes before the Repair clock, the crew must evacuate, seek rescue, or face catastrophic failure.

Decision Point: Players must choose between thorough repairs (slower, more checks) and quick patches (faster, riskier, potentially creating new problems later).

GM Usage Notes

  • Use clocks to make abstract progress concrete and visible. Players should see their efforts accumulating.
  • Choose clock size based on narrative weight and desired pacing: 4 for quick, 6 for standard, 8 for extended.
  • Advance clocks based on appropriate triggers: success-based for player goals, time-based for threats, event-based for background developments.
  • Run multiple clocks to create dynamic tension, but don’t overwhelm the table. Two to three active clocks are usually sufficient.
  • Be transparent about what advances clocks unless mystery is essential. Visible progress creates investment.
  • Adjust clock sizes mid-game if pacing feels off. Narrative needs trump initial choices.
  • Completing a clock should trigger meaningful change—goals achieved, threats realized, or situations transformed.

Clocks turn “someday” into “soon” and “eventually” into “now.” They create urgency, track progress, and make time mechanically meaningful. Use them to pace long-term arcs, build tension, and give players a sense of momentum or mounting danger.

Chapter 06: Skills Introduction

Skills are fiction-first capabilities that define what your character can do when the outcome is uncertain. They represent practiced competencies—from climbing a castle wall to negotiating a trade deal, from baking a wedding cake to navigating hyperspace. Skills turn uncertainty into motion, risk into opportunity.

This chapter explains how skills work, how to choose them during character creation, and how to interpret skill entries in the genre-specific chapters that follow.

What Skills Represent

Skills are not abstract numbers. They are grounded capabilities shaped by your character’s experience, training, and genre context. A character with Stealth knows how to move quietly, read patrol patterns, and exploit shadows. A character with Persuasion understands how to frame requests, read social dynamics, and build rapport.

When you roll a skill check, you are asking: “Can my character accomplish this goal given the fiction and the circumstances?” The DC reflects how difficult the task is. The outcome—success, partial success, or failure—determines what happens next in the story.

Skills operate on a fiction-first principle: if the fiction doesn’t support the action, no roll happens. You cannot use Persuasion to convince a dragon to become a vegetarian if the dragon has no reason to listen. You cannot use Stealth to sneak past a magical alarm that detects life force. The GM adjudicates what is possible based on the established fiction, then calls for a roll if the outcome is uncertain and interesting.

Skill Categories

Skills are organized into six broad categories. These categories help you navigate skill choices and understand how different skills interact:

  • Exploration: Skills for navigating, investigating, and interacting with the environment—Stealth, Perception, Athletics, Investigation, Wayfinding.
  • Social: Skills for influencing, understanding, and interacting with people—Persuasion, Insight, Negotiation, Intimidation, Comfort.
  • Technical: Skills requiring specialized knowledge or tools—Medicine, Thievery, Hacking, Engineering, Forensics.
  • Combat: Skills for direct conflict and tactical maneuvers—Melee Combat, Archery, Firearms, Tactics, Zero-G Combat.
  • Arcane/Divine: Skills for magical, spiritual, or supernatural knowledge—Arcana, Religion, Ritual, Occult Lore.
  • Wilderness: Skills for surviving and thriving in natural environments—Survival, Animal Handling, Gardening, Foraging.

These categories are descriptive, not restrictive. Some skills bridge multiple categories. The categories exist to help you find the right tool for the situation.

How to Read Skill Entries

Each skill entry in Chapters 07-11 follows a consistent format:

Skill Name — Category: The skill’s name and primary category.

Description: A fiction-first explanation of what the skill represents and how it feels in play.

Default Actions: Common tasks associated with this skill, along with DC ranges (typically 12-22). These are examples, not exhaustive lists. The GM adjusts DCs based on position, effect, and circumstances.

Synergies: Proficiencies, tags, or conditions that enhance this skill. For example, Stealth synergizes with the Dim tag or Cloaked condition.

Counters: Tags or conditions that make this skill harder or less effective. For example, Stealth is countered by the Alert tag or Tracking Dogs condition.

GM Usage: Practical guidance for adjudicating this skill at the table—how to make it interesting, when to call for rolls, and how to interpret outcomes.

Example: A brief worked example showing the skill in action within the genre context.

When reading skill entries, focus on the fiction first, then the mechanics. Ask: “What does this skill let my character do?” Then consider: “What circumstances make this easier or harder?”

Choosing Skills for Character Creation

During character creation, you will select skills that define your character’s capabilities. The number of skills you start with depends on your game’s character creation system—consult your GM or the Player Guide for specifics.

When choosing skills, consider:

  1. Genre Fit: Choose skills appropriate for your game’s genre. A cozy village game rarely needs Firearms. A sci-fi campaign rarely needs Baking (unless you are the ship’s cook).
  2. Character Concept: Choose skills that reflect who your character is. A former guard might have Perception and Tactics. A traveling bard might have Performance and Streetwise.
  3. Party Synergy: Consider what your party needs. If no one has Medicine, someone should cover it. If everyone has Persuasion, consider diversifying.
  4. Growth Potential: Choose skills you want to improve through proficiencies. If you take Cooking, you might later gain the Baker proficiency to specialize.

Skills are not permanent straitjackets. Over time, you can acquire new skills through downtime training or narrative milestones. See Chapter 20 for progression mechanics.

Creating Custom Skills

If the premade skills in Chapters 07-11 do not fit your character concept, you can create a custom skill with your GM’s approval. Use this template:

Skill Name — Category

Description: A 1-2 sentence fiction-first description of what this skill represents.

Default Actions: 3-5 common tasks with DC ranges (12-22). Include at least one opposed check or group action.

Synergies: Proficiencies, tags, or conditions that enhance this skill.

Counters: Tags or conditions that make this skill harder.

Variants: Genre-specific or context-specific variations (e.g., “Urban vs Wilderness”).

GM Usage: How to adjudicate this skill at the table. What makes it interesting? When should you call for rolls?

Example: A brief worked example showing the skill in action.

Balance Guidelines

When creating custom skills:

  • Scope: Skills should be broad enough to be useful but focused enough to have identity. “Investigation” is good. “Noticing Things” is too broad. “Analyzing Fingerprints” is too narrow.
  • DC Ranges: Use the standard DC ladder (see Chapter 02). Simple tasks start at DC 12. Challenging tasks range from 14-18. Difficult or complex tasks range from 18-22.
  • Synergies and Counters: Identify at least two synergies and two counters. These create tactical depth and help the GM adjudicate edge cases.
  • Genre Fit: Ensure the skill fits your game’s genre and tone. A skill called “Social Media Savvy” fits Modern but not Fantasy.

Bring your custom skill to your GM for review. Adjust as needed. The goal is to create a skill that feels distinct, useful, and fun to roll.

Using This Section

Chapters 07-11 provide genre-specific skill packages:

  • Chapter 07: Cozy Genre Skills — Domestic, crafting, community, and nature skills for slice-of-life games.
  • Chapter 08: Fantasy Genre Skills — Combat, exploration, social, arcane, and wilderness skills for heroic adventures.
  • Chapter 09: Horror/Mystery Genre Skills — Investigation, psychological, occult, and survival skills for tense, investigative games.
  • Chapter 10: Modern Genre Skills — Professional, technical, urban, and tactical skills for contemporary settings.
  • Chapter 11: Sci-Fi Genre Skills — Piloting, engineering, science, and combat skills for space-faring campaigns.

Use the chapter that fits your game’s genre. Mix and match skills across genres if your game blends tones (e.g., a cozy fantasy village game might use both Cozy and Fantasy skills).

For proficiencies that enhance these skills, see Chapters 13-17. For tags and conditions that modify skill checks, see Chapter 18.

Now, turn to the genre that fits your game and start exploring what your character can do.

Chapter 07: Cozy Genre Skills

Cozy games celebrate the everyday: baking bread, tending gardens, organizing festivals, caring for animals. These skills turn domestic challenges into meaningful moments. They are grounded in community, craft, and care—where the stakes are real but rarely violent, and success builds connection rather than conquest.

This chapter provides ready-to-use skills for cozy and slice-of-life games. Use these skills when your game focuses on village life, rural communities, artisan crafts, or pastoral adventures. Mix them with Fantasy skills (Chapter 08) for cozy fantasy settings.

Genre Overview

Cozy skills emphasize:

  • Domestic Mastery: Cooking, homemaking, hospitality—skills that create comfort and belonging.
  • Artisan Crafts: Baking, knitting, pottery, woodworking—skills that produce tangible, meaningful goods.
  • Community Connection: Gossip, comfort, mediation—skills that build and maintain social bonds.
  • Nature Harmony: Gardening, animal husbandry, foraging—skills that work with the land rather than against it.

Cozy skills rarely involve direct conflict. Instead, they navigate social dynamics, solve logistical problems, and create beauty. The tension comes from time pressure, resource scarcity, or emotional stakes—will the pie win the contest? Will the festival happen on time? Will the injured lamb recover?

For proficiencies that enhance these skills, see Chapter 13 (Cozy Proficiencies). For tags and conditions, see Chapter 18.

Domestic Skills

Cooking — Technical

Description: Cooking transforms ingredients into meals that nourish body and spirit. A skilled cook knows how to balance flavors, manage heat, and improvise with what is on hand.

Default Actions: - Prepare Meal: DC 12-14 — Prepare a satisfying, everyday meal. - Cook to Impress: DC 16-18 — Create a memorable dish for special occasions. - Improvise with Limited Ingredients: DC 16 — Make something delicious from pantry scraps. - Bulk Cooking (Downtime): Prepare meals for a community event or long journey.

Synergies: Baker proficiency, Warm Hearth tag, Fresh Ingredients tag.

Counters: Shortages tag, Burned condition (for stoves), Time Pressure.

GM Usage: Use Cooking to create social moments—inviting someone for dinner, winning a cooking contest, impressing a guest. Partials succeed but consume scarce ingredients or take extra time. Failures result in bland food or minor mishaps (burned crusts, oversalted stew).

Example: Mira prepares a feast to welcome the new mayor. The GM calls for DC 16 (Cook to Impress). Mira rolls well, and the meal becomes the talk of the village, granting her Advantage on her next Persuasion check with the mayor.

Homemaking — Technical

Description: Homemaking is the art of maintaining a comfortable, functional living space. It includes cleaning, organizing, mending, and creating a sense of order and warmth.

Default Actions: - Tidy Home: DC 12 — Clean and organize a living space. - Deep Clean: DC 14-16 — Remove stubborn grime, repair minor damage. - Create Welcoming Atmosphere: DC 14 — Arrange a space to feel inviting and comfortable. - Preserve Goods: DC 14-16 — Properly store food, textiles, or tools for long-term use.

Synergies: Homekeeper proficiency, Cozy Interior tag, Organized condition.

Counters: Clutter tag, Disrepair tag, Time Pressure.

GM Usage: Homemaking creates comfort and order. Use it to prepare safe havens, impress guests, or maintain resources. Partials clean most areas but miss a spot, or take longer than expected. Failures leave messes or damage fragile items.

Example: Finn prepares his cottage for winter. The GM calls for DC 14 (Deep Clean and Preserve Goods). A partial success means the cottage is tidy, but Finn discovers a drafty window that will need repair later (add a clock: “Seal Window Before Snowfall”).

Hospitality — Social

Description: Hospitality is the skill of making others feel welcome, safe, and valued. It includes hosting guests, offering comfort, and navigating the unspoken rules of social grace.

Default Actions: - Welcome Guest: DC 12-14 — Make a visitor feel at ease. - Host Event: DC 16-18 — Organize and run a gathering smoothly. - Diffuse Tension at Table: DC 16 — Navigate awkward social moments during meals. - Offer Comfort: DC 14 — Provide emotional support through hospitality.

Synergies: Festival Organizer proficiency, Warm Hearth tag, Well-Stocked Larder tag.

Counters: Hostile Guest tag, Shortages tag, Unexpected Guests.

GM Usage: Hospitality builds relationships and creates safe spaces. Partials welcome guests but strain resources or miss a detail (forgotten preference). Failures create awkwardness or offense.

Example: Elara hosts the town council at her inn during a tense negotiation. The GM calls for DC 16 (Host Event and Diffuse Tension). Elara rolls well, and her warm hospitality eases the mood, granting the council Advantage on their next Negotiation check.

Crafting Skills

Baking — Technical

Description: Baking is precision and patience. Dough must proof at the right temperature, ovens must hold steady heat, and timing is everything. A good baker knows the rhythms of yeast and fire.

Default Actions: - Bake Everyday Bread: DC 12-14 — Produce a reliable, satisfying loaf. - Bake Specialty Item: DC 16-18 — Create pastries, cakes, or contest-worthy goods. - Bulk Baking (Downtime): Prepare goods for market or festival. - Diagnose Baking Problem: DC 14 — Identify why dough did not rise or crust burned.

Synergies: Baker proficiency, Warm Hearth tag, Fresh Ingredients tag.

Counters: Shortages tag, Humid Weather tag, Damaged Oven.

GM Usage: Baking creates social currency—winning contests, attracting customers, gifting goodwill. Partials produce good results but consume extra ingredients or take longer. Failures produce edible but unremarkable goods.

Example: Tessa enters the village baking contest with her famous berry tart. The GM calls for DC 18 (Bake to Impress). Tessa rolls a 19—her tart wins first place and earns her the Mayor’s Favor tag for future social checks.

Knitting — Technical

Description: Knitting transforms yarn into warmth and beauty. A skilled knitter can create garments, blankets, and gifts that carry emotional weight and practical value.

Default Actions: - Knit Simple Item: DC 12-14 — Create a scarf, hat, or dishcloth. - Knit Complex Garment: DC 16-18 — Create a sweater, blanket, or intricate pattern. - Repair Knitwear: DC 14 — Mend holes or unravel and re-knit sections. - Rush Project: DC 16 — Complete a project faster than normal without sacrificing quality.

Synergies: Seamstress proficiency, Comfortable Chair tag, Quality Yarn tag.

Counters: Tangled Yarn tag, Poor Light tag, Time Pressure.

GM Usage: Knitting is meditative and productive. Use it for downtime activities, gifts, or preparing for winter. Partials complete projects but with minor flaws or extra time. Failures create tangled messes or mismatched sizes.

Example: Rowan knits a blanket for the newborn twins. The GM calls for DC 16 (Knit Complex Garment). Rowan rolls a 17—the blanket is beautiful and becomes a cherished family heirloom, granting Rowan the Trusted Friend tag with the family.

Pottery — Technical

Description: Pottery is earth and water shaped by hands and fire. A potter knows how to center clay, form vessels, and glaze surfaces for beauty and function.

Default Actions: - Throw Simple Vessel: DC 12-14 — Create a bowl, cup, or plate. - Throw Complex Piece: DC 16-18 — Create a large vase, intricate sculpture, or decorative work. - Glaze and Fire: DC 14-16 — Apply glaze and fire a kiln without cracking or warping. - Repair Pottery: DC 16 — Mend a broken piece with kintsugi or similar techniques.

Synergies: Potter proficiency, Steady Hands tag, Quality Clay tag.

Counters: Damaged Kiln tag, Poor Clay tag, Time Pressure.

GM Usage: Pottery creates functional art. Use it for trade, gifts, or community projects. Partials produce usable but imperfect pieces. Failures crack in the kiln or warp during drying.

Example: Lian creates a ceremonial tea set for the town elder’s retirement. The GM calls for DC 18 (Throw Complex Piece). Lian rolls a 20—the set is exquisite and becomes the centerpiece of the ceremony, earning Lian the Artisan’s Reputation tag.

Woodworking — Technical

Description: Woodworking is the craft of shaping timber into tools, furniture, and structures. A woodworker knows grain, joints, and finishes.

Default Actions: - Build Simple Item: DC 12-14 — Create a stool, shelf, or tool handle. - Build Furniture: DC 16-18 — Create a table, chair, or cabinet. - Repair Wood Structure: DC 14-16 — Fix a fence, door, or roof beam. - Carve Decorative Detail: DC 16 — Add ornamental carvings or inlays.

Synergies: Carpenter proficiency, Quality Tools tag, Dry Lumber tag.

Counters: Dull Tools tag, Wet Wood tag, Time Pressure.

GM Usage: Woodworking builds infrastructure and beauty. Use it for repairs, gifts, or community projects. Partials succeed but leave rough edges or require extra time. Failures result in warped wood or unstable joints.

Example: Kael repairs the village hall’s front door before the harvest festival. The GM calls for DC 16 (Repair Wood Structure and Carve Decorative Detail). Kael rolls an 18—the door is sturdy and beautiful, and the village council grants Kael a commission for more work.

Social Skills

Gossip — Social

Description: Gossip is the currency of small communities. Knowing who said what, who is courting whom, and what the miller thinks of the new baker gives you social leverage and insight.

Default Actions: - Gather Rumors: DC 12-14 — Learn recent news or social dynamics. - Spread Information: DC 14-16 — Plant a rumor or shape public opinion. - Verify Gossip: DC 16 — Determine if a rumor is true or exaggerated. - Navigate Social Politics: DC 16-18 — Use gossip to broker alliances or avoid conflicts.

Synergies: Town Crier proficiency, Market Day tag, Social Gathering tag.

Counters: Tight-Lipped Community tag, Isolated Location tag.

GM Usage: Gossip reveals information and shapes social landscapes. Partials provide incomplete or slightly inaccurate rumors. Failures spread misinformation or offend someone.

Example: Marta wants to learn why the blacksmith and the baker stopped speaking. The GM calls for DC 14 (Gather Rumors). Marta rolls a 16—she learns the blacksmith accidentally insulted the baker’s cinnamon rolls, and now she can mediate.

Comfort — Social

Description: Comfort is the skill of easing sorrow, calming worry, and offering presence when words fail. It includes listening, empathy, and knowing when to offer tea or silence.

Default Actions: - Soothe Distress: DC 12-14 — Calm someone who is upset or worried. - Provide Emotional Support: DC 14-16 — Help someone process grief, fear, or uncertainty. - Lift Spirits: DC 14 — Cheer someone up with small gestures or kind words. - Group Comfort: DC 16-18 — Help a community process collective trauma or loss.

Synergies: Herbalist proficiency (calming teas), Warm Hearth tag, Trusted Friend tag.

Counters: Distant condition (on the target), Grief-Stricken condition, Time Pressure.

GM Usage: Comfort builds trust and heals emotional wounds. Partials soothe but do not fully resolve the issue. Failures come across as dismissive or overwhelming.

Example: After a barn fire, Iris comforts the farmer’s family. The GM calls for DC 16 (Group Comfort). Iris rolls an 18—the family feels supported and begins to rebuild with hope. The GM removes the Despairing condition from the family.

Mediation — Social

Description: Mediation is the art of finding common ground. A mediator listens, reframes, and helps conflicting parties see solutions they could not find alone.

Default Actions: - Broker Agreement: DC 14-16 — Help two parties reach a compromise. - De-escalate Conflict: DC 16 — Calm a tense argument before it worsens. - Facilitate Group Decision: DC 16-18 — Guide a community toward consensus. - Repair Relationship: DC 18 — Help estranged friends or family reconcile.

Synergies: Festival Organizer proficiency, Neutral Ground tag, Community Respect tag.

Counters: Stubborn Parties tag, Long-Standing Feud tag, Emotional Volatility.

GM Usage: Mediation resolves conflicts and builds community cohesion. Partials find temporary truces or partial agreements. Failures entrench positions or create new grievances.

Example: Two farmers dispute a property line. The GM calls for DC 16 (Broker Agreement). The mediator rolls a 17—the farmers agree to a shared fence and a boundary marker, and the conflict is resolved.

Nature Skills

Gardening — Wilderness/Technical

Description: Gardening is patience and observation. A gardener knows soil, seasons, and the rhythms of growth. They coax life from seeds and tend it through harvest.

Default Actions: - Plant and Tend: DC 12-14 — Grow vegetables, herbs, or flowers. - Cure Blight: DC 16-18 — Diagnose and treat plant disease or pest infestations. - Plan Garden Layout: DC 14 — Design a productive, beautiful garden. - Harvest and Preserve: DC 12-14 — Gather crops and prepare them for storage.

Synergies: Herbalist proficiency, Fertile Soil tag, Seasonal Rain tag.

Counters: Drought tag, Pest Infestation tag, Poor Soil tag.

GM Usage: Gardening provides food, medicine, and beauty. Partials grow edible but small yields or require extra care. Failures result in poor harvests or spreading blight.

Example: Tove tends her herb garden through a dry summer. The GM calls for DC 16 (Cure Blight and Improvise Watering). Tove rolls a 17—the garden survives, and she harvests enough basil and mint for the village healer.

Animal Husbandry — Wilderness

Description: Animal husbandry is the care and management of livestock and working animals. A skilled herder knows feeding schedules, breeding practices, and how to keep animals healthy and productive.

Default Actions: - Tend Herd: DC 12-14 — Feed, water, and maintain livestock health. - Treat Injury or Illness: DC 16-18 — Diagnose and care for sick or injured animals. - Breeding and Birthing: DC 16 — Oversee breeding and assist with births. - Calm Spooked Animals: DC 14-16 — Soothe frightened livestock.

Synergies: Beekeeper proficiency, Pasture tag, Quality Feed tag.

Counters: Predators tag, Disease tag, Harsh Weather tag.

GM Usage: Animal husbandry sustains livelihoods and communities. Partials treat animals but require extra resources or time. Failures result in lost animals or worsening conditions.

Example: During lambing season, one ewe struggles to deliver. The GM calls for DC 18 (Breeding and Birthing). The herder rolls a 19—the lamb is born healthy, and the ewe recovers quickly.

Foraging — Wilderness

Description: Foraging is the art of finding food, herbs, and useful materials in the wild. A forager knows which plants are edible, which are medicinal, and where to look for them.

Default Actions: - Gather Food: DC 12-14 — Find edible plants, berries, or mushrooms. - Find Medicinal Herbs: DC 16 — Locate herbs for healing or remedies. - Identify Safe Plants: DC 14 — Distinguish edible plants from poisonous lookalikes. - Track Seasonal Resources: DC 14-16 — Predict when and where resources will be abundant.

Synergies: Herbalist proficiency, Forest tag, Seasonal Bounty tag.

Counters: Barren Land tag, Poisonous Plants tag, Time Pressure.

GM Usage: Foraging supplements food supplies and provides crafting materials. Partials find resources but in smaller quantities or mixed with inedible plants. Failures waste time or gather toxic plants.

Example: Asa forages for mushrooms after a rainstorm. The GM calls for DC 14 (Gather Food and Identify Safe Plants). Asa rolls a 16—she returns with a basket of edible mushrooms and a few rare medicinal fungi, which she trades to the herbalist.

Worked Examples

Example 1: Baking Contest
