Mechanically, proficiencies offer concrete advantages: - Reducing DCs by 2-4 points in specific situations - Granting advantage on checks related to their specialty - Unlocking new actions entirely (crafting complex items, performing specialized techniques) - Reducing downtime clocks or speeding progress on long-term projects - Providing synergies with related skills, tags, or conditions

Narratively, proficiencies define expertise and identity. They signal what a character has invested time mastering, what communities they belong to, and what doors are open to them. A character with Court Etiquette moves differently in formal settings than one without. A character with Network Admin thinks in systems diagrams. These are not just mechanical benefits—they are lenses that shape how characters perceive and solve problems.

Proficiency Tiers

Proficiencies progress through four tiers, each reflecting deepening mastery:

Novice (N): Basic familiarity with the proficiency’s techniques. You have completed foundational training and can perform routine tasks with confidence, but complex situations still challenge you. Novice proficiencies typically grant advantage on simple checks or reduce DCs by 2 in straightforward contexts.

Trained (T): Professional competence. You have internalized the fundamentals and can handle most situations that arise in your specialty. Trained proficiencies grant advantage on a broader range of checks, reduce DCs by 2-3, and often unlock specialized techniques or downtime activities unavailable to novices.

Expert (E): Exceptional mastery. You are recognized as an authority in your field, capable of handling complex challenges and teaching others. Expert proficiencies grant significant advantages (advantage plus DC reduction, or DC reduction of 4+), unlock advanced techniques, and provide leadership benefits when coordinating others in your specialty.

Master (M): Peak achievement. You have reached the limits of what is commonly known in your proficiency and may have developed unique techniques or insights. Master proficiencies grant game-changing abilities, allow you to attempt the near-impossible, and often provide benefits to allies or reshape entire scenes. Masters are rare—most campaigns will feature only a handful of master-tier proficiencies across all characters.

Acquiring Proficiencies

Proficiencies are not gained through simple experience or abstract advancement points. They require dedicated downtime training, access to mentors or instructional materials, and narrative justification.

Training Requirements

To acquire or advance a proficiency, a character must:

  1. Find a Teacher or Resource: A mentor with the proficiency at the desired tier or higher, a guild that offers structured training, instructional texts (for some knowledge-based proficiencies), or extended practice in the field.

  2. Commit Downtime: Training takes time. The exact duration depends on the tier and campaign pacing, but typical benchmarks are:

    • Novice: 1-2 downtime sessions (weeks to a month)
    • Trained: 2-4 downtime sessions (1-3 months)
    • Expert: 4-8 downtime sessions (3-6 months)
    • Master: 8-12+ downtime sessions (6-12+ months)

  3. Pay Costs: Training is not free. Costs scale by tier and may include:

    • Novice: Modest fees (enough to cover materials and a teacher’s time)
    • Trained: Significant investment (tuition, equipment, guild dues)
    • Expert: Substantial costs (rare materials, specialized instruction, research)
    • Master: Major commitments (unique quests, rare favors, legendary components)

  4. Demonstrate Aptitude: At the GM’s discretion, advancing a proficiency may require a final test—a check or challenge that proves the character has internalized the training.

Mentors and Guilds

The fiction of proficiency acquisition matters. Finding a master blacksmith willing to teach Armorer (Expert) might be its own adventure. Gaining entry to the Cartographer’s Guild might require proving your worth on a dangerous survey mission. Accessing forbidden texts to study Ritualist (Master) might risk sanity or legal consequences.

GMs should treat proficiency training as narrative opportunities, not bookkeeping. The character who spends downtime training with the retired weapons master learns not just techniques, but stories, connections, and complications. The character who joins the Baker’s Guild gains not just the Baker proficiency, but a place in the community’s social fabric.

How to Read Proficiency Entries

Proficiency entries in the following chapters follow a consistent format:

Name and Tier: The proficiency’s name and required tier (N, T, E, or M).

Description: A 1-2 sentence overview of what the proficiency represents and what kind of expertise it embodies.

Benefits: The specific mechanical advantages the proficiency grants. This might include DC reductions, advantage on checks, unlocked actions, or special techniques.

Synergies: Related skills, tags, or other proficiencies that pair well with this one. For example, First Aid synergizes with the Medicine skill and conditions like Poisoned or Bleeding.

Downtime Activities: What the character can do with this proficiency during downtime—earning income, gathering resources, building reputation, or advancing personal projects.

GM Usage: Practical guidance for incorporating the proficiency into play. This includes when to call for checks, how to adjudicate partials, and how to make the proficiency feel meaningful at the table.

Creating Custom Proficiencies

The proficiencies in chapters 13-17 are not exhaustive. Players and GMs are encouraged to create custom proficiencies that fit their characters and settings.

Custom Proficiency Template

Use this template as a starting point:

[Proficiency Name] — [Tier]

Description: [What this proficiency represents in 1-2 sentences]

Benefits: - [Specific mechanical benefit 1] - [Specific mechanical benefit 2] - [Optional: specific mechanical benefit 3]

Synergies: [Related skills, tags, proficiencies, or conditions]

Downtime Activities: [What you can do with this during downtime]

GM Usage: [How to make this meaningful in play]

Scaling Guidelines

When designing custom proficiencies, consider these scaling principles:

Novice: Should grant advantage on simple checks OR reduce DC by 2 in straightforward situations. Should not fundamentally change what a character can attempt, but should make routine tasks more reliable.

Trained: Should grant advantage on a broader range of checks AND/OR reduce DC by 2-3 in their specialty. May unlock new techniques or downtime activities. Should make the character clearly more capable than untrained peers in specific contexts.

Expert: Should grant significant advantages (advantage + DC reduction, or DC -4, or unlocking advanced techniques). Should be broadly useful in their domain and potentially grant benefits to others (teaching, leadership, coordination bonuses).

Master: Should grant game-changing abilities or allow the character to attempt things that would otherwise be impossible. Should be rare and narratively significant. Consider whether this proficiency should exist in your campaign at all—masters represent the pinnacle of their craft and should feel legendary.

Balance Considerations

Good proficiencies are situational, not universal. A proficiency should shine in its domain but not overshadow characters with different specialties. First Aid should make you invaluable in medical crises, but it should not replace the need for other characters’ skills in exploration, negotiation, or investigation.

Proficiencies should interact cleanly with skills and tags. They should enhance existing mechanics, not create parallel systems. When in doubt, grant advantage or reduce DCs rather than introducing new resolution mechanics.

Proficiencies should require narrative justification. Do not allow players to acquire proficiencies simply by spending downtime abstractly. The story of how they gained the proficiency matters as much as the mechanical benefits.

Using This Section

The following chapters present proficiency sets organized by genre: - Chapter 13: Cozy genre proficiencies (artisanal, domestic, community, nature) - Chapter 14: Fantasy genre proficiencies (martial, magical, wilderness, social) - Chapter 15: Horror/Mystery genre proficiencies (investigation, occult, survival, medical) - Chapter 16: Modern genre proficiencies (professional, technical, tactical, social) - Chapter 17: Sci-Fi genre proficiencies (starship, scientific, cybernetic, combat)

Each chapter provides 6-10 complete proficiencies with worked examples showing them in play. Use these as starting points, modify them to fit your setting, and create your own as needed. Proficiencies should feel earned, meaningful, and distinct—they are not just numbers on a character sheet, but reflections of dedication, training, and identity.

Chapter 13: Cozy Genre Proficiencies

Cozy proficiencies celebrate the art of making, tending, and connecting. They reflect mastery of crafts passed down through generations, the satisfaction of work done well, and the quiet heroism of keeping communities functioning. These proficiencies transform everyday tasks into sources of pride, income, and narrative possibility.

Genre Overview

Cozy games center on domestic rhythms, artisanal crafts, community service, and harmonious relationships with nature. Proficiencies in this genre enable characters to: - Create goods that comfort, delight, or sustain others - Maintain homes, gardens, and communal spaces - Organize festivals, mediate disputes, and strengthen social bonds - Work with animals, plants, and the land in respectful partnership

Unlike combat-focused proficiencies, cozy proficiencies measure success in relationships deepened, traditions honored, and small joys multiplied. A successful Baking check might mend a rift between neighbors. A partial success on Festival Organizing might create a memorable event despite logistical chaos. These proficiencies thrive in low-stakes contexts where care and competence matter more than danger and drama.

Cross-reference: See Chapter 7: Cozy Skills for related skills like Cooking, Homemaking, Gardening, and Hospitality.

Proficiencies

Baker — Trained

Description: You know thealchemy of flour, water, heat, and time. Your bread rises evenly, your pastries flake perfectly, and your creations carry the warmth that turns a meal into a memory.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when baking to impress, comfort, or celebrate - Advantage on Cooking checks when preparing baked goods specifically - Produce festival-grade items that can shift community mood or mend social rifts - Create signature recipes that become locally famous

Synergies: Cooking skill, Homemaking, Hospitality; pairs well with Warm Hearth or Community Gathering tags.

Downtime Activities: Run a bakery or stall, supply festivals or markets, teach apprentices, develop new recipes, participate in baking competitions.

GM Usage: Baking is social glue in cozy settings. A successful bake can open conversations, earn favors, or change NPC attitudes. Partials might produce delicious goods that strain ingredient supplies or attract too much attention (now everyone wants your cinnamon rolls and you cannot keep up). Use baking as a lens for community dynamics—who gets first access to the baker’s oven? What recipes are kept secret? What traditions surround harvest bread or wedding cakes?

Potter — Novice

Description: Clay yields to your hands, becoming vessels that hold water, soup, and sentiment. Your work is functional first, beautiful second, but the two are not separate.

Benefits: - Create serviceable pottery for household or trade use - Advantage on Crafting checks when working with clay - Reduce DC by 2 when repairing ceramic goods - Produce basic decorative items (vases, tiles, ornamental pieces)

Synergies: Crafting skill, Artisan communities; pairs well with Workshop or Market Day tags.

Downtime Activities: Produce pottery for sale or trade, take commissions for custom pieces, teach basic pottery, gather and prepare clay.

GM Usage: Pottery is patient work that rewards focus. Use it to create calm, meditative moments or to explore themes of impermanence and care (a cherished bowl that cracks, a rushed repair that fails). Partials might produce functional but flawed pieces—a cup with an uneven lip, a plate that wobbles. These imperfections can become narrative hooks (the mayor insists on only perfect dishware for the banquet; now what?).

Seamstress — Trained

Description: Needle and thread are extensions of your intent. You mend torn hems, tailor ill-fitting garments, and create clothing that flatters, protects, or makes a statement.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when mending or altering garments - Advantage on Crafting checks for sewing, embroidery, or tailoring - Create custom clothing that grants social advantage in specific contexts (formal wear for Court Etiquette, work clothes that signal trade affiliation) - Repair damaged fabric goods quickly during downtime

Synergies: Crafting skill, Fashion sense, Hospitality; pairs well with Market Day, Community Gathering, or Workshop tags.

Downtime Activities: Run a tailor shop, take commissions, teach sewing classes, create clothing for festivals or ceremonies, mend community wardrobe stock.

GM Usage: Clothing carries meaning. A well-tailored outfit can shift social position or effect in formal settings. Seamstress checks can also represent repairing gear, sails, or banners—functional sewing under pressure. Partials might complete the work but leave small tells (visible stitching, slight misalignment) or require more time than available. Use this proficiency to explore themes of presentation, identity, and care (mending a beloved coat, creating a wedding dress, disguising someone’s station).

Homekeeper — Novice

Description: You maintain order and comfort in domestic spaces. Floors stay swept, hearths stay warm, and visitors feel welcome without knowing exactly why.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when organizing, cleaning, or maintaining a household - Advantage on Homemaking checks to create welcoming environments - Efficiently manage household supplies, reducing waste and stretching resources - Notice when domestic spaces are disturbed or unusual

Synergies: Homemaking skill, Hospitality; pairs well with Warm Hearth, Clean Space, or Cozy Interior tags.

Downtime Activities: Manage a household, prepare spaces for guests or events, coordinate cleaning or maintenance efforts, mentor others in domestic skills.

GM Usage: Homekeeping might seem mundane, but it creates the baseline against which disruption registers. A Homekeeper notices when something is out of place—evidence of intrusion, illness, or distress. Use this proficiency to establish setting details (a cluttered study signals distraction; an immaculate kitchen signals control or anxiety). Partials might achieve cleanliness but miss subtleties (you clean the inn, but the hidden note behind the loose board stays hidden).

Chef — Trained

Description: You do not merely cook—you compose. Ingredients harmonize under your direction, and meals become events that linger in memory long after plates are cleared.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 3 when cooking to impress, comfort, or celebrate - Advantage on Cooking checks for complex or multi-course meals - Create dishes that grant temporary morale boosts or shift NPC attitudes - Identify quality ingredients by sight, smell, and touch - Improvise meals from limited or unusual ingredients

Synergies: Cooking skill, Hospitality, Foraging; pairs well with Feast Prepared, Warm Hearth, or Community Gathering tags.

Downtime Activities: Run a restaurant or inn kitchen, cater events, develop signature dishes, teach cooking, source rare ingredients, participate in culinary competitions.

GM Usage: Cooking is an intimate act that builds trust and opens conversations. A meal prepared with skill can turn rivals into allies, soothe tensions, or celebrate victories. Partials might produce delicious food that exhausts supplies, takes longer than expected, or inadvertently reveals cultural misunderstandings (you prepared the traditional dish, but used the festival version at a funeral—oops). Use cooking to explore themes of care, tradition, and resourcefulness.

Town Crier — Novice

Description: Your voice carries news, warnings, and celebrations through streets and squares. People listen not just because you are loud, but because you are trusted.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when spreading news or rallying public attention - Advantage on Persuasion or Performance checks when making public announcements - Quickly assess public mood by reading crowd reactions - Gain access to community news networks and gossip chains

Synergies: Persuasion skill, Performance, Gossip; pairs well with Market Day, Community Gathering, or Public Square tags.

Downtime Activities: Spread official announcements, relay community news, mediate public disputes, gather information from public spaces.

GM Usage: Town Criers are information hubs. They know what is being talked about, what is being hidden, and what is being spun. Use this proficiency to connect characters to community events and rumors. Partials might spread the message but garble details, or deliver accurate news that sparks unintended reactions (announcing the mayor’s engagement accidentally reveals the secret affair that preceded it). Town Criers can also be targeted—silencing the crier silences the message.

Festival Organizer — Trained

Description: You turn chaos into celebration. You know how to rally volunteers, coordinate schedules, and ensure that the maypole does not collapse mid-dance.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when organizing community events or rallying volunteers - Advantage on Event Planning checks for festivals, markets, or gatherings - Reduce chaos or complication clocks by 1 segment on full success when organizing events - Coordinate multiple parallel tasks efficiently, delegating with clarity - Anticipate logistical problems before they escalate

Synergies: Event Planning skill, Persuasion, Leadership; pairs well with Community Gathering, Market Day, or Festival Preparation tags.

Downtime Activities: Organize seasonal festivals, coordinate market days, plan community celebrations, build event infrastructure, recruit and train volunteers.

GM Usage: Festival Organizer checks often involve juggling multiple clocks—supply shortages, weather complications, interpersonal conflicts. Success means the event runs smoothly and strengthens community bonds. Partials might achieve a memorable event that leaves some frayed nerves or depleted resources. Use festivals as pressure-cooker scenarios that reveal character dynamics (who helps? who undermines? who claims credit?). Festivals also invite external complications (traveling merchants with dubious wares, unexpected guests, sudden storms).

Herbalist — Trained

Description: You know plants not as decorations but as partners—which soothe, which invigorate, which must never be brewed together. Your remedies carry generations of careful observation.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when identifying, gathering, or preparing medicinal herbs - Advantage on Gardening or Foraging checks related to herbs and medicinal plants - Brew remedies that ease minor ailments (headaches, indigestion, insomnia, minor wounds) - Create poultices and salves that reduce recovery time for injuries or illnesses - Recognize poisonous or dangerous plants on sight

Synergies: Gardening skill, Foraging, Medicine; pairs well with Garden, Forest Edge, or Apothecary tags.

Downtime Activities: Gather and dry herbs, prepare remedies for trade or community use, tend medicinal gardens, teach herblore, consult on health concerns.

GM Usage: Herbalists occupy the space between folk medicine and professional healing. Use this proficiency to address minor ailments that do not require dramatic intervention but still matter to characters (soothing a sick child, easing an elder’s aching joints). Partials might produce effective remedies with unpleasant side effects (the tea works, but tastes vile; the salve heals, but stains skin green for days). Herbalists also know local ecology intimately—they notice when plants sicken, when seasons shift unusually, when something in the environment is wrong.

Beekeeper — Novice

Description: You tend the hives with respect and patience. In return, the bees grant you honey, wax, and a small miracle of cooperative labor.

Benefits: - Safely harvest honey and beeswax without harming the hive - Advantage on Animal Handling checks involving bees or hive management - Recognize hive health and diagnose common bee ailments - Produce honey and wax for trade, cooking, or crafting - Understand pollination patterns and can advise on garden placement

Synergies: Animal Handling skill, Gardening, Nature Lore; pairs well with Garden, Meadow, or Orchard tags.

Downtime Activities: Tend hives, harvest honey and wax, expand or relocate hives, trade bee products, consult on pollination and crop health.

GM Usage: Beekeeping is steady, seasonal work that connects characters to natural rhythms. Use it to mark time (honey harvest signals late summer) and to introduce environmental complications (hives struggling signals ecological stress). Partials might yield good honey but agitate the hive (now you are covered in stings), or produce wax but in quantities too small for the planned candle order. Bees also serve as narrative sensors—when the bees abandon their hives, something is wrong. When they thrive, the land is healthy.

Worked Examples

Example 1: Baker Mending a Rift

Context: Two neighbors, Marna and Tobin, have not spoken in weeks after a property dispute. The annual Harvest Festival is days away, and their feud is souring the community mood. Elara, a Trained Baker, decides to intervene.

Action: Elara bakes a honey-walnut loaf—a traditional reconciliation gift in her village. She delivers it to Marna’s door with a carefully neutral message: “For sharing at the festival, if you are willing.”

Check: Elara’s player rolls Cooking (to bake the loaf) with advantage (Baker proficiency) and a DC reduced by 2 (Baker benefit for baking to mend social rifts). The GM sets the base DC at 14 (Marna is stubborn). With the reduction, DC becomes 12.

Outcome: Elara rolls a full success. The bread is perfect—flaky crust, soft interior, fragrant with honey. Marna accepts it, and the gesture opens a conversation. By the festival, Marna and Tobin are civil, if not warm. The community tension eases. Elara’s bakery gains a reputation for peacemaking as well as pastries.

Partial Alternative: On a partial success, the bread is good, but the gesture is interpreted as taking sides. Marna accepts it gladly, but Tobin feels slighted. The rift shifts rather than mends. Elara now has to bake a second loaf for Tobin to restore balance—more work, more time, more tension before resolution.

Example 2: Festival Organizer Under Pressure

Context: The Midsummer Festival is tomorrow, and a supply wagon has broken down miles outside town. Half the decorations, most of the candles, and the centerpiece maypole ribbons are stuck in the mud. Kael, a Trained Festival Organizer, must improvise.

Action: Kael gathers available volunteers, reallocates tasks, and sends runners to nearby farms to borrow materials. He coordinates three parallel efforts: repairing the wagon, sourcing substitute decorations, and reassuring increasingly anxious vendors.

Check: The GM frames this as an Event Planning check with three clocks: Wagon Repair (4 segments), Substitute Decorations (4 segments), and Vendor Morale (6 segments). Kael has advantage (Festival Organizer proficiency) and can reduce chaos clocks by 1 segment on a full success.

Outcome: Kael rolls and achieves a full success on the coordination check. He advances Wagon Repair by 2 segments (volunteers work efficiently under his direction), Substitute Decorations by 2 segments (local farmers donate ribbon and garlands), and Vendor Morale by 3 segments (Kael’s calm confidence spreads). The chaos clock, which was at 3 segments, reduces by 1. The festival happens on time, with a patchwork charm that becomes its own story.

Partial Alternative: On a partial, Kael fixes two of the three problems but not the third. The wagon is repaired and decorations sourced, but vendor morale cracks—several pull out, leaving gaps in the market. The festival happens, but it feels diminished. Kael gains a reputation for competence under pressure, but also for cutting corners. Next time, some vendors might hesitate to commit.

Example 3: Herbalist Diagnosing Blight

Context: The village’s medicinal herb garden is wilting. Leaves yellow, stems droop, and the usually fragrant plants smell faintly of rot. Sienna, a Trained Herbalist, investigates.

Action: Sienna examines the soil, roots, and nearby plants. She checks for pests, water quality, and signs of disease.

Check: The GM calls for a Gardening check with advantage (Herbalist proficiency grants advantage on checks involving medicinal plants). The DC is 16 (the blight is subtle and unfamiliar).

Outcome: Sienna rolls a full success. She identifies the problem: runoff from a new tannery upstream is contaminating the water supply with trace chemicals. The blight is not disease, but poisoning. Armed with this knowledge, the village can address the source—either relocating the garden’s water intake or negotiating with the tannery to change their disposal practices.

Partial Alternative: On a partial, Sienna identifies that something is wrong with the water, but not the exact cause. She recommends stopping irrigation temporarily and switching to rainwater collection. This buys time and prevents further damage, but does not solve the root issue. The blight slows but does not stop. Sienna must investigate further, perhaps seeking help from a Trained Chemist or Water Engineer in a nearby town—turning the problem into a quest rather than a quick fix.

GM Guidance: Making Cozy Proficiencies Matter

Cozy proficiencies thrive in campaigns where relationships, traditions, and community matter. Here are strategies for making them feel meaningful:

Tie proficiencies to community needs: A Baker is more valuable when food is culturally significant, a Festival Organizer matters more when festivals are community anchors, and a Herbalist shines when access to professional medicine is limited or expensive.

Let proficiencies open narrative doors: Characters with cozy proficiencies should gain access to social networks, resources, and information unavailable to others. The Town Crier hears rumors first. The Chef is invited to private dinners where deals are made. The Seamstress knows who is commissioning wedding clothes and who is mending traveling cloaks—both signal change.

Use downtime as story fuel: Downtime is not dead time. When a character spends downtime running a bakery, ask what happens. Do they notice someone hoarding flour? Does a traveling merchant offer rare spices? Does a regular customer stop showing up? Downtime proficiency use should generate hooks, complications, and opportunities.

Embrace partials creatively: Partial successes in cozy contexts should not feel like failures. They should feel like life—messy, complicated, bittersweet. The bread is delicious, but now you are out of flour for the week. The festival is a success, but you are exhausted and owe three favors. The herb remedy works, but the patient is grumpy about the taste and complains loudly.

Celebrate small victories: Cozy proficiencies measure success in warmth, comfort, and connection. A perfect loaf of bread, a smoothly run festival, a garden brought back to health—these are victories. Treat them as such. Let players feel the satisfaction of work done well, care given freely, and communities strengthened.

Chapter 14: Fantasy Genre Proficiencies

Fantasy proficiencies embody the specialized training that separates adventurers from common folk. They represent martial discipline, arcane study, wilderness expertise, and social refinement honed through apprenticeship, guild membership, or hard-won experience. These proficiencies enable characters to survive dungeons, navigate courts, and master the strange and wondrous.

Genre Overview

Fantasy settings blend magic and steel, ancient lore and frontier survival. Proficiencies in this genre enable characters to: - Master weapons, armor, and tactical combat techniques - Study and manipulate magical forces through ritual, alchemy, or scholarly discipline - Navigate wilderness terrain, track quarry, and endure harsh conditions - Engage with noble courts, merchant guilds, and arcane societies

Fantasy proficiencies often unlock access to specialized equipment, restricted knowledge, or social networks. A character with Armorer can maintain enchanted plate; a character with Ritualist can perform rites that others dare not attempt; a character with Court Etiquette can navigate noble intrigue without giving offense.

Cross-reference: See Chapter 8: Fantasy Skills for related skills like Melee Combat, Arcana, Survival, and Persuasion.

Proficiencies

First Aid — Trained

Description: You have learned to stabilize the wounded under pressure—compress bleeding, splint breaks, and keep someone breathing until proper healing arrives. You are not a miracle worker, but you are the difference between life and death.

Benefits: - Advantage on Medicine checks to stabilize dying or critically wounded characters - Reduce DC by 2 when treating injuries in clean or prepared conditions (camp, infirmary) - Prevent conditions from worsening (Bleeding, Poisoned, Exhausted) with timely intervention - Reduce recovery clocks for injuries by 1 segment on full success

Synergies: Medicine skill, Survival, Herbalist proficiency; pairs well with Clean Space, Infirmary, or Field Camp tags. Counters Bleeding, Poisoned, and Critical Injury conditions.

Downtime Activities: Run clinic shifts, train others in basic first aid, stockpile medical supplies, assist professional healers.

GM Usage: First Aid is battlefield triage, not miraculous healing. Use it to give characters meaningful choices under pressure (do you stabilize the fallen ally now, or advance while you have advantage?). Partials should save lives but introduce costs—the patient lives but gains Exhausted, or the treatment works but consumes a precious healing kit. First Aid does not erase consequences; it buys time.

Armorer — Trained

Description: You know the language of rivets, straps, and tempered steel. Armor under your care stays fitted, functional, and ready for the next blow.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when repairing, fitting, or maintaining armor - Advantage on Crafting checks related to armor construction or modification - Repair damaged armor during short rests or downtime, restoring functionality - Identify armor quality, enchantments, and weaknesses on inspection - Fit armor to unusual body types (smaller or larger than human standard)

Synergies: Crafting skill, Smithing, Weapon Master proficiency; pairs well with Workshop, Forge, or Armory tags.

Downtime Activities: Repair and maintain party armor, take commissions for custom work, train apprentices, source rare materials (mithral, dragonscale), identify and appraise looted armor.

GM Usage: Armor degrades. Straps snap, plates crack, joints stiffen. Armorers keep characters protected. Use equipment wear as pacing mechanisms—after brutal fights, armor needs attention. Partials might restore function but leave cosmetic damage (cracked paint, dented plates that restrict movement slightly) or require more time than available. Let Armorers shine when the party finds exotic armor that needs adjustment or when negotiating with smiths and guilds.

Weapon Master — Expert

Description: You have transcended competence and entered mastery. Your chosen weapon is an extension of intent—no wasted motion, no hesitation, no uncertainty.

Benefits: - Choose one weapon type (longsword, bow, spear, etc.). Gain advantage on all combat checks with that weapon. - Reduce DC by 3 when performing advanced techniques (disarms, precision strikes, defensive maneuvers) with your mastered weapon - Recognize weapon craftsmanship, balance, and quality on sight - Teach weapon techniques to others, granting them temporary advantage during training scenes - Perform weapon maintenance that extends durability and prevents breakage

Synergies: Melee Combat skill, Archery, Tactics; pairs well with Training Yard, Arena, or Battlefield tags.

Downtime Activities: Train students, participate in tournaments, maintain weapon collections, craft or commission masterwork weapons, study exotic weapon styles.

GM Usage: Weapon Masters should feel exceptional in combat with their chosen weapon. Grant them narrative spotlight moments—disarming the villain’s blade, shooting the rope mid-swing, holding a doorway against overwhelming odds. Partials might achieve tactical goals (you disarm the opponent) but create new complications (the weapon falls into the chasm, now unrecoverable). Weapon mastery is also a social signal—other warriors recognize and respect it, opening doors to martial societies and honor duels.

Apothecary — Novice

Description: You brew remedies from roots, resins, and careful patience. Your potions are not magical, but they are effective—balms that numb pain, tinctures that ease fever, poultices that draw out infection.

Benefits: - Brew basic remedies (pain relief, fever reduction, mild antidotes) given time and ingredients - Advantage on Herbalism or Alchemy checks when creating mundane potions - Reduce DC by 2 when identifying herbs, fungi, or alchemical components - Create poultices that reduce recovery time for Poisoned or Diseased conditions

Synergies: Alchemy skill, Herbalism, Medicine; pairs well with Workshop, Apothecary Shop, or Forest Edge tags.

Downtime Activities: Gather reagents, brew potions for trade or party use, experiment with new recipes, consult on ailments, maintain ingredient stocks.

GM Usage: Apothecaries occupy the space between folk remedies and magical potions. Use them to create resource loops—characters gather rare mushrooms during exploration to fuel potion-making during downtime. Partials might produce weaker versions of intended potions (half duration, reduced effect) or require substitutions that add side effects (the antidote works, but causes drowsiness). Let Apothecaries solve problems through preparation and resourcefulness.

Scribe — Trained

Description: You preserve knowledge with ink and care. Your hand is steady, your script clear, and your copies faithful. You encode messages, translate tongues, and ensure that words outlast their speakers.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when copying, encoding, or decoding written texts - Advantage on Lore checks involving ancient or obscure scripts - Recognize forgeries, alterations, or coded messages in written documents - Copy texts quickly and accurately, preserving formatting and marginalia - Encode messages using simple ciphers or symbolic notation

Synergies: Lore skill, Languages, Librarian proficiency; pairs well with Archive, Library, or Scriptorium tags.

Downtime Activities: Copy manuscripts for clients, translate documents, encode private correspondence, maintain archives, research obscure topics, authenticate historical texts.

GM Usage: Scribes are information gatekeepers. Use them to reveal hidden context in ancient texts, detect altered records, or encode secrets that must travel safely. Partials might complete copies with minor errors (a name misspelled, a date transposed) that become clues or complications later. Scribes also interface with libraries, guilds, and institutions—access to rare texts often requires their mediation.

Ritualist — Expert

Description: You perform rites that touch the fabric of reality—blessings that hold, wards that endure, summonings that answer. You understand that ritual is not magic by rote, but magic by relationship.

Benefits: - Perform complex magical rituals (wards, summonings, consecrations) that others cannot attempt - Reduce DC by 3 when conducting rituals in prepared or consecrated spaces - Recognize ritual components, diagrams, and symbolic structures on sight - Adapt rituals to unusual circumstances (limited components, hostile environments, time pressure) - Teach ritual techniques to others, though mastery takes time

Synergies: Arcana skill, Religion, Occult Lore; pairs well with Temple, Sacred Ground, or Ritual Circle tags. Counters or inflicts Cursed, Hexed, or Warded conditions.

Downtime Activities: Study ritual traditions, prepare components, consecrate spaces, perform commissioned rituals (weddings, funerals, protective wards), research lost or forbidden rites.

GM Usage: Rituals are slow magic with lasting impact. They take time, require preparation, and carry consequences. Use Ritualist checks to frame high-stakes moments (warding the village against undead, summoning a spirit guide, consecrating a desecrated temple). Partials should achieve the ritual’s goal but introduce complications (the ward holds but requires daily renewal; the summoning succeeds but the spirit is offended by your haste). Ritualists walk the line between the sacred and the dangerous—let them feel powerful and responsible in equal measure.

Huntsman — Trained

Description: You know how to read trails, prepare game, and survive where others would starve. The wilderness is not hostile to you—it is legible.

Benefits: - Advantage on Survival checks to track game, forage for food, or navigate wilderness - Reduce DC by 2 when preparing game (butchering, preserving, cooking) - Set snares and traps that provide food or early warning - Recognize animal behavior patterns and predict movements - Move quietly through natural terrain, reducing chance of alerting prey or predators

Synergies: Survival skill, Animal Handling, Tracking; pairs well with Forest, Wilderness, or Hunting Ground tags.

Downtime Activities: Hunt to provide food for the party or settlement, set traplines, scout terrain, sell furs and hides, teach wilderness survival.

GM Usage: Huntsmen turn wilderness into resource. Use them to mitigate survival challenges (food, navigation, shelter) so players can focus on other story elements. Partials might yield food but at a cost (time spent hunting delays travel; a successful hunt attracts predators or rivals). Let Huntsmen notice environmental storytelling (disturbed game trails signal danger; abundant prey signals safe territory; absent wildlife signals corruption or predation).

Tracker — Trained

Description: Footprints, broken twigs, disturbed stones—you read the passage of others as clearly as text. Every creature leaves a signature; you simply know how to see it.

Benefits: - Advantage on Survival or Investigation checks to follow tracks or identify signs of passage - Reduce DC by 2 when tracking in familiar terrain or ideal conditions (mud, snow, soft earth) - Estimate how long ago tracks were made and how many individuals passed - Identify species, size, and load carried from track patterns - Notice attempts to conceal or obscure tracks

Synergies: Survival skill, Investigation, Huntsman proficiency; pairs well with Wilderness, Urban, or Dungeon tags depending on specialty.

Downtime Activities: Work as a guide or scout, track bounties or fugitives, teach tracking skills, study unusual or magical creature signs.

GM Usage: Tracking is detective work in motion. Use it to turn pursuits into tense skill challenges (can you catch up before nightfall? before they cross the river?). Partials might follow tracks successfully but lose time (you find the trail, but it loops and backtracks, costing hours) or gain partial information (you know something passed here, but cannot identify what). Let Trackers notice when quarry is aware of pursuit (deliberate misdirection, traps set along the trail).

Climber — Trained

Description: Vertical surfaces are puzzles, not barriers. You see handholds where others see smooth stone, and you ascend with confidence born from practice and judgment.

Benefits: - Advantage on Athletics checks to climb natural or constructed surfaces - Reduce DC by 2 when using climbing gear (rope, pitons, harnesses) - Rig secure climbing routes for others, granting them advantage - Assess climbing hazards (loose stone, ice, weather risks) before attempting ascent - Descend quickly and safely when speed is critical

Synergies: Athletics skill, Survival, Mountaineer specialization; pairs well with Elevated, Cliffside, or Mountain tags.

Downtime Activities: Scout vertical routes, teach climbing techniques, maintain climbing gear, guide expeditions into mountainous or vertical terrain.

GM Usage: Climbers open alternative paths. Use vertical terrain to reward their expertise—the guarded gate is impassable, but the wall is merely difficult. Partials might ascend successfully but slowly (your caution costs time; pursuers gain ground) or create unstable routes (you make it, but the rope frays; the next climber risks a fall). Let climbing be tense and consequential—falls should matter, and success should feel earned.

Court Etiquette — Novice

Description: You know the bows, pauses, and phrasings that open doors in noble courts. You navigate rank, precedence, and unspoken rules with practiced ease.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when making formal requests, petitions, or appeals to nobility - Advantage on Persuasion checks in courtly or formal social settings - Recognize social hierarchies, heraldry, and protocol at a glance - Avoid giving offense through ignorance of custom or rank - Gain access to formal events and spaces that would otherwise be closed

Synergies: Persuasion skill, Insight, Diplomat proficiency; pairs well with Royal Court, Formal Banquet, or Noble Estate tags.

Downtime Activities: Attend courtly functions, cultivate noble patrons, study heraldry and lineage, mediate formal disputes, navigate bureaucracy.

GM Usage: Court Etiquette is social armor—it protects against unintentional offense and opens pathways through hierarchies. Use it to differentiate social challenges: a character without Court Etiquette might offend a duke by addressing him incorrectly; a character with it navigates the same conversation smoothly. Partials might achieve goals without giving offense, but fail to make strong impressions (your petition is accepted, but barely; you are granted audience, but only briefly). Let Court Etiquette be a lens through which characters perceive and manipulate power structures.

Diplomat — Trained

Description: You broker peace, negotiate treaties, and find middle ground between entrenched positions. You understand that most conflicts are solvable if both sides can save face.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when mediating disputes, negotiating treaties, or de-escalating conflicts - Advantage on Persuasion checks involving formal diplomacy or high-stakes negotiation - Recognize negotiation tactics (bluffs, delays, concessions) and respond effectively - Maintain neutrality and credibility even when representing partisan interests - Facilitate communication between hostile or culturally distinct parties

Synergies: Persuasion skill, Insight, Court Etiquette proficiency; pairs well with Neutral Ground, Embassy, or Treaty Hall tags.

Downtime Activities: Mediate disputes, negotiate trade agreements, represent factions or nations, cultivate diplomatic contacts, study cultural customs and languages.

GM Usage: Diplomats solve problems through words rather than swords. Use them to create alternatives to violence—the war might be prevented if someone can broker terms both sides will accept. Partials might achieve ceasefires or temporary agreements that buy time but do not resolve underlying tensions. Let Diplomats navigate complex social terrain where every word carries weight, and where success means reading context as much as stating terms.

Worked Examples

Example 1: First Aid Under Fire

Context: During a skirmish with bandits, Rowan the fighter takes a crossbow bolt to the shoulder and collapses, bleeding heavily. Mira, a Trained First Aid specialist, must stabilize him while arrows still fly.

Action: Mira drags Rowan behind an overturned cart (cover) and presses cloth against the wound to slow bleeding. She needs to stabilize him before blood loss becomes critical.

Check: The GM calls for a Medicine check. Base DC is 16 (critical injury under hostile conditions). Mira has advantage (First Aid proficiency) and can reduce the DC by 2 if conditions are clean or prepared. The cart provides improvised cover but is not clean—GM rules no DC reduction. Mira rolls with advantage.

Outcome: Mira rolls a full success. She compresses the wound, slows the bleeding, and stabilizes Rowan. He is unconscious but stable—he will live if they can retreat and get proper care. Mira’s quick action prevents the Bleeding condition from advancing to Critical.

Partial Alternative: On a partial success, Mira stabilizes Rowan, but he gains the Exhausted condition from blood loss. He is alive but weakened—if combat continues, he cannot fight effectively. Additionally, Mira uses her last bandages. The party must find or improvise medical supplies before the next injury.

Failure Alternative: On a failure, Mira cannot stop the bleeding in time. Rowan’s condition worsens to Critical. Now someone must carry him to safety while Mira attempts a second, more desperate check—or the party must retreat immediately and hope they reach a healer before he dies.

Example 2: Ritualist Consecrating a Defiled Shrine

Context: The party discovers a shrine to a nature deity that has been defiled by cultists. Dark runes cover the altar, the sacred spring runs black, and an oppressive presence lingers. Elira, an Expert Ritualist, offers to consecrate the shrine and restore its sanctity.

Action: Elira studies the defilement, gathers components (blessed water, sacred herbs, chalk for new runes), and begins a multi-hour ritual to cleanse and re-consecrate the shrine.

Check: The GM frames this as a ritual with a 6-segment clock (Consecration Progress). Each successful Arcana or Religion check advances the clock. Base DC is 18 (powerful defilement). Elira has advantage and reduces DC by 3 in prepared spaces—she has spent an hour preparing the ritual circle, so the DC becomes 15.

Outcome: Elira rolls a full success on the first check, advancing the clock by 2 segments. Over the next several hours, she completes the ritual. The dark runes fade, the spring runs clear, and the oppressive presence lifts. The shrine is restored, and the nature deity’s blessing returns—granting travelers who rest there a minor boon (advantage on the next Survival check).

Partial Alternative: On a partial success, Elira advances the clock by 1 segment, but the defilement resists. The ritual requires additional components she did not bring (rare incense, a silver offering). The party must venture to a nearby ruin to retrieve them, turning the ritual into a quest. Alternatively, the ritual succeeds but Elira is Exhausted from the spiritual exertion, unable to perform another ritual until she rests.

Failure Alternative: On a failure, the defilement reacts violently. The dark runes flare, summoning a minor corrupted spirit that must be fought or banished. The shrine remains defiled, and Elira must start over—but now the cultists know someone is interfering and may send reinforcements.

Example 3: Tracker Pursuing a Fleeing Assassin

Context: An assassin has murdered a noble and fled into the forest. The party gives chase, but the assassin has a head start and knows the terrain. Kael, a Trained Tracker, leads the pursuit.

Action: Kael examines the forest floor—broken branches, disturbed leaves, footprints in soft earth. He estimates the assassin’s speed and direction, then leads the party forward.

Check: The GM frames this as a pursuit with competing clocks: Pursuit Progress (6 segments, party) vs. Escape Progress (6 segments, assassin). Kael rolls Survival to track. Base DC is 14 (forest terrain, recent tracks). Kael has advantage (Tracker proficiency) and reduces DC by 2 in ideal conditions (recent rain left mud). DC becomes 12.

Outcome: Kael rolls a full success, advancing Pursuit Progress by 2 segments. The GM advances Escape Progress by 1 (the assassin is fast but not cautious). Kael’s expertise keeps the party on track without losing time. Over several rounds of tracking, the party closes the gap and corners the assassin at a river crossing.

Partial Alternative: On a partial, Kael advances Pursuit Progress by 1 segment but also advances Escape Progress by 1 (the trail loops and backtracks, costing time). The pursuit becomes tense—can they catch up before the assassin reaches allies or exits the forest?

Failure Alternative: On a failure, Kael loses the trail. The party must stop and search, allowing Escape Progress to advance by 2 segments. The assassin gains ground, and the party must decide: press on with incomplete information (risking the wrong direction), or abandon the chase and pursue other leads.

GM Guidance: Making Fantasy Proficiencies Matter

Fantasy proficiencies bridge the gap between competence and mastery. Here are strategies for making them feel meaningful:

Reward specialization: Fantasy campaigns often feature diverse challenges—combat, exploration, social intrigue, magical mysteries. Let proficiencies grant clear advantages in their domains. A Weapon Master should dominate in their weapon’s niche. A Ritualist should access magic unavailable to others. A Diplomat should turn conflicts into conversations.

Make equipment and access matter: Proficiencies often interact with gear, guilds, and locations. Armorers need forges and tools. Scribes need libraries and inks. Ritualists need sacred spaces and rare components. Let these requirements create quests and complications—finding a master scribe to teach advanced techniques, securing access to a guild’s restricted archives, gathering components for a critical ritual.

Use proficiencies to differentiate characters: In a party of warriors, the one with Weapon Master feels distinct from the one with Climber or First Aid. Let proficiencies define roles and spotlight moments. The climber scales the tower while others find another way. The ritualist performs the consecration while others guard the perimeter. The diplomat negotiates while others prepare for the possibility of violence.

Embrace the limits of proficiencies: Proficiencies grant expertise, not omnipotence. A Tracker can follow trails, but cannot summon trails where none exist. A Ritualist can perform rituals, but cannot ignore material costs or time requirements. These limits create meaningful choices—do we wait for the ritual, or act now without its protection?

Let proficiencies grow with the campaign: As characters advance proficiency tiers, their capabilities should expand noticeably. A Novice Apothecary brews simple remedies. A Trained Apothecary creates potent potions. An Expert Apothecary might develop unique formulas or teach others. This progression rewards long-term investment and gives players goals to pursue.

Chapter 15: Horror/Mystery Genre Proficiencies

Horror and mystery proficiencies reflect the specialized knowledge required to investigate the inexplicable, survive the dangerous, and confront the forbidden. They represent forensic rigor, occult scholarship, urban survival instincts, and the psychological fortitude to pursue truth into dark places. These proficiencies enable characters to solve mysteries, endure terrors, and emerge with answers—though not always unscathed.

Genre Overview

Horror and mystery settings blend investigation with dread, revelation with cost. Proficiencies in this genre enable characters to: - Analyze crime scenes, decode evidence, and reconstruct events with forensic precision - Study occult lore, recognize supernatural signs, and perform protective rituals - Survive hostile urban environments, evade pursuit, and navigate dangerous social networks - Provide medical or psychological care under extreme conditions

These proficiencies often carry risks. Studying forbidden texts can erode sanity. Performing occult rituals can attract unwanted attention. Investigating too deeply can make you a target. Horror and mystery proficiencies grant power, but demand caution.

Cross-reference: See Chapter 9: Horror/Mystery Skills for related skills like Research, Forensics, Composure, and Occult Lore.

Proficiencies

Forensics — Trained

Description: You read crime scenes like texts. Blood spatter patterns, fiber evidence, tool marks—each tells a story if you know the grammar. You preserve, document, and extract meaning from chaos.

Benefits: - Advantage on Investigation checks to analyze physical evidence at crime scenes - Reduce DC by 2 when preserving or documenting evidence under proper conditions (lab, controlled environment) - Recognize trace evidence (fibers, hairs, residues) and understand their significance - Maintain chain of custody, ensuring evidence remains admissible or credible - Estimate time of death, cause of injury, or sequence of events from physical signs

Synergies: Investigation skill, Medicine, Science; pairs well with Crime Scene, Laboratory, or Morgue tags. Interacts with Bleeding, Poisoned, or Dead conditions to extract information.

Downtime Activities: Process evidence in lab settings, consult on cold cases, train others in evidence collection, maintain forensic equipment, testify or report findings.

GM Usage: Forensics turns crime scenes into information sources. Use successful checks to reveal clues (the victim fought back; the killer wore gloves; this was staged to look like suicide). Partials might yield partial truths (you know the weapon type, but not the exact model) or require additional time or resources (the analysis works, but you need access to a better microscope). Let Forensics specialists feel competent and methodical, but also vulnerable—gathering evidence often means lingering in dangerous places.

Archivist — Novice

Description: You know how to navigate archives, catalogs, and collections. You understand classification systems, preservation methods, and the patience required to sift truth from dust.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when searching archives, libraries, or records for specific information - Advantage on Research checks in organized collections (libraries, historical societies, databases) - Recognize misfiled, altered, or missing records - Preserve fragile documents and artifacts safely - Compile research into usable summaries quickly

Synergies: Research skill, History, Librarian proficiency; pairs well with Library, Archive, or Museum tags.

Downtime Activities: Catalog collections, research obscure topics, authenticate documents, consult for scholars or investigators, maintain archives.

GM Usage: Archivists are information hunters. Use them to frame research as active challenges rather than passive lookups (you find the record, but it is in a restricted section; the information exists, but is cataloged under an obsolete system you must decode). Partials might yield fragments of truth (you find references to the ritual, but not the full instructions) or attract attention (the librarian notices your interest and asks uncomfortable questions). Let archives be atmospheric—dim reading rooms, crumbling ledgers, the sense that you are not alone among the stacks.

Cryptographer — Trained

Description: You decode secrets hidden in patterns. Ciphers, codes, symbolic languages—you unravel them with logic, intuition, and persistence. You also encode, ensuring your own secrets stay safe.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when decoding ciphers, encrypted messages, or symbolic texts - Advantage on Investigation or Research checks involving coded information - Recognize cipher types (substitution, transposition, symbolic) on sight - Create simple to moderate ciphers to protect sensitive information - Detect patterns in seemingly random data (repeated phrases, keyword hints, frequency analysis clues)

Synergies: Investigation skill, Lore (Forbidden), Mathematics; pairs well with Archive, Secret Society, or Hidden Message tags.

Downtime Activities: Decode intercepted messages, encode sensitive correspondence, study historical cipher systems, consult on cryptanalysis, develop new encoding methods.

GM Usage: Cryptography transforms inscriptions, letters, and documents into puzzles. Use successful checks to unlock hidden layers of meaning (the diary is in cipher; once decoded, it reveals the cult’s meeting location). Partials might decode portions (you crack half the message, enough to know it is urgent but not what it says) or take longer than available time (you will break it, but it will take days, and the ritual is tonight). Let ciphers be clues and obstacles—the information exists, but you must work for it.

Occult Scholar — Trained

Description: You have studied traditions most dismiss as superstition. You know which symbols ward, which summon, and which must never be drawn. Your knowledge is a tool, but also a burden.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when identifying occult symbols, rituals, or supernatural phenomena - Advantage on Occult Lore checks to recall or research esoteric traditions - Recognize magical or supernatural signs (wards, curses, summoning marks) - Craft simple protective charms or wards against supernatural threats - Understand the costs and dangers of occult practices, avoiding common traps

Synergies: Occult Lore skill, Research, Ritualist proficiency; pairs well with Forbidden Library, Occult Site, or Haunted tags. May counter or inflict Cursed, Hexed, or Haunted conditions.

Downtime Activities: Study forbidden texts, research supernatural phenomena, craft protective charms, consult on occult matters, maintain occult libraries or collections.

GM Usage: Occult Scholars walk the line between knowledge and danger. Use successful checks to provide context and warnings (this symbol summons, not binds; the ritual requires a blood price; the entity cannot be destroyed, only contained). Partials might yield correct but incomplete information (you know the ward works against spirits, but not which kind) or cost sanity (you find the answer, but the text describes things that disturb your sleep for weeks—gain a minor psychological condition). Let occult knowledge be powerful and risky.

Medium — Expert

Description: You perceive the threshold between life and death. You sense presences others miss, hear whispers in the silence, and communicate with those who linger. This gift is not always gentle.

Benefits: - Sense supernatural presences (ghosts, spirits, lingering emotional echoes) in a location - Advantage on Occult Lore or Insight checks when attempting to communicate with or understand spirits - Reduce DC by 3 when assisting wards, banishments, or exorcisms with spiritual insight - Gain impressions or visions related to haunted locations or objects - Recognize when a location or person is spiritually afflicted

Synergies: Occult Lore skill, Insight, Ritualist proficiency; pairs well with Haunted, Sacred Ground, or Graveyard tags. Interacts with Haunted, Possessed, or Cursed conditions.

Downtime Activities: Conduct seances, investigate hauntings, assist exorcisms, consult on spiritually troubled locations, meditate to process spiritual impressions.

GM Usage: Mediums are supernatural sensors. Use them to reveal hidden layers of setting (the room feels cold; you sense anger lingering here; a presence watches you). Partials might sense presence without understanding intent (something is here, but you cannot tell if it is hostile or benign) or misidentify type (you sense a ghost, but it is actually a demon mimicking one). Let Medium abilities be evocative but not omniscient—spirits do not always tell the truth, and some presences are too alien to communicate clearly. Mediums also attract attention; entities notice when they are noticed.

Urban Survivalist — Trained

Description: You thrive in the cracks of civilization. You know where to sleep safely, how to move unseen, and which alleys lead where. The city is hostile to the unwary, but you are neither.

Benefits: - Advantage on Streetwise or Stealth checks in urban environments - Reduce DC by 2 when finding shelter, food, or safe routes in cities - Recognize urban dangers (gang territories, surveillance zones, unsafe structures) at a glance - Navigate city infrastructure (sewers, rooftops, maintenance tunnels) safely - Blend into urban crowds, reducing chance of detection or pursuit

Synergies: Streetwise skill, Stealth, Navigation; pairs well with Urban Ruins, Alleyway, or Undercity tags.

Downtime Activities: Map urban terrain, establish safe houses, cultivate street contacts, scavenge supplies, teach urban survival techniques.

GM Usage: Urban Survivalists turn hostile cities into navigable terrain. Use them to frame challenges as opportunities (the direct route is watched, but you know the rooftops; the safe house is compromised, but you know three alternatives). Partials might achieve safety but at a cost (you find shelter, but it is in gang territory—now you owe favors) or with delays (you reach the safe zone, but it takes twice as long, and pursuers gain ground). Let cities be characters—complex, dangerous, but knowable to those with expertise.

Lockpicking — Novice

Description: You understand the simple elegance of pins, tumblers, and tension. Locks are not barriers; they are invitations to those with patience and skill.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when picking simple to moderate locks - Advantage on Thievery checks involving mechanical locks or latches - Recognize lock types, quality, and potential traps on inspection - Open locks quietly and without leaving obvious signs of tampering - Improvise lockpicking tools from common objects if proper tools are unavailable

Synergies: Thievery skill, Stealth, Investigation; pairs well with Locked Door, Secure Vault, or Restricted Area tags.

Downtime Activities: Practice on various lock types, source or craft lockpicking tools, consult on security systems, teach lockpicking techniques (ethically or otherwise).

GM Usage: Lockpicking opens literal and metaphorical doors. Use it to provide alternative routes (you cannot bluff past the guard, but you can bypass the lock). Partials might open locks but trigger alarms, leave evidence of tampering, or take longer than safe (you open it, but someone heard the scratching and is coming to investigate). Let lockpicking be tense—time pressure, risk of discovery, the possibility of traps. Not all locks are mechanical; some require keys obtained through other means.

Field Medic — Trained

Description: You stabilize trauma under pressure. Gunshot wounds, lacerations, chemical burns—you have seen it all and kept people breathing through it. You are not a surgeon, but you buy time for one to arrive.

Benefits: - Advantage on Medicine checks to stabilize critical injuries or treat trauma - Reduce DC by 2 when treating injuries in improvised or hostile conditions (battlefield, crime scene, safe house) - Prevent conditions from worsening (Bleeding, Poisoned, Shock) with timely intervention - Triage multiple casualties efficiently, prioritizing those most likely to survive with treatment - Improvise medical supplies from available materials when proper equipment is lacking

Synergies: Medicine skill, First Aid, Science; pairs well with Field Hospital, Safe House, or Crisis Scene tags. Counters Bleeding, Shock, Poisoned, and Critical Injury conditions.

Downtime Activities: Stockpile medical supplies, train others in trauma care, work shifts in clinics or emergency rooms, consult on medical cases, maintain field kits.

GM Usage: Field Medics operate in chaos. Use them to create high-stakes triage scenarios (three wounded, limited supplies, who do you treat first?). Partials should save lives but introduce complications (you stabilize the patient, but infection risk is high without proper antibiotics; you stop the bleeding, but the patient is weakened and cannot move without help). Let Field Medics feel competent and stressed—they save lives, but the costs are visible and the resources finite.

Psychiatrist — Expert

Description: You treat wounds that do not bleed. You listen, interpret, and guide those struggling with trauma, obsession, or dread. Your tools are words, insight, and patience.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 3 when diagnosing or treating psychological conditions (Traumatized, Obsessed, Paranoid, Phobic) - Advantage on Insight checks to understand psychological states or motivations - Provide therapeutic interventions that reduce severity or duration of mental conditions - Recognize signs of manipulation, delusion, or psychological breakdown in others - Offer counseling that grants temporary advantage on Composure checks to allies

Synergies: Insight skill, Medicine, Composure; pairs well with Safe Space, Therapy Session, or Confidential Setting tags. Interacts with Traumatized, Obsessed, Paranoid, Shaken, and similar psychological conditions.

Downtime Activities: Conduct therapy sessions, research psychological trauma, consult on mental health cases, provide support to traumatized allies, study psychological effects of supernatural exposure.

GM Usage: Psychiatrists address the toll that horror and mystery take on the mind. Use them to provide mechanical and narrative relief from psychological conditions (you can reduce Traumatized severity with therapy, but it takes time and safety). Partials might ease symptoms without resolving root causes (the nightmares lessen, but return under stress) or uncover deeper issues (treating the Obsession reveals it stems from a Curse, not psychology—now the problem is occult, not medical). Let Psychiatrists be valuable in campaigns that track sanity or psychological damage, and let them provide moments of care and recovery between horrors.

Worked Examples

Example 1: Forensics at a Murder Scene

Context: A wealthy patron has been found dead in his locked study. The door was bolted from the inside, the windows sealed. The police suspect suicide, but the family believes otherwise and has hired the investigators. Elena, a Trained Forensics specialist, examines the scene.

Action: Elena photographs the body position, measures blood spatter, and examines the wound. She notes the angle of entry, the lack of powder burns, and the position of the gun in the victim’s hand.

Check: The GM calls for an Investigation check to analyze the scene. Base DC is 16 (complex scene with contradictory evidence). Elena has advantage (Forensics proficiency) and reduces DC by 2 in proper conditions—she has brought her field kit and the scene is undisturbed, so DC becomes 14.

Outcome: Elena rolls a full success. She concludes: the wound angle is inconsistent with suicide; the gun placement is staged; and faint bruising on the victim’s wrist suggests he was restrained. This was murder, disguised to look like suicide. The locked room is a puzzle, not proof of self-harm. Elena’s findings redirect the investigation toward suspects with access to the study and knowledge of the victim’s habits.

Partial Alternative: On a partial success, Elena identifies that something is wrong (the wound angle is odd, the gun placement seems unnatural) but cannot conclusively rule out suicide. Her findings introduce doubt but not certainty. The investigation must continue, seeking additional evidence (witness statements, forensic analysis of the lock mechanism, toxicology reports).

Example 2: Medium Investigating a Haunting

Context: A family reports escalating disturbances in their home—cold spots, objects moving, whispers at night. Standard explanations (drafts, settling foundation, imagination) fail to account for the intensity and consistency. Marcus, an Expert Medium, is called to investigate.

Action: Marcus walks through the house, paying attention to his impressions. In the nursery, he feels overwhelming grief. In the basement, anger. He asks the family about the home’s history.

Check: The GM calls for an Occult Lore check to sense and interpret the presence. Base DC is 14 (moderate haunting, emotional but not malicious). Marcus has advantage and reduces DC by 3 (Medium proficiency in spiritual perception). DC becomes 11.

Outcome: Marcus rolls a full success. He perceives the presence clearly—a young woman who died in childbirth in this house decades ago. She lingers, drawn by unfinished grief and a need to protect children. The disturbances are not malicious; they are attempts to communicate or soothe. Marcus conducts a small ritual with the family’s permission, acknowledging the spirit and offering closure. The hauntings cease, replaced by an occasional sense of warmth—a protective presence, no longer agitated.

Partial Alternative: On a partial success, Marcus senses the presence but misinterprets intent. He perceives grief but also anger, and cannot tell if the spirit is protective or hostile. He recommends caution and further investigation (what happened here? why is the spirit agitated?). Alternatively, he senses the spirit but the contact disturbs him—he gains a minor Shaken condition from the intensity of the grief he felt.

Example 3: Urban Survivalist Evading Pursuit

Context: Nia has uncovered evidence implicating a powerful crime family in a series of disappearances. She is now being hunted through the city by enforcers who want the evidence destroyed—and her silenced. Nia, a Trained Urban Survivalist, must evade pursuit and reach a safe house across town.

Action: Nia ducks into an alley, climbs a fire escape to the rooftops, and navigates across buildings to avoid street-level pursuers. She knows the city’s layout and uses maintenance tunnels and shortcuts invisible to outsiders.

Check: The GM frames this as a chase with competing clocks: Escape Progress (6 segments, Nia) vs. Pursuit Progress (6 segments, enforcers). Nia rolls Streetwise or Stealth to evade. Base DC is 14 (urban pursuit, determined hunters). Nia has advantage (Urban Survivalist proficiency) and reduces DC by 2 in urban environments. DC becomes 12.

Outcome: Nia rolls a full success, advancing Escape Progress by 2 segments. The GM advances Pursuit Progress by 1 (the enforcers are organized but unfamiliar with rooftop routes). Over several rounds of evasion, Nia widens the gap and reaches the safe house undetected. She has bought time to analyze the evidence and plan her next move.

Partial Alternative: On a partial, Nia advances Escape Progress by 1 segment but also advances Pursuit Progress by 1 (she evades temporarily but leaves signs of passage—disturbed laundry on a rooftop, a kicked-over trash can). The chase continues, tense and uncertain. Alternatively, she reaches the safe house but realizes she was followed partway—the enforcers do not know exactly where she is, but they know the neighborhood. The safe house is compromised in the long term.

GM Guidance: Making Horror/Mystery Proficiencies Matter

Horror and mystery proficiencies transform investigation and survival from abstract challenges into concrete, tense interactions. Here are strategies for making them meaningful:

Make information valuable and costly: Proficiencies like Forensics, Cryptographer, and Occult Scholar provide information, but information in horror and mystery should come with cost or risk. Decoding the cipher takes time you do not have. Analyzing the crime scene means lingering where the killer might return. Studying the forbidden text risks sanity. Let players choose when to pay these costs.

Track psychological toll: Horror and mystery wear on characters. Use conditions like Traumatized, Obsessed, Shaken, or Paranoid to reflect cumulative stress. Proficiencies like Psychiatrist and Medium offer relief, making them valuable beyond their primary functions. Let characters recover, but make recovery take time and safety.

Let proficiencies reveal layers: Mysteries should unfold through investigation. Each successful check should peel back a layer—Forensics reveals the murder weapon; Archivist uncovers the victim’s secret identity; Cryptographer decodes the threatening letter. Build mysteries as onions, not locked boxes. Proficiencies are the knives that peel them.

Use partials to complicate, not block: Partial successes should advance the story with complications. You crack the cipher, but it implicates someone you trust. You sense the ghost, but now it knows you can perceive it. You evade pursuit, but abandon your supplies. Partials keep momentum while raising stakes.

Respect expertise, but not omniscience: Characters with proficiencies should feel competent in their domains, but not invulnerable. A Forensics expert can analyze evidence, but cannot magic evidence into existence. A Medium can sense spirits, but cannot control them. An Urban Survivalist can evade pursuit, but cannot outrun radios and organized search patterns indefinitely. Proficiencies grant advantage, not immunity to consequences.

Make failure interesting: Failed checks should not halt the investigation—they should redirect it or raise costs. You fail to decode the cipher in time, so the ritual happens; now you must stop it by other means. You fail to stabilize the victim; now someone died on your watch, and you carry that weight. Failure in horror and mystery should feel consequential, not frustrating.

Chapter 16: Modern Genre Proficiencies

Modern proficiencies represent expertise in contemporary trades, professions, and tactical disciplines. They reflect years of formal education, professional certification, hands-on experience, and specialized training. These proficiencies enable characters to navigate complex systems—legal, technical, medical, social—with competence and authority.

Genre Overview

Modern settings are defined by specialization and infrastructure. Proficiencies in this genre enable characters to: - Practice licensed professions (law, medicine, accounting) with credibility and access - Master technical systems (networks, vehicles, electronics) through training and certification - Employ tactical skills (firearms, surveillance, negotiations) honed through professional or military experience - Navigate social and institutional power structures with expertise and finesse

Modern proficiencies often come with credentials—degrees, licenses, certifications—that grant legal authority, professional access, or institutional trust. A character with the Doctor proficiency is not merely skilled in medicine; they are recognized as a physician, with all the responsibilities and privileges that entails.

Cross-reference: See Chapter 10: Modern Skills for related skills like Law, Medicine, Hacking, and Firearms.

Proficiencies

Lawyer — Trained

Description: You navigate legal systems with precision. You draft contracts, argue cases, and interpret statutes. You understand that law is both shield and weapon.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when interpreting laws, drafting legal documents, or arguing legal points - Advantage on Persuasion or Insight checks in legal contexts (courtrooms, negotiations, contract disputes) - Recognize legal risks, loopholes, and precedents quickly - Access legal databases, court records, and restricted legal resources - Gain professional credibility in legal settings, influencing judges, clerks, and law enforcement

Synergies: Law skill, Persuasion, Research; pairs well with Courtroom, Law Office, or Contract Negotiation tags.

Downtime Activities: Practice law (represent clients, draft documents, consult on cases), research case law, maintain bar certification, cultivate professional networks, testify as expert witness.

GM Usage: Lawyers shape outcomes through procedure and argument. Use them to turn legal obstacles into challenges rather than blocks (you cannot enter without a warrant, but you can obtain one with the right motion). Partials might win points but not cases (your argument is sound, but the judge defers ruling until more evidence is presented) or achieve legal victories with social costs (you win the case, but make an enemy of the opposing counsel). Let legal proficiency feel like chess—strategic, consequential, bound by rules that can be bent but not broken.

Doctor — Expert

Description: You diagnose, treat, and heal with the authority of medical training. You read test results, prescribe interventions, and make life-or-death calls with the confidence earned through years of practice.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 3 when diagnosing illnesses, treating injuries, or performing medical procedures - Advantage on Medicine checks in clinical or well-equipped settings (hospitals, clinics, ambulances) - Perform complex medical interventions (surgery, advanced trauma care, disease treatment) that others cannot attempt - Prescribe medications and treatments with legal and professional authority - Recognize medical malpractice, misdiagnosis, or unusual symptoms on sight

Synergies: Medicine skill, Science, Field Medic proficiency; pairs well with Hospital, Clinic, or Emergency Room tags. Counters Bleeding, Poisoned, Diseased, Shock, and Critical Injury conditions.

Downtime Activities: Maintain medical practice, perform surgeries or procedures, research medical conditions, consult on difficult cases, train medical students, maintain licensing and certifications.

GM Usage: Doctors operate at the expert tier of medical care. Use them to solve medical crises that would overwhelm lesser-trained characters (stabilizing multiple trauma patients, diagnosing rare diseases, performing emergency surgery). Partials should achieve medical goals but introduce complications (the surgery succeeds, but recovery will be long and requires rare medications; you diagnose the illness, but treatment requires equipment not available here). Let Doctors feel authoritative and burdened—they save lives, but the responsibility and the failures weigh heavily.

Accountant — Trained

Description: You track money through mazes of ledgers, invoices, and tax codes. You find discrepancies others miss and understand that numbers tell stories if you know the language.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when analyzing financial records, detecting fraud, or preparing financial documents - Advantage on Investigation or Research checks involving financial data, audits, or economic patterns - Recognize accounting irregularities (embezzlement, money laundering, tax evasion) quickly - Navigate tax codes, regulatory requirements, and financial compliance frameworks - Access financial databases and institutional records with professional credibility

Synergies: Finance skill, Law, Research; pairs well with Office, Audit Room, or Financial District tags.

Downtime Activities: Manage finances, conduct audits, prepare tax filings, consult on financial crimes, research economic trends, maintain professional certifications.

GM Usage: Accountants turn financial trails into narratives. Use them to uncover hidden connections (the shell company funnels money to the crime syndicate; the charity is a front for money laundering). Partials might identify irregularities but not their full scope (you know something is wrong with the books, but need more records to prove it) or attract attention from those being investigated (your audit triggers a threatening visit from someone with something to hide). Let accounting be investigative work—patient, meticulous, and revealing.

Network Admin — Trained
