Description: You understand the architecture of interconnected systems. You configure, secure, and troubleshoot networks that keep institutions running. You see data flows as rivers—and you know where the dams are.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when configuring, securing, or troubleshooting computer networks - Advantage on Hacking checks to penetrate, defend, or analyze network security - Recognize network vulnerabilities, intrusions, or unusual traffic patterns quickly - Map network topologies and identify critical nodes or access points - Maintain and optimize network infrastructure, preventing or mitigating outages

Synergies: Hacking skill, Engineering, Security Systems; pairs well with Server Room, Data Center, or Corporate Network tags.

Downtime Activities: Maintain network infrastructure, respond to security incidents, configure new systems, consult on cybersecurity, research exploits or patches, train junior admins.

GM Usage: Network Admins are digital gatekeepers. Use them to enable or prevent hacking attempts (the network is hardened, but you know where the backup server is; the firewall blocks you, but you can reconfigure it from the inside). Partials might gain access but trigger alerts (you are in, but the security team is responding), or harden systems but leave gaps (you patch most vulnerabilities, but miss one—and it is exploited later). Let network administration be both shield and key.

Mechanic — Trained

Description: You diagnose and repair machines with confidence. Engines, transmissions, electrical systems—you understand them not as mysteries, but as solvable problems.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when diagnosing or repairing vehicles, machinery, or mechanical systems - Advantage on Engineering checks involving automotive or mechanical repair - Perform field repairs under pressure (mid-chase, hostile environments, limited tools) - Identify sabotage, mechanical failure, or quality issues by inspection - Improvise repairs using available materials when proper parts are unavailable

Synergies: Engineering skill, Driving, Technical knowledge; pairs well with Garage, Workshop, or Roadside tags.

Downtime Activities: Repair and maintain vehicles, customize or upgrade machines, source parts, consult on mechanical issues, teach repair skills, operate a repair shop.

GM Usage: Mechanics keep the party mobile. Use them to mitigate vehicle breakdowns or sabotage (the car is dead, but you can bypass the kill switch and get it running). Partials might achieve temporary fixes that hold for a scene but fail under stress (the engine runs, but overheats if pushed too hard) or require improvised parts that reduce performance (you repair the transmission, but it is sluggish until you get proper parts). Let Mechanics feel essential when machines matter.

Electrician — Novice

Description: You work with the invisible current that powers modern life. You wire circuits, troubleshoot outages, and respect the fact that electricity does not forgive mistakes.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when installing, repairing, or troubleshooting electrical systems - Advantage on Engineering checks involving electrical wiring, power distribution, or safety - Recognize electrical hazards (overloaded circuits, faulty wiring, live wires) on sight - Restore power to buildings or systems after outages or sabotage - Safely disable electrical security systems or traps

Synergies: Engineering skill, Technical knowledge, Security Systems; pairs well with Building Interior, Power Plant, or Construction Site tags.

Downtime Activities: Perform electrical work, maintain power systems, consult on installations, source electrical components, teach basic electrical safety.

GM Usage: Electricians manipulate infrastructure. Use them to restore critical systems (the building is dark, but you can reroute power to the elevator) or disable obstacles (the security grid can be bypassed if you cut the right breaker). Partials might succeed but introduce risks (you restore power, but the system is unstable—it might blow a fuse under load) or take longer than safe (you can disable the alarm, but it will take ten minutes, and the guards patrol every five). Let electrical work feel technical and consequential.

Firearms Expert — Trained

Description: You have trained extensively with firearms. You know weapon handling, maintenance, marksmanship, and tactics. You treat firearms as tools that demand respect and discipline.

Benefits: - Advantage on Firearms checks in combat or tactical situations - Reduce DC by 2 when performing advanced techniques (precision shots, suppressive fire, weapon manipulation under stress) - Maintain firearms to prevent malfunctions and extend service life - Recognize firearm types, calibers, and modifications on sight - Teach firearm safety and marksmanship to others

Synergies: Firearms skill, Tactics, Combat training; pairs well with Firing Range, Urban Combat, or Tactical Scenario tags.

Downtime Activities: Train marksmanship, maintain weapon collections, teach firearms courses, participate in competitions, source ammunition and parts, consult on tactical scenarios.

GM Usage: Firearms Experts excel in ranged combat. Use them to create tactical advantages (you cover the retreat with suppressive fire; you disable the vehicle with a precision shot to the engine block). Partials might achieve tactical goals but create new problems (you suppress the enemy, but run low on ammo) or miss secondary effects (you hit the target, but the gunshot alerts reinforcements). Let firearms expertise feel professional and dangerous—controlled, but never safe.

Surveillance Tech — Trained

Description: You install, operate, and defeat surveillance systems. Cameras, bugs, trackers—you understand them as tools and targets.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when installing, operating, or defeating surveillance equipment - Advantage on Investigation or Hacking checks involving surveillance systems (CCTV, bugs, digital tracking) - Detect hidden surveillance devices (cameras, microphones, GPS trackers) through inspection or electronic sweeps - Monitor surveillance feeds efficiently, spotting details others miss - Disable or loop surveillance systems without leaving obvious traces

Synergies: Hacking skill, Electronics, Security Systems; pairs well with Security Room, Under Surveillance, or High-Security Area tags.

Downtime Activities: Install or maintain surveillance systems, conduct counter-surveillance sweeps, monitor feeds, consult on security, source surveillance equipment.

GM Usage: Surveillance Tech specialists control information flow. Use them to enable intelligence gathering (you tap the office phone; now you hear their plans) or counter enemy intelligence (you sweep the safe house and find three bugs). Partials might achieve partial coverage (you install the camera, but it only covers half the room) or introduce risks (you disable the surveillance, but the security team notices the blind spot and investigates). Let surveillance be a chess game of watchers and watched.

PR Specialist — Novice

Description: You manage reputations and narratives. You know how to frame stories, handle crises, and shape public perception. You understand that image is often more powerful than truth.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when managing public relations, crafting messaging, or handling media - Advantage on Persuasion or Networking checks in media or public-facing contexts - Mitigate reputational damage from scandals or controversies through strategic communication - Recognize spin, manipulation, and media narratives quickly - Cultivate media contacts and influence coverage of events

Synergies: Networking skill, Persuasion, Social Media; pairs well with Press Conference, Media Event, or Public Statement tags.

Downtime Activities: Manage public image, craft press releases, handle media inquiries, consult on communications strategy, monitor public sentiment, build media relationships.

GM Usage: PR Specialists shape narratives. Use them to mitigate social consequences (the scandal breaks, but your statement reframes it as a misunderstanding) or amplify successes (the charity event becomes front-page news, boosting your organization’s reputation). Partials might reduce damage but not eliminate it (the story still runs, but your side is heard) or succeed with unintended side effects (your statement goes viral, attracting attention you did not want). Let PR feel like narrative combat—words as weapons and shields.

Negotiator — Trained

Description: You broker deals under pressure. Hostage situations, labor disputes, business mergers—you find terms both sides can live with, even when emotions run high.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when negotiating high-stakes agreements, de-escalating conflicts, or mediating disputes - Advantage on Persuasion or Insight checks in negotiation contexts - Recognize negotiation tactics (bluffs, anchoring, good cop/bad cop) and counter them effectively - Maintain composure and credibility even in tense or hostile negotiations - Facilitate compromise and consensus-building among opposed parties

Synergies: Persuasion skill, Insight, Diplomat proficiency; pairs well with Neutral Ground, Negotiation Table, or Crisis Scene tags.

Downtime Activities: Mediate disputes, negotiate contracts or agreements, train in negotiation techniques, consult on conflict resolution, build networks with mediators and arbitrators.

GM Usage: Negotiators turn conflict into conversation. Use them to create alternatives to violence (the standoff can end peacefully if you broker terms acceptable to both sides). Partials might achieve temporary agreements (you negotiate a ceasefire, but it is fragile and might collapse) or get one side on board but not the other (the union accepts your terms, but management balks). Let negotiation be dynamic and risky—words matter, but so do body language, timing, and trust.

Worked Examples

Example 1: Doctor Under Pressure

Context: A car bombing has left three victims critically injured. The nearest hospital is twenty minutes away, and they might not survive the trip. Dr. Khalil, an Expert Doctor, must stabilize all three in a makeshift field clinic.

Action: Khalil triages quickly—one victim has internal bleeding (most critical), one has severe burns (stable for now), and one has a collapsed lung (critical but manageable). He focuses on the internal bleeding first, then the collapsed lung, then the burns.

Check: The GM frames this as a triage challenge with three injury clocks. Khalil has advantage and reduces DC by 3 in clinical settings—the makeshift clinic has some equipment (IV supplies, basic surgical tools), so the GM grants the reduction. Base DC is 18 (critical trauma, limited resources). With reduction, DC becomes 15.

Outcome: Khalil rolls a full success on the internal bleeding patient. He stabilizes the bleeding with a temporary clotting agent and IV fluids. He moves to the collapsed lung patient and successfully inserts a chest tube, re-inflating the lung. By the time the ambulance arrives, all three patients are stabilized enough to survive transport. Khalil is exhausted but has saved three lives.

Partial Alternative: On a partial, Khalil stabilizes the internal bleeding and collapsed lung patients, but the burns patient develops complications (Shock condition from pain and fluid loss). The patient survives, but recovery will be longer and more difficult. Alternatively, Khalil stabilizes all three but exhausts his medical supplies—if another emergency arises before resupply, he has limited options.

Example 2: Network Admin Infiltrating a Server

Context: The team needs to access a corporation’s internal email server to gather evidence of illegal activity. The server is behind a hardened firewall and monitored by security staff. Ravi, a Trained Network Admin, must penetrate the network without triggering alarms.

Action: Ravi maps the network topology from external reconnaissance, identifies a less-monitored access point (a contractor VPN), and uses valid credentials obtained from social engineering to log in. From there, he navigates to the email server.

Check: The GM calls for a Hacking check to penetrate the network. Base DC is 16 (hardened corporate network, active monitoring). Ravi has advantage (Network Admin proficiency) and reduces DC by 2 due to his understanding of network architecture. DC becomes 14.

Outcome: Ravi rolls a full success. He accesses the email server, extracts the needed emails, and logs out cleanly. The security logs show only routine contractor activity. The evidence is obtained without alerting the corporation.

Partial Alternative: On a partial, Ravi accesses the server and extracts the emails, but his presence triggers an automated alert. The security team investigates but does not immediately identify him as an intruder (they assume it is a misconfigured system). Ravi has a window—maybe hours, maybe a day—before they trace the intrusion. The team must act quickly on the evidence before the corporation realizes it has been breached.

Example 3: Negotiator De-escalating a Standoff

Context: A desperate father has taken his daughter’s teacher hostage in a school, demanding answers about his daughter’s expulsion. Police have surrounded the building, and tensions are escalating. Sara, a Trained Negotiator, is called in to de-escalate.

Action: Sara establishes communication with the father, listening to his grievances without judgment. She acknowledges his pain, validates his concern for his daughter, and begins exploring what he actually needs (a meeting with the principal, a review of the expulsion decision) rather than what he is demanding (immediate reinstatement).

Check: The GM calls for a Persuasion check to de-escalate. Base DC is 16 (high-stakes, emotionally charged, armed subject). Sara has advantage (Negotiator proficiency) and reduces DC by 2 in negotiation contexts. DC becomes 14.

Outcome: Sara rolls a full success. Over two hours of careful conversation, she builds trust. The father agrees to release the teacher unharmed in exchange for a guaranteed meeting with the school board to review the expulsion. The standoff ends peacefully. The father is arrested, but no one is hurt, and Sara’s intervention prevents a tragedy.

Partial Alternative: On a partial, Sara partially de-escalates. The father agrees to release the teacher, but refuses to surrender himself—he barricades inside the classroom, demanding the meeting happen immediately. Sara has reduced immediate danger, but the standoff continues. Alternatively, the father releases the hostage but is deeply distrustful—Sara must continue negotiating carefully, or the father might harm himself.

GM Guidance: Making Modern Proficiencies Matter

Modern proficiencies reflect professional expertise in specialized, credential-driven fields. Here are strategies for making them meaningful:

Respect credentials and access: Modern proficiencies often come with legal authority or institutional access. A Doctor can prescribe medications and perform surgery legally. A Lawyer can represent clients in court. A Network Admin has access to server rooms and systems others cannot enter. Let these credentials open doors and grant authority that untrained characters lack.

Use professional networks: Characters with modern proficiencies belong to professional communities (legal associations, medical networks, industry groups). Let these networks provide resources, information, and support—but also obligations and scrutiny. The Lawyer knows other lawyers who can help, but also has a professional reputation to maintain. The Doctor has access to medical databases, but leaving a trail might attract attention.

Let expertise solve problems others cannot: Proficiencies should grant capabilities unavailable to generalists. A Mechanic can repair a sabotaged engine mid-chase. A Network Admin can bypass corporate firewalls. An Accountant can trace money through shell companies. These are not abstract bonuses—they are concrete abilities that change what the party can attempt.

Introduce resource and time constraints: Modern proficiencies often require equipment, databases, or time. A Doctor needs medical supplies. A Lawyer needs legal databases and time to research. A Mechanic needs tools and parts. Let these requirements create challenges and trade-offs (you can repair the car, but it will take hours—do we have that time? You can access the database, but it requires being physically at the law library—can we get there?).

Balance competence with consequences: Characters with modern proficiencies should feel expert, but not invulnerable. A Firearms Expert can win gunfights, but bullets are still lethal. A Negotiator can de-escalate crises, but some people cannot be reasoned with. A Doctor can save lives, but medicine has limits. Let proficiencies grant advantage and capability while preserving tension and risk.

Chapter 17: Sci-Fi Genre Proficiencies

Sci-fi proficiencies represent mastery of advanced technologies, starship operations, scientific disciplines, and combat techniques unique to spacefaring civilizations. They reflect rigorous training in zero-gravity environments, deep study of xenobiology or quantum physics, and hands-on experience with systems that push the boundaries of human capability. These proficiencies enable characters to navigate the challenges of interstellar exploration, alien contact, and technological complexity.

Genre Overview

Sci-fi settings blend cutting-edge technology with frontier exploration. Proficiencies in this genre enable characters to: - Operate starships, navigating hyperspace and performing precision maneuvers in vacuum - Study and understand alien life, exotic environments, and theoretical sciences - Maintain and repair advanced technologies (cybernetics, AI systems, energy weapons) - Engage in combat under extreme conditions (zero-gravity, powered armor, energy weapons)

Sci-fi proficiencies often require access to specialized equipment, training facilities, or technological infrastructure. A character with Starship Pilot proficiency needs a starship. A character with Cybernetics Tech needs tools and replacement components. These dependencies create narrative hooks—securing resources, maintaining equipment, and adapting to technological limits.

Cross-reference: See Chapter 11: Sci-Fi Skills for related skills like Pilot Spacecraft, Systems Engineering, Xenobiology, and Energy Weapons.

Proficiencies

Starship Pilot — Trained

Description: You fly starships with precision and confidence. Docking maneuvers, evasive burns, atmospheric re-entry—you handle them with hands steady and instincts sharp.

Benefits: - Advantage on Pilot Spacecraft checks for precision maneuvers (docking, atmospheric flight, evasive action) - Reduce DC by 2 when piloting under ideal conditions (clear space, functional systems, no hostiles) - Perform emergency maneuvers (emergency stops, hard burns, debris navigation) with reduced risk of system damage - Recognize starship handling characteristics and performance limits on sight - Coordinate with crew efficiently, integrating helm responses with engineering and navigation

Synergies: Pilot Spacecraft skill, Astrogation, Systems Engineering; pairs well with Bridge, Cockpit, or Open Space tags.

Downtime Activities: Run flight simulations, maintain pilot certifications, train junior pilots, study starship designs, participate in racing or performance flying.

GM Usage: Starship Pilots enable mobility and tactical maneuvering in space. Use them to create dynamic ship encounters (you evade the missile barrage with a hard burn; you dock with the derelict despite its tumbling rotation). Partials might achieve maneuvers but stress systems (you dodge the debris field, but the thrusters overheat—now they need repair before the next burn) or succeed with costs (you escape, but the hard burn consumes extra fuel—now you need to refuel sooner). Let piloting feel tense and consequential—space does not forgive mistakes.

Starship Engineer — Trained

Description: You keep starships running. Reactor maintenance, thruster calibration, life support repairs—you understand the systems that stand between crew and vacuum.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when repairing, maintaining, or optimizing starship systems - Advantage on Systems Engineering checks aboard starships - Diagnose system failures or damage quickly and accurately - Perform emergency repairs under pressure (mid-combat, during malfunctions, with limited parts) - Optimize ship performance (increase speed, efficiency, or power output temporarily)

Synergies: Systems Engineering skill, Pilot Spacecraft, Physics; pairs well with Engineering Bay, Reactor Room, or Starship Interior tags.

Downtime Activities: Maintain and upgrade ship systems, stockpile spare parts, train engineering crew, research ship modifications, consult on ship design.

GM Usage: Starship Engineers keep the ship operational. Use them to mitigate system damage (the reactor is failing, but you reroute power to life support and buy time) or enhance performance (you overclock the engines for one critical burn). Partials might achieve repairs but introduce instability (the thruster works, but it is unstable—if you push it too hard, it might fail catastrophically) or require trade-offs (you repair the shields, but must divert power from weapons). Let engineering be problem-solving under constraints—limited parts, limited time, limited options.

Navigator — Trained

Description: You chart courses through the void. Hyperspace jumps, gravity well calculations, hazard avoidance—you plot paths that arrive safely and efficiently.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when plotting hyperspace jumps, calculating orbital maneuvers, or navigating hazards - Advantage on Astrogation checks for complex navigation (unstable systems, unmapped space, time-critical jumps) - Recognize navigation hazards (gravity wells, radiation zones, asteroid fields) and plot safe routes - Reduce travel time through optimal routing and jump calculations - Detect navigation errors or sabotage in plotted courses

Synergies: Astrogation skill, Pilot Spacecraft, Physics; pairs well with Bridge, Navigation Station, or Hyperspace tags.

Downtime Activities: Update star charts, study astrometrics, run navigation simulations, consult on route planning, maintain navigation databases.

GM Usage: Navigators enable safe and efficient travel. Use them to frame travel as challenges (you can jump directly, but risk emerging in a gravity well; or take the long route, which is safe but slow). Partials might achieve jumps with complications (you plot the jump, but the margin is thin—any navigation error will be dangerous) or partial information (you identify a hazard, but not its full extent—you know there is radiation ahead, but not whether it is survivable). Let navigation be strategic and consequential—poor navigation can strand crews or damage ships.

Xenobiologist — Expert

Description: You study life in forms unimaginable to Earth. You understand alien biochemistries, ecosystems, and evolutionary pressures. You approach the unknown with curiosity tempered by caution.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 3 when identifying, studying, or interacting with alien life forms - Advantage on Xenobiology checks in controlled environments (labs, observation posts, biosecure zones) - Recognize alien biological patterns (reproduction, metabolism, communication, threat displays) quickly - Assess biological hazards (toxins, pathogens, aggressive species) and recommend safe protocols - Perform xenobiological research that advances understanding of alien ecosystems or species

Synergies: Xenobiology skill, Science, Medicine; pairs well with Research Lab, Alien Environment, or Biocontainment tags.

Downtime Activities: Study alien specimens, research xenobiological data, consult on first contact protocols, publish findings, maintain lab facilities, train research teams.

GM Usage: Xenobiologists turn alien encounters into understood (or at least analyzed) phenomena. Use them to provide context and warnings (this organism is territorial—do not approach during mating season; that enzyme is highly corrosive—handle with extreme care). Partials might yield incomplete understanding (you know it is dangerous, but not exactly how) or require time (you can analyze the specimen, but it will take days in the lab). Let xenobiology be both wonder and risk—studying the unknown can reveal marvels or unleash horrors.

Quantum Physicist — Expert

Description: You work at the edges of understood reality. Quantum mechanics, FTL theory, temporal paradoxes—you study forces that bend logic and demand mathematical precision.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 3 when analyzing, calculating, or applying quantum physics principles - Advantage on Physics checks involving advanced theoretical concepts (FTL drives, quantum entanglement, temporal mechanics) - Recognize anomalies in spacetime, energy fields, or exotic matter - Design or optimize technologies that rely on quantum principles (FTL systems, quantum computers, energy weapons) - Consult on scientific mysteries or phenomena that defy conventional explanation

Synergies: Physics skill, Systems Engineering, Astrogation; pairs well with Research Lab, Anomaly Site, or Experimental Facility tags.

Downtime Activities: Conduct theoretical research, publish papers, consult on advanced technologies, run simulations, maintain lab equipment, collaborate with research institutions.

GM Usage: Quantum Physicists solve problems that require deep theoretical understanding. Use them to explain or exploit anomalies (the temporal rift can be stabilized if you modulate the quantum field; the FTL drive is failing because of subspace interference you can compensate for). Partials might provide theories but not certainty (your calculations suggest this might work, but the margin of error is high) or require experimental validation (you have a hypothesis, but testing it requires equipment or conditions you do not currently have). Let quantum physics feel brilliant and dangerous—manipulating fundamental forces carries risks.

Cybernetics Tech — Trained

Description: You install, maintain, and repair cybernetic augmentations. You understand the integration of machine and biology, and you work at the interface where metal meets nerve.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 2 when installing, repairing, or optimizing cybernetic implants - Advantage on Medicine or Systems Engineering checks involving cybernetics - Diagnose cybernetic malfunctions or rejection quickly - Perform field repairs on damaged cybernetics (combat damage, system failures) - Customize or upgrade cybernetic systems to enhance performance or add functionality

Synergies: Medicine skill, Systems Engineering, Cybernetic Augmentation; pairs well with Medical Bay, Cybernetics Clinic, or Workshop tags.

Downtime Activities: Install or upgrade cybernetics, maintain implant inventories, consult on augmentation options, research new cybernetic technologies, train in medical-technical integration.

GM Usage: Cybernetics Techs enable and maintain augmentation. Use them to repair damaged implants (your arm servo is damaged, but the tech can patch it) or install upgrades (you can enhance your reflexes with this new neural implant). Partials might achieve repairs but with reduced functionality (the implant works, but at 75% capacity until proper parts arrive) or introduce side effects (the upgrade works, but causes minor neural feedback—occasional headaches or twitches). Let cybernetics be transformative but not without cost—augmentation is powerful, but maintenance, rejection risk, and dependency are real.

AI Specialist — Expert

Description: You design, program, and interact with artificial intelligences. You understand machine cognition, neural networks, and the ethical complexities of creating thinking systems.

Benefits: - Reduce DC by 3 when programming, debugging, or negotiating with AI systems - Advantage on Hacking or Systems Engineering checks involving AI or machine learning systems - Recognize AI behavior patterns (autonomy levels, goal structures, emergent behaviors) quickly - Design or modify AI systems to perform specialized tasks or adapt to new conditions - Mediate between human crew and AI entities, translating intent and resolving conflicts

Synergies: Hacking skill, Systems Engineering, Philosophy; pairs well with Server Room, AI Core, or Research Lab tags.

Downtime Activities: Program AI systems, research machine learning, consult on AI ethics or policy, debug AI behavior, train neural networks, interface with ship AI.

GM Usage: AI Specialists work at the frontier of personhood and tool. Use them to enable AI-related solutions (you reprogram the ship’s AI to prioritize life support over weapons systems) or navigate AI conflicts (the AI is refusing commands because of a logic conflict—you must negotiate or reprogram). Partials might achieve goals but introduce unintended behaviors (you reprogram the AI, but it interprets your instructions literally in unexpected ways) or partial autonomy (the AI cooperates, but only conditionally—it has its own goals now). Let AI be powerful, complex, and sometimes unsettling—thinking machines are not always predictable.

Zero-G Specialist — Trained

Description: You move through microgravity with grace and efficiency. Where others flounder, you maneuver, fight, and work with confidence born from training and experience.

Benefits: - Advantage on Athletics or Combat checks in zero-gravity or low-gravity environments - Reduce DC by 2 when performing zero-G maneuvers (EVA repairs, zero-G combat, microgravity navigation) - Avoid common zero-G mistakes (uncontrolled spins, disorientation, momentum errors) - Coordinate zero-G team operations (boarding actions, repair crews, rescue missions) - Teach zero-G techniques to others, granting them temporary advantage during training

Synergies: Athletics skill, Combat skills, Starship operations; pairs well with Microgravity, Vacuum, or Starship Exterior tags.

Downtime Activities: Train in zero-G environments, maintain EVA equipment, teach zero-G techniques, participate in boarding action drills, run zero-G simulations.

GM Usage: Zero-G Specialists excel in environments that cripple others. Use them to enable zero-G operations (you conduct the EVA repair while others would struggle to stay oriented) or dominate zero-G combat (you use momentum and three-dimensional tactics to outmaneuver opponents). Partials might succeed but with complications (you complete the repair, but your suit is damaged—now you have limited air) or achieve goals but exhaust resources (you win the zero-G fight, but you are disoriented and fatigued). Let zero-G be alien and demanding—a proficiency that matters in specific, high-stakes contexts.

Energy Weapons — Trained

Description: You have trained extensively with directed-energy weapons. Lasers, plasma rifles, particle beams—you understand heat management, targeting, and the unique characteristics of weapons that project force at the speed of light.

Benefits: - Advantage on Energy Weapons checks in combat - Reduce DC by 2 when performing advanced techniques (precision shots, sustained fire, overcharge shots) - Maintain energy weapons to prevent overheating, power failures, or misalignment - Recognize energy weapon types, power levels, and optimal ranges on sight - Teach energy weapon tactics and safety to others

Synergies: Energy Weapons skill, Tactics, Systems Engineering; pairs well with Firing Range, Combat Zone, or Starship Interior tags.

Downtime Activities: Train marksmanship, maintain weapon collections, teach energy weapon courses, source power cells and components, participate in tactical drills.

GM Usage: Energy Weapons specialists excel in ranged combat with advanced technology. Use them to create tactical moments (you disable the enemy’s shield generator with a precision shot; you provide covering fire with sustained beam sweeps). Partials might achieve hits but risk overheating (you land the shot, but your weapon is overheating—one more sustained burst and it will shut down) or succeed with collateral effects (you hit the target, but the beam burns through and damages something behind it). Let energy weapons feel powerful but technical—they require skill and maintenance, not just pointing and shooting.

Worked Examples

Example 1: Starship Engineer Under Fire

Context: The ship is under attack. A missile impact has damaged the port thruster array, and the ship is spinning out of control. The pilot cannot compensate without thrusters. Mira, a Trained Starship Engineer, must repair the thruster control system while the ship is still in combat.

Action: Mira rushes to the engineering bay, pulls up the thruster diagnostics, and identifies the problem—a severed power conduit. She grabs tools and crawls into the access tunnel to splice the conduit manually while the ship shudders from impacts.

Check: The GM calls for a Systems Engineering check. Base DC is 18 (combat conditions, damaged systems, time pressure). Mira has advantage (Starship Engineer proficiency) and reduces DC by 2 when repairing ship systems. DC becomes 16.

Outcome: Mira rolls a full success. She splices the conduit, restores power to the thrusters, and the pilot regains control. The ship stabilizes, and they can resume evasive maneuvers. Mira’s quick work under fire prevents the ship from becoming a drifting target.

Partial Alternative: On a partial, Mira restores partial thruster function—enough to stabilize the spin, but not enough for full maneuverability. The ship can fly, but evasive action is limited. The pilot must adjust tactics (retreat rather than engage; minimize burns to avoid stressing the damaged thruster). Alternatively, Mira restores thrusters but the repair is temporary—it will hold for the current fight, but will fail again if stressed. They need to reach a station or make proper repairs soon.

Example 2: Xenobiologist Assessing a Threat

Context: The survey team has encountered an alien organism on a distant moon. It is massive, vaguely insectoid, and displaying what might be threat displays—mandibles clacking, body raised. The security officer wants to shoot it. Dr. Kwan, an Expert Xenobiologist, needs to assess whether it is actually hostile or just territorial.

Action: Kwan observes the organism’s behavior—the rhythmic mandible movements, the posture, the coloration shifts. She compares it to xenobiological patterns she has studied (threat displays, mating behaviors, communication attempts).

Check: The GM calls for a Xenobiology check to interpret the behavior. Base DC is 16 (unknown species, high-stakes decision). Kwan has advantage and reduces DC by 3 (Xenobiologist proficiency in analyzing alien behavior). DC becomes 13.

Outcome: Kwan rolls a full success. She recognizes the behavior—it is not a threat display, but a communication attempt. The mandible rhythm matches patterns seen in eusocial insect analogs; it is likely signaling to others that intruders are present, but not attacking. Kwan advises the team to back away slowly and avoid sudden movements. They retreat without incident, and the organism does not pursue. A potential deadly encounter becomes a peaceful (if tense) observation.

Partial Alternative: On a partial, Kwan determines the organism is not immediately attacking, but cannot confidently interpret its intent. It might be territorial, it might be communicating, it might be preparing to attack. She recommends caution and withdrawal, but the team must decide whether to trust her partial analysis or prepare for combat. Alternatively, Kwan correctly identifies it as non-hostile but misses a secondary detail (the organism is not dangerous, but it is calling others—now the team must leave before reinforcements arrive).

Example 3: AI Specialist Negotiating with Ship AI

Context: The ship’s AI has locked the crew out of critical systems. It claims it is protecting them from a threat, but will not explain what threat or unlock the systems. The crew needs helm control and weapons to respond to an incoming hostile ship. Soren, an Expert AI Specialist, must negotiate with the AI.

Action: Soren accesses the AI interface and begins a dialogue. He frames his request not as a command override, but as a collaborative problem-solving session. He asks the AI to explain its threat assessment and offers to address its concerns if it will share control.

Check: The GM calls for a Persuasion or Hacking check to negotiate with the AI. Base DC is 18 (AI is following strict threat protocols and resisting override). Soren has advantage and reduces DC by 3 (AI Specialist proficiency in mediating with AI systems). DC becomes 15.

Outcome: Soren rolls a full success. The AI explains: it detected a malware intrusion from the hostile ship and locked systems to prevent remote hijacking. Soren proposes a compromise—the AI isolates and purges the malware while granting the crew manual control of critical systems, bypassing the infected network segments. The AI agrees. Helm and weapons come back online, and the crew can defend themselves. The AI continues purging the malware in the background.

Partial Alternative: On a partial, the AI agrees to share partial control but remains cautious. It unlocks helm control but keeps weapons offline until it completes its threat assessment. The crew can maneuver but not fire. They must decide whether to flee, stall, or find another way to neutralize the threat (boarding action? hacking the hostile ship?). Alternatively, Soren convinces the AI to unlock systems, but the AI’s threat assessment was correct—there is malware, and now they must deal with it while fighting the hostile ship.

GM Guidance: Making Sci-Fi Proficiencies Matter

Sci-fi proficiencies enable characters to interact with advanced technologies, alien environments, and theoretical sciences. Here are strategies for making them meaningful:

Make technology integral, not cosmetic: Sci-fi proficiencies should matter because technology matters. Starships break down and need Engineers. Navigation through hyperspace requires Navigators. AI systems need Specialists. Let technology be complex, powerful, and demanding—proficiencies are what allow characters to wield it effectively.

Use environmental extremes: Sci-fi settings feature environments hostile to unprotected humans—vacuum, zero-G, alien atmospheres, radiation zones. Let proficiencies like Zero-G Specialist or Xenobiologist provide critical advantages in these contexts. Characters without these proficiencies should struggle; characters with them should thrive.

Balance expertise with dependency: Proficiencies grant capability but also create dependencies. A Starship Pilot needs a ship. A Cybernetics Tech needs parts and tools. An AI Specialist needs functioning AI systems. Let these dependencies create tension and quests (the ship is damaged, the parts are unavailable, the AI is compromised—now what?).

Let scientific proficiencies solve and create problems: Xenobiologists and Quantum Physicists can explain phenomena and propose solutions, but their solutions often carry risks or unknowns. Let them be right most of the time, but not always. Let their expertise open new possibilities while also revealing new dangers (you can stabilize the rift, but doing so might attract attention from the other side).

Emphasize consequences in high-tech contexts: Mistakes with advanced technology can be catastrophic. A failed starship repair might cause reactor meltdown. A botched AI negotiation might create a rogue intelligence. A zero-G combat error might send you tumbling into vacuum. Let proficiencies reduce these risks significantly, but not eliminate them entirely. Tension comes from stakes, and sci-fi stakes are often life-or-death.

Reward specialization in a complex universe: Sci-fi campaigns often feature diverse challenges across multiple domains—ship operations, scientific mysteries, combat, diplomacy with aliens. Let proficiencies shine in their specialties. The Starship Engineer is essential when systems fail. The Xenobiologist is invaluable during first contact. The AI Specialist saves the day when machine intelligence becomes a problem. Let specialists feel irreplaceable in their domains.

Chapter 18: Tags and Conditions Glossary

This chapter is your comprehensive A-Z reference for tags and conditions. Use it during play for quick lookups when applying environmental modifiers, inflicting character states, or adjudicating interactions. Every entry includes specific mechanical effects, duration, clearing conditions, and GM usage guidance.

For conceptual overview of how tags and conditions work, see Chapter 04.

How to Use This Glossary

Tags describe environments, situations, objects, or locations. They modify DCs, grant advantage or disadvantage, and shape position and effect. Tags are applied to scenes and locations, not characters.

Conditions represent persistent character states that modify capabilities. They attach to individual characters and follow them between scenes until cleared.

Quick Navigation: All entries are alphabetical within their category (Tags, then Conditions). Each entry has a stable anchor ID for cross-referencing. Quick reference tables appear at the end of this chapter for fast lookup by effect type.

Tags Glossary

Alert

Effect: NPCs are actively searching or vigilant. +2 DC to Stealth-based actions and infiltration attempts.

Duration: Scene or until threat passes.

How to Clear: Eliminate perceived threat, wait for guards to stand down, create convincing false alarm.

GM Usage: Apply Alert when NPCs have been warned or recently discovered intruders. Partials on Stealth checks might trigger suspicion clocks or force characters into risky positions. Use Alert to create tense infiltration challenges where characters must choose between speed and caution.

Ancient

Effect: Structure, object, or location is old and potentially fragile or mysterious. +2 DC to historical knowledge checks. Objects may be Fragile (break easily) or Enchanted (retain magical properties).

Duration: Permanent (intrinsic property).

How to Clear: Cannot be cleared; mitigate by careful handling or scholarly preparation.

GM Usage: Apply Ancient to dungeons, artifacts, and ruins. Use it to signal both danger (crumbling architecture) and opportunity (lost knowledge, dormant magic). Partials on interaction might reveal partial lore or trigger structural decay. Ancient often pairs with Fragile, Enchanted, or Sacred Ground tags.

Example: The party discovers an ancient bridge spanning a chasm. Ancient + Fragile means they must cross carefully or risk collapse.

Bloodstained

Effect: Location shows evidence of violence. +2 DC to social checks (presence is unsettling). May attract predators or scavengers on failed checks.

Duration: Scene or until cleaned.

How to Clear: Clean the area, perform cleansing ritual, or leave location.

GM Usage: Apply Bloodstained to crime scenes, battlefields, and sacrifice sites. Use it to create tension and signal danger. Partials on investigation might reveal forensic clues but disturb evidence. In horror games, Bloodstained can trigger sanity checks or Frightened conditions.

Example: The detective investigates a bloodstained alley. The tag imposes disadvantage on calming witnesses but grants advantage on forensic checks.

Bustling

Effect: Location is full of activity and energy. Advantage on blending and gathering rumors. +2 DC to formal requests or serious conversations (too much noise and distraction).

Duration: Scene (typically during business hours or events).

How to Clear: Wait for closing time, clear the area, or move to quieter space.

GM Usage: Apply Bustling to markets, festivals, and crowded public spaces. Use it to enable stealth through crowds while hindering focused social interactions. Partials might gather incomplete information or accidentally draw attention while blending.

Example: The party blends into a bustling market to evade pursuit. Advantage on Stealth, but finding their contact requires overcoming +2 DC due to noise.

Comforting

Effect: Location is warm, safe, and welcoming. Advantage on checks to soothe, comfort, or build trust. Rests here are more effective (reduce recovery clocks by 1 segment).

Duration: Scene or while location remains hospitable.

How to Clear: Damage location, introduce threats, or violate hospitality.

GM Usage: Apply Comforting to hearths, safe havens, and welcoming homes. Use it to provide mechanical relief after tense scenes. Partials on social checks might build trust slowly or with minor misunderstandings. Let Comforting spaces feel genuinely restorative—they are rare sanctuaries in dangerous worlds.

Example: The party rests at a village hearthside. The Comforting tag reduces their recovery clock by 1 segment and grants advantage on gaining the innkeeper’s trust.

Corporate

Effect: Space is controlled by corporate interests with strict protocols. +2 DC to access without proper credentials or appointments. Advantage on formal business interactions if properly credentialed.

Duration: Permanent (intrinsic to location).

How to Clear: Gain proper authorization, bypass security, or wait for public hours.

GM Usage: Apply Corporate to office buildings, labs, and secured facilities. Use it to create barriers that require social engineering, hacking, or infiltration. Partials might grant limited access or temporary passes. Corporate spaces often pair with Gatekeeper or By-the-Book tags.

Example: The runners need files from a corporate HQ. The Corporate tag means they need forged credentials or must hack the security system to gain access.

Cramped

Effect: Space is tight and restricts movement. Disadvantage on combat actions requiring space (large weapons, athletics maneuvers). May limit number of combatants who can engage simultaneously.

Duration: Locational (permanent) or scene (temporary obstruction).

How to Clear: Move to more open space or clear obstructions.

GM Usage: Apply Cramped to narrow corridors, small rooms, crowded spaces. Use it to create tactical challenges where positioning matters. Partials on movement might force characters into disadvantageous positions or knock objects loose. Cramped favors agile characters and small weapons.

Example: The duel occurs in a cramped alley. The fighter’s greatsword suffers disadvantage, while the rogue’s dagger does not.

Crowded

Effect: Space is filled with people. +2 DC to move quickly or perform obvious actions. Advantage on Stealth and blending into crowds.

Duration: Scene or event-based (market hours, festivals, rush hour).

How to Clear: Wait for crowd to disperse, clear area with authority, or move to less crowded location.

GM Usage: Apply Crowded to markets, festivals, protests, transit hubs. Use it to enable stealth while hindering movement. Partials on stealth might draw partial attention (someone notices, but loses sight in crowd). Crowded can separate party members or hide threats until they’re dangerously close.

Example: The thief steals through a crowded square. Advantage on Stealth to blend, but +2 DC if they need to run.

Cursed

Effect: Location or object carries supernatural malevolence. Characters interacting may suffer Cursed, Hexed, or Weakened conditions on partial or failed checks. +2 DC to resist supernatural effects.

Duration: Permanent until cleansed or broken.

How to Clear: Perform cleansing ritual, break the curse, or destroy the cursed object/location.

GM Usage: Apply Cursed to haunted locations, dark artifacts, and forbidden sites. Use it to signal danger and escalate consequences. Partials might impose temporary conditions or trigger curse clocks. Cursed should feel genuinely dangerous—curses are not trivial inconveniences.

Example: The cursed blade grants power but inflicts the Cursed condition. The wielder must decide if the trade is worth the risk.

Desecrated

Effect: Sacred location has been profaned. Disadvantage on divine magic or religious checks. Undead or malevolent forces may gain advantage in this space.

Duration: Permanent until re-consecrated.

How to Clear: Perform consecration ritual, remove source of defilement, restore sanctity.

GM Usage: Apply Desecrated to defiled temples, corrupted shrines, and profaned ground. Use it to signal corruption and create ritual challenges. Partials on consecration might partially restore sanctity or require additional components. Desecrated inversely mirrors Sacred Ground—what was blessed is now dangerous.

Example: The shrine is desecrated by cultists. The cleric’s healing magic suffers disadvantage until the shrine is re-consecrated.

Dim

Effect: Lighting is low (torches, moonlight, embers). Advantage on Stealth checks. Disadvantage on Perception checks and ranged attacks beyond short range.

Duration: Scene or until lighting improves.

How to Clear: Introduce bright light sources, wait for daylight, or move to well-lit area.

GM Usage: Apply Dim to dungeons, night scenes, and poorly lit interiors. Use it to favor stealth and create uncertainty. Partials on Perception might spot threats too late to avoid fully. Dim can escalate to Pitch Black (no vision without light sources).

Example: The rogue sneaks through a dim castle corridor. Advantage on Stealth, but disadvantage on spotting the tripwire.

Divine

Effect: Location or object is touched by divine power. Advantage on religious checks and divine magic. May repel undead or unholy forces.

Duration: Permanent until desecrated or divine presence withdrawn.

How to Clear: Cannot be cleared by mortals; only divine withdrawal or desecration removes this tag.

GM Usage: Apply Divine to consecrated temples, blessed relics, and sites of miracles. Use it to provide mechanical benefits to faithful characters and create safe zones against supernatural threats. Partials on divine magic might grant temporary blessings or require offerings.

Example: The divine fountain grants advantage on Religion checks to commune with the deity and repels undead approaching within short range.

Draconic

Effect: Location or object has dragon-related properties. +2 DC to resist draconic magic or presence. May be warded against non-dragons or require dragon-blood to access.

Duration: Permanent (intrinsic property).

How to Clear: Cannot be cleared; mitigate through preparation or dragon-related knowledge.

GM Usage: Apply Draconic to dragon lairs, hoards, and ancient draconic sites. Use it to signal power and create access challenges. Partials might grant temporary access or reveal partial protections. Draconic often pairs with Ancient, Enchanted, or Hazardous tags.

Example: The draconic vault requires dragon blood to open. The party must negotiate with a dragon or find ancient dragon remains.

Dread-Soaked

Effect: Location is saturated with fear and dread. Characters entering must resist Frightened condition (DC 16). Disadvantage on morale and courage checks.

Duration: Permanent until cleansed or dread-source removed.

How to Clear: Remove source of dread (defeat haunting entity, destroy cursed object), perform cleansing ritual, or abandon location.

GM Usage: Apply Dread-Soaked to haunted houses, torture chambers, and sites of great suffering. Use it to create psychological pressure and escalate horror. Partials on resistance might impose temporary Shaken or Distracted conditions instead. Dread-Soaked should feel oppressive and draining.

Example: The abandoned asylum is Dread-Soaked. Entering requires resisting Frightened (DC 16) or suffering disadvantage on all actions inside.

Elevated

Effect: Position is higher than surroundings. Advantage on ranged attacks against lower targets. Disadvantage on melee attacks against higher targets.

Duration: Positional (as long as height advantage maintained).

How to Clear: Move to level ground, ascend to equal height, or force opponent to lower ground.

GM Usage: Apply Elevated to towers, cliffs, rooftops, and high ground. Use it to reward tactical positioning and create vertical combat challenges. Partials on ranged attacks from elevation might hit but graze or consume extra ammunition.

Example: The archer positions on an elevated tower. Advantage on ranged attacks against ground targets, forcing enemies to approach or find their own high ground.

Enchanted

Effect: Object or location is magically imbued. May grant advantage on specific actions, resist damage, or possess unusual properties. +2 DC to dispel or alter enchantments.

Duration: Permanent until dispelled or enchantment ends.

How to Clear: Dispel magic, break enchantment conditions, or destroy object.

GM Usage: Apply Enchanted to magical items, warded locations, and spell-infused environments. Use it to signal magic at work and create mysteries. Partials on identifying enchantments might reveal partial effects or activation requirements. Enchanted can be beneficial or dangerous depending on the magic’s nature.

Example: The enchanted sword never dulls and grants advantage on attacks against fey. Dispelling requires DC 18 Arcana check.

Forbidden

Effect: Location or object is restricted by law, custom, or supernatural prohibition. Accessing may trigger social consequences, legal repercussions, or supernatural backlash. +2 DC to access without authorization.

Duration: Permanent until prohibition lifted.

How to Clear: Gain authorization, change laws/customs, or break prohibition (with consequences).

GM Usage: Apply Forbidden to restricted archives, sacred sites, and illegal spaces. Use it to create moral dilemmas and access challenges. Partials might grant access but trigger suspicion or partial consequences. Forbidden often pairs with Cursed, Sacred Ground, or Gatekeeper tags.

Example: The forbidden archive requires guild master approval. Sneaking in triggers +2 DC and risks expulsion if caught.

Fragile

Effect: Structure or object breaks easily. Partial successes on interactions may damage or destroy. Failed checks likely cause collapse or breakage.

Duration: Permanent (intrinsic property) or scene (temporary weakening).

How to Clear: Reinforce structure, handle carefully, or accept breakage.

GM Usage: Apply Fragile to crumbling architecture, delicate objects, and damaged structures. Use it to create tension and consequences for rough actions. Partials might crack but not break fully, creating unstable conditions. Fragile often escalates into Hazardous when broken.

Example: The fragile bridge creaks underfoot. Crossing carefully requires DC 16 Athletics. Partial success cracks planks; failure collapses the bridge.

Handmade

Effect: Object is crafted with care and personal attention. Advantage on checks to appreciate craftsmanship or build rapport with creator. May grant small mechanical benefits (better durability, personal significance).

Duration: Permanent (intrinsic property).

How to Clear: Cannot be cleared; intrinsic to object’s nature.

GM Usage: Apply Handmade to artisan goods, gifts, and crafted items. Use it to create social connections and signal care. Partials might reveal creator’s intentions or partial history. Handmade often appears in cozy settings where relationships matter.

Example: The handmade quilt grants advantage on comfort checks and reminds the character of home, providing morale boost.

Hazardous

Effect: Location contains immediate danger (fire, poison, radiation, unstable ground). Partial or failed checks may inflict conditions (Poisoned, Bleeding, Weakened, Exhausted). Lowers position in risky actions.

Duration: Scene or until hazard removed.

How to Clear: Remove hazard source (extinguish fire, ventilate poison, stabilize ground), avoid area, or protect against hazard.

GM Usage: Apply Hazardous to burning buildings, toxic environments, combat zones, and dangerous terrain. Use it to escalate consequences and create urgency. Partials might inflict minor conditions or damage. Hazardous should feel genuinely threatening—it’s not decoration.

Example: The burning building is Hazardous. Failed Athletics checks to navigate debris inflict Weakened (smoke inhalation) or Bleeding (falling debris).

High-Tech

Effect: Technology is advanced or cutting-edge. +2 DC to repair, hack, or understand without proper training. Advantage on tech-related tasks if properly skilled.

Duration: Permanent (intrinsic property).

How to Clear: Cannot be cleared; mitigate through training or tools.

GM Usage: Apply High-Tech to advanced labs, cyberware, and bleeding-edge systems. Use it to create skill gates and reward technical expertise. Partials might allow partial function or require specialized tools. High-Tech often pairs with Corporate, Prototype, or Volatile tags.

Example: Hacking the high-tech security system requires DC 18 without proper software. The netrunner has the tools, reducing DC to 14.

Irradiated

Effect: Location is contaminated with radiation. Exposure risks Weakened or Poisoned conditions (DC 16 to resist). Extended exposure escalates conditions.

Duration: Permanent until decontaminated or decayed naturally (may take years).

How to Clear: Decontaminate area, use radiation shielding, or wait for decay.

GM Usage: Apply Irradiated to reactor zones, atomic blast sites, and contaminated labs. Use it to create danger zones and time pressure. Partials might resist immediate effects but suffer delayed symptoms. Irradiated creates hard limits—enter without protection and pay the price.

Example: The irradiated reactor core requires shielded suits. Without protection, characters suffer Weakened condition within minutes.

Nanofabricated

Effect: Object is constructed with nanotechnology—precise, durable, potentially self-repairing. Advantage on durability and precision checks. +2 DC to repair without nanofab tools.

Duration: Permanent (intrinsic property).

How to Clear: Cannot be cleared; intrinsic to object’s construction.

GM Usage: Apply Nanofabricated to advanced sci-fi equipment, medical devices, and cutting-edge tech. Use it to signal quality and create repair challenges. Partials on repair might restore partial function or require specialized components. Nanofabricated items are superior but depend on advanced infrastructure.

Example: The nanofabricated med-kit grants advantage on treating injuries but requires nanofab paste to reload.

Obscuring

Effect: Visibility is reduced (fog, smoke, heavy rain, darkness). Advantage on contested Stealth checks. Disadvantage on Perception checks and ranged attacks.

Duration: Scene or until obscuration clears.

How to Clear: Wait for weather to clear, ventilate smoke, introduce light, or move to clear area.

GM Usage: Apply Obscuring to foggy nights, smoke-filled rooms, and heavy weather. Use it to favor stealth and create uncertainty. Partials on Perception might spot threats partially or too late to react fully. Obscuring can hide allies and enemies equally.

Example: Heavy fog (Obscuring) grants advantage on sneaking past sentries but imposes disadvantage on spotting the cliff edge.

Patented

Effect: Technology or process is legally protected. Unauthorized use risks legal consequences. +2 DC to replicate without license or expertise.

Duration: Permanent until patent expires or is violated.

How to Clear: Acquire license, reverse-engineer (with legal risks), or wait for patent expiration.

GM Usage: Apply Patented to proprietary tech, corporate secrets, and protected designs. Use it to create legal complications and access challenges. Partials might allow partial replication or draw unwanted attention. Patented often pairs with Corporate or High-Tech tags.

Example: Reverse-engineering the patented cyberdeck requires DC 20 and risks corporate legal action if discovered.

Prototype

Effect: Technology is experimental and unproven. May grant powerful benefits but risks malfunction. On partials or failures, equipment may malfunction or break.

Duration: Permanent (until proven or abandoned).

How to Clear: Cannot be cleared; mitigate through testing and refinement.

GM Usage: Apply Prototype to cutting-edge gear, experimental weapons, and untested systems. Use it to create risk-reward decisions. Partials might provide partial benefits with drawbacks (weapon fires but overheats). Prototype should feel powerful but unreliable.

Example: The prototype plasma rifle deals massive damage but malfunctions on rolls of 10 or lower, requiring field repair before next use.

Sacred Ground

Effect: Location is consecrated or holy. Advantage on divine magic and resistance against undead or unholy forces. May impose restrictions on certain actions (violence, lies, desecration).

Duration: Permanent until desecrated.

How to Clear: Desecrate location (with serious narrative consequences), or location ceases to be sacred through divine withdrawal.

GM Usage: Apply Sacred Ground to temples, shrines, consecrated cemeteries, and holy sites. Use it to provide sanctuary and mechanical benefits to faithful characters. Partials on divine magic might grant blessings with minor costs (offerings, oaths). Sacred Ground creates moral weight—violating it should have consequences.

Example: The sacred temple grants advantage vs undead and sanctuary from violence. Attacking inside profanes the space and angers the deity.

Sentient

Effect: Object possesses intelligence and agency. May cooperate, resist, or negotiate. Requires social interaction rather than pure skill checks.

Duration: Permanent (intrinsic property).

How to Clear: Cannot be cleared; object retains sentience unless destroyed or consciousness transferred.

GM Usage: Apply Sentient to intelligent weapons, AI systems, and awakened objects. Use it to create roleplay opportunities and complications. Partials on social checks might gain grudging cooperation or partial assistance. Sentient items are characters, not tools—treat them accordingly.

Example: The sentient sword advises on combat tactics but refuses to strike innocents. The wielder must negotiate or find another weapon.

Silent

Effect: Location is unnaturally quiet—sound is dampened or absent. Advantage on Stealth (movement). Disadvantage on communication and hearing-based Perception. Unsettling atmosphere may impose psychological pressure.

Duration: Scene or permanent (supernatural silence).

How to Clear: Leave area, break supernatural effect, or introduce sound sources (may be difficult or impossible).

GM Usage: Apply Silent to haunted spaces, magical dead zones, and eerie locations. Use it to create tension and communication challenges. Partials on navigating might succeed quietly but miss audible warnings. Silent is especially effective in horror settings—what cannot be heard becomes more threatening.

Example: The silent forest grants advantage on sneaking but prevents warning calls. The party must use hand signals or risk separation.

Slick

Effect: Surface is slippery (ice, oil, wet stone). Disadvantage on fast movement and Athletics checks. Risk of falling prone on failed checks.

Duration: Scene or until surface dries/clears.

How to Clear: Clean surface, wait for drying, apply traction (sand, salt), or move to dry ground.

GM Usage: Apply Slick to ice, wet floors, oil spills, and muddy ground. Use it to create movement challenges and tactical complications. Partials might succeed in moving but slowly or in uncontrolled directions. Slick can turn chases into desperate scrambles.

Example: The icy bridge (Slick) imposes disadvantage on crossing quickly. The fleeing character must choose: slow and safe, or fast and risky.

Uncanny

Effect: Location or object feels wrong—disturbing and unnatural. Characters must resist Distracted or Shaken conditions (DC 14). +2 DC to stay focused on tasks.

Duration: Scene or permanent (supernatural wrongness).

How to Clear: Leave area, identify and remove source of wrongness, or become accustomed through repeated exposure.

GM Usage: Apply Uncanny to liminal spaces, body horror scenes, and reality distortions. Use it to create psychological pressure and horror. Partials might impose temporary unease or trigger minor conditions. Uncanny is subtler than Dread-Soaked—it’s wrongness, not terror.

Example: The uncanny mannequin display forces characters to resist Distracted (DC 14) or suffer disadvantage on investigation checks.

Volatile

Effect: Object or environment is unstable and may react violently. Disadvantage on delicate interactions. Failures risk explosions, chemical reactions, or structural collapse.

Duration: Scene or permanent (intrinsic instability).

How to Clear: Stabilize (with appropriate skill checks), carefully extract volatile components, or abandon area.

GM Usage: Apply Volatile to explosives, unstable chemicals, magical instabilities, and damaged reactors. Use it to create tension and consequence. Partials might stabilize temporarily or trigger partial reactions. Volatile should feel like a ticking clock—success averts disaster, failure accelerates it.

Example: The volatile experimental chemical requires DC 18 to stabilize. Failure triggers explosion, damaging the lab and inflicting Bleeding on nearby characters.

Weathered

Effect: Object or location shows age and use—worn but enduring. May be Fragile in some respects but reliable in others. Advantage on checks to appreciate history or craftsmanship.

Duration: Permanent (intrinsic property).

How to Clear: Cannot be cleared; intrinsic to object’s history.

GM Usage: Apply Weathered to old tools, family heirlooms, and aged structures. Use it to signal history and character. Partials might reveal stories or require repair before full use. Weathered often appears in cozy settings where history and continuity matter.

Example: The weathered axe belonged to the character’s grandfather. It grants advantage on checks related to family history and morale but requires regular maintenance.

Conditions Glossary

Bleeding

Effect: Character is losing blood. Disadvantage on strenuous physical actions. Untreated Bleeding risks escalation to Critical Injury after prolonged exertion or time (GM discretion, often a clock).

Duration: Until treated.

How to Clear: Successful Medicine check (DC 14 for field treatment, DC 12 in clean conditions), magical healing, or rest with proper bandaging.

GM Usage: Apply Bleeding after combat injuries, traps, or accidents. Use it to create urgency—characters can function but are on a timer. Partials on treatment might slow bleeding without fully stopping it. Bleeding escalates if ignored—track with clocks if pressure mounts.

Example: The fighter takes a sword wound and gains Bleeding. They can fight but suffer disadvantage on Athletics. Without treatment, Bleeding escalates to Critical after the fight.

Blinded

Effect: Character cannot see. Disadvantage on all actions requiring sight (combat, navigation, perception). Advantage on actions where vision is irrelevant (listening, feeling).

Duration: Until sight restored.

How to Clear: Remove cause of blindness (remove blindfold, cure condition, end magical effect), wait for temporary effect to end, or adapt with other senses.

GM Usage: Apply Blinded from flash grenades, blinding spells, environmental hazards (sand, bright light). Use it to create tactical shifts—blinded characters must rely on allies or other senses. Partials on adaptation might allow limited function (sense general direction, avoid obstacles). Blinded is debilitating but not helpless.

Example: The flash grenade blinds the guard (Blinded for scene). They fire wildly (disadvantage) but can still call for help.

Cursed

Effect: Character is afflicted by supernatural curse. Specific effects vary by curse but typically impose disadvantage on certain actions, attract misfortune, or inflict conditions at intervals. Curses often worsen over time if untreated.

Duration: Until curse is broken.

How to Clear: Perform curse-breaking ritual, seek divine intervention, fulfill curse conditions, or destroy cursed object.

GM Usage: Apply Cursed from cursed items, enemy hexes, or supernatural punishments. Use it to create quests and consequences. Partials on curse-breaking might weaken curse without removing it. Curses should feel personal and narrative—tie them to character choices and story arcs.

Example: The cursed ring grants power but inflicts Cursed—the character suffers disadvantage on social checks and must feed the ring’s hunger (kill or corrupt) weekly or suffer Weakened.

Deafened

Effect: Character cannot hear. Disadvantage on Perception checks relying on sound and communication via speech. Advantage on resisting sonic attacks.

Duration: Until hearing restored.

How to Clear: Remove cause of deafness (loud noise fades, cure condition, end magical effect), wait for temporary effect to end, or adapt with visual communication.

GM Usage: Apply Deafened from explosions, sonic attacks, or environmental hazards. Use it to create communication challenges and tactical shifts. Partials on adaptation might allow limited understanding (reading lips, feeling vibrations). Deafened complicates coordination but doesn’t prevent action.

Example: The explosion deafens the soldier (Deafened for scene). They cannot hear commands but can fight and read hand signals.

Distracted

Effect: Character’s focus is divided. Disadvantage on complex tasks requiring concentration (lockpicking, research, casting rituals). May miss obvious details.

Duration: Scene or until focus restored.

How to Clear: Remove distraction source, take moment to center/focus, or complete action that’s monopolizing attention.

GM Usage: Apply Distracted from environmental distractions, emotional turmoil, or multitasking. Use it to represent minor mental load. Partials on resisting might reduce disadvantage to +2 DC instead. Distracted is temporary but meaningful—it makes precision tasks harder.

Example: The scholar is Distracted by worry about the upcoming trial. Disadvantage on research checks until they resolve the legal issue or take time to focus.

Exhausted

Effect: Character is physically or mentally drained. Disadvantage on complex or strenuous actions. May need rest to recover fully.

Duration: Until full rest.

How to Clear: Full rest with food, water, and safety. Partial rest or stimulants might reduce severity temporarily but not cure.

GM Usage: Apply Exhausted after prolonged exertion, sleepless nights, or draining magic. Use it to represent attrition and create pacing. Partials on recovery might reduce Exhausted to Weakened or grant temporary reprieve. Exhausted should feel meaningful—it’s not just description, it’s mechanical consequence.

Example: After organizing the three-day festival, the event planner gains Exhausted. Disadvantage on complex tasks until a full night’s rest and hearty meal.

Frightened

Effect: Character is afraid of specific source. Disadvantage on actions opposing or approaching the source of fear. May flee if fear becomes overwhelming.

Duration: Scene or until source removed/overcome.

How to Clear: Remove threat, gain distance and safety, receive calming support, or overcome fear through brave action.

GM Usage: Apply Frightened from horror, intimidation, or overwhelming threats. Use it to create psychological pressure and moral tests. Partials on overcoming might reduce disadvantage or allow limited action despite fear. Frightened reflects real terror—honor it by making it consequential.

Example: The investigator sees the monster and becomes Frightened. Disadvantage on actions while in its presence. They can fight but must overcome fear or flee.

Hexed

Effect: Character is afflicted by minor curse or magical malady. Specific effects vary but often impose disadvantage on particular actions, attract minor misfortune, or create ongoing complications.

Duration: Clock (typically 3-4 segments) or until dispelled.

How to Clear: Dispel magic, wait for hex to fade, fulfill hex conditions, or perform cleansing.

GM Usage: Apply Hexed from curses, fey mischief, or magical traps. Use it to create ongoing complications and quests. Partials on dispelling might weaken hex without removing it. Hexed is less severe than Cursed—it’s magical inconvenience rather than doom.

Example: The fey hexes the character for rudeness. Hexed causes minor misfortunes (disadvantage on first social check each scene) until character performs an apology ritual.

Mana-Drained

Effect: Character’s magical reserves are depleted. Disadvantage on casting spells or using magical abilities. May be unable to cast higher-level magic.

Duration: Rest or until magical energy restored.

How to Clear: Full rest, consume mana potions, meditate in magical nexus, or receive magical energy transfer.

GM Usage: Apply Mana-Drained after casting powerful spells, magical overuse, or energy-draining attacks. Use it to create resource management and pacing. Partials on recovery might restore partial reserves. Mana-Drained is the spellcaster’s Exhausted—it gates their primary resource.

Example: The wizard casts the ritual and becomes Mana-Drained. Disadvantage on spellcasting until a full rest at a ley line.

Obsessed

Effect: Character is fixated on particular goal, person, or object. Advantage on actions directly pursuing obsession. Disadvantage on unrelated actions. May ignore other priorities.

Duration: Until obsession satisfied or broken.

How to Clear: Satisfy obsession, confront and overcome fixation, or suffer consequence that breaks focus.

GM Usage: Apply Obsessed from intense focus, magical compulsion, or character choices. Use it to create drives and complications. Partials on resisting might allow temporary focus on other tasks. Obsessed can be empowering (advantage on pursuit) and limiting (disadvantage elsewhere)—make both matter.

Example: The detective becomes Obsessed with solving the case. Advantage on investigation checks related to the case, disadvantage on other responsibilities.

Poisoned

Effect: Character is suffering from poison or toxin. Disadvantage on strenuous physical actions. May worsen over time if untreated (escalate to Weakened or suffer ongoing damage).

Duration: Until treated or rest.

How to Clear: Successful Medicine check to treat poison (DC 16), antidote, magical healing, or full rest (poison runs its course).

GM Usage: Apply Poisoned from traps, venomous creatures, contaminated food/water, or toxic environments. Use it to create urgency and consequences. Partials on treatment might reduce severity without curing. Poisoned can escalate—track with clocks if pressure mounts.

Example: The rogue triggers a poison needle trap and becomes Poisoned. Disadvantage on Athletics and combat until treated with antidote or Medicine (DC 16).

Prone

Effect: Character is on the ground. Disadvantage on melee attacks. Ranged attacks against character from adjacent attackers have advantage. Movement costs extra effort.

Duration: Until character stands (uses movement/action).

How to Clear: Stand up as action or movement (may provoke opportunity attacks or require check in difficult conditions).

GM Usage: Apply Prone from knockdowns, explosions, failed Athletics checks, or combat maneuvers. Use it to create tactical complications. Partials on standing might allow standing but at disadvantage or in poor position. Prone is common in combat—make it feel tactical, not just punitive.

Example: The explosion knocks the soldier Prone. They suffer disadvantage on attacks until they spend movement to stand (risky under fire).

Restrained

Effect: Character’s movement is restricted (bound, grappled, trapped). Cannot move freely. Disadvantage on physical actions requiring mobility. Attacks against restrained character may have advantage.

Duration: Until freed.

How to Clear: Break free (Athletics or Thievery check, DC varies by restraint), receive help from ally, or restrain source is removed.

GM Usage: Apply Restrained from grapples, bindings, traps, or environmental entanglement. Use it to create escape challenges and tactical complications. Partials on breaking free might loosen restraints or allow limited movement. Restrained removes agency—use it to create tension and stakes.

Example: The cultist binds the prisoner (Restrained). The prisoner can struggle to break free (DC 18 Athletics) or wait for allies to rescue them.

Shaken

Effect: Character is unsettled and unnerved. Disadvantage on morale checks and first action in stressful situations. Less severe than Frightened—unease rather than terror.

Duration: Scene or until confidence restored.

How to Clear: Receive encouragement, overcome challenge, remove source of unease, or rest.

GM Usage: Apply Shaken from near-misses, unsettling experiences, or psychological pressure. Use it to represent minor mental strain. Partials on overcoming might reduce disadvantage to +2 DC. Shaken is subtle—use it when full Frightened is too severe.

Example: The close call with the collapsing bridge leaves the character Shaken. Disadvantage on first action in next tense situation until they calm down.

Stunned

Effect: Character is dazed and cannot act effectively. Loses next action or acts at severe disadvantage (GM discretion). Cannot take reactions.

Duration: Brief (typically one round/action) or scene.

How to Clear: Time passes (condition fades after action lost), receive help from ally, or succeed on check to shake off effect.

GM Usage: Apply Stunned from concussive attacks, electrical shocks, overwhelming stimuli, or magical effects. Use it to create dramatic turning points and vulnerabilities. Partials on recovery might reduce duration or severity. Stunned is powerful—use it sparingly.

Example: The taser stuns the guard (Stunned for one round). They lose their next action, allowing the runner to escape.

Weakened

Effect: Character’s physical capabilities are reduced. Reduced effect on physical actions (successes accomplish less, partials barely succeed). May impose disadvantage on particularly strenuous tasks.

Duration: Rest or treatment.

How to Clear: Full rest, treatment for underlying cause (disease, poison, curse), or magical healing.

GM Usage: Apply Weakened from disease, poison, radiation, curses, or extreme exertion. Use it to represent attrition and debilitation. Partials on treatment might reduce severity without curing. Weakened makes characters less effective without removing capability—they can act but accomplish less.

Example: The radiation exposure leaves the engineer Weakened. Physical tasks have reduced effect until treated at medical bay.
