This glossary provides the mechanical foundation for tags and conditions across all genres. For combat-specific applications, see Chapter 19. For skill and proficiency interactions, see Chapters 06-17. For GM guidance on creating custom tags and conditions, see the GM Guide.

Chapter 19: Combat Mechanics Reference

Combat in this system is fiction-first and consequence-driven. Fights are not separate from the narrative—they are tense scenes where position, timing, and environment shape outcomes as much as skill. This chapter provides quick reference for combat structure, actions, positioning, and genre-specific examples.

For skill details, see Chapters 06-11. For tags and conditions affecting combat, see Chapter 18.

Turn Structure

Combat flows narratively rather than through rigid initiative systems. The GM determines when action becomes structured turn-based play based on pacing and tension.

When to Use Turn-Based Combat

Use structured turns when: - Multiple combatants act simultaneously and order matters - Positioning, timing, and action economy are tactically significant - Players benefit from clarity about who acts when

Stay narrative when: - Combat is one-sided (ambush, overwhelming force) - Fiction determines outcome (surprise attack, environmental kill) - Structured turns slow pacing without adding tension

Initiative (Optional)

If using initiative, keep it simple:

Narrative Initiative: GM determines order based on fiction. Who has advantage? Who strikes first? Who is caught flat-footed?

Simple Roll: Each combatant rolls d20. Highest goes first. Re-roll ties or let tied characters act simultaneously.

Speed-Based: Fast actions (drawing weapon, shouting warning) go before slow actions (casting ritual, reloading crossbow). GM adjudicates.

GM Guidance: Initiative is a tool, not a rule. Use it when it clarifies action. Drop it when it slows play without benefit.

Action Economy

On your turn, you can typically:

  • Move up to your movement range (close/near/far zones, or specific distances if using maps)
  • Take one action (attack, defend, maneuver, use item, help ally, other)
  • Take one reaction (respond to enemy action, if triggered)

GM Guidance: Keep action economy simple. If a player wants to do something reasonable (draw weapon while moving, shout while attacking), allow it unless it grants unfair advantage. Fiction-first means bending rules when it makes sense.

Combat Actions

Attack

Description: Strike an opponent with weapon, spell, or improvised object.

Default DCs: - Unaware Target: DC 12 (surprise attack, target doesn’t know you’re there) - Aware Target: DC 14-16 (standard combat engagement) - Defended Target: DC 18-20 (target actively defending, armored, behind cover)

Outcomes: - Success: Deal damage, achieve tactical goal (disarm, knock prone, push back). - Partial: Deal reduced damage or achieve goal with cost (counterattack, lose position, consume resource). - Failure: Miss, opponent gains advantage (counterattack, repositioning, morale boost).

Modifiers: Elevated (adv ranged from high ground), Dim (disadv ranged beyond short), Obscuring (disadv perception, adv stealth), Prone (disadv attacker, adv adjacent ranged attacks on prone target).

GM Guidance: Attacks should feel dynamic. Partials hit but glance off armor, or strike true but leave you exposed. Failures should complicate—missed shots alert guards, wild swings break cover, empty magazines require reloads.

Defend

Description: Actively protect yourself or ally from harm.

Default DCs: - Block/Parry Attack: Opposed check vs attacker - Take Cover: DC 14 (find and use available cover) - Protect Ally: DC 16-18 (intercept attack meant for someone else)

Outcomes: - Success: Negate attack, avoid harm, protect ally fully. - Partial: Reduce damage or consequences, but suffer cost (condition, disadvantage, lose position). - Failure: Defense fails, take full attack or worse consequences.

Modifiers: Fragile (cover breaks on partial/fail), Cramped (hard to maneuver defensively), Elevated (harder to defend from low ground).

GM Guidance: Defense is active and interesting. Partials might block but lose shield, or dodge but end up Prone. Let defending matter—it’s not just “lose your turn.”

Maneuver

Description: Tactical action to gain position, create advantage, or disrupt enemy.

Default DCs: - Gain High Ground: DC 14-16 (climb, jump, reposition to elevation) - Flank Opponent: DC 14 (coordinate with ally to attack from multiple sides) - Disarm/Trip: DC 16-18 (opposed check, remove weapon or knock prone) - Create Distraction: DC 14-16 (draw attention, enable ally action)

Outcomes: - Success: Gain advantage (adv on next action), impose disadvantage on enemy, or change battlefield state. - Partial: Achieve goal partially or temporarily, possibly at cost. - Failure: Maneuver fails, enemy capitalizes (you’re exposed, they gain advantage).

Modifiers: Slick (disadv fast movement), Cramped (limited maneuver space), Crowded (hard to move, easy to blend).

GM Guidance: Maneuvers create tactical depth. Let players describe creative actions and reward them mechanically. Partials might grant temporary advantage or enable one ally to capitalize. Maneuvers should feel like smart plays, not wasted turns.

Use Item

Description: Deploy equipment, consume potion, activate device.

Default DCs: - Simple Item (drink potion, light torch): No check or DC 10 - Complex Item (activate tech, set trap): DC 14-16 - Under Pressure (use item while under fire, threatened): +2 DC

Outcomes: - Success: Item functions as intended. - Partial: Item works but imperfectly (partial effect, delayed activation, resource cost). - Failure: Item fails, breaks, or triggers unintended consequence.

Modifiers: High-Tech (requires training, +2 DC without), Prototype (risk malfunction), Volatile (risk explosion/reaction).

GM Guidance: Items solve problems. Potions heal but taste vile (roleplay moment). Tech activates but draws attention. Partials create tension—grenade thrown but bounces unpredictably, medkit applied but supplies depleted.

Help

Description: Assist an ally’s action, granting them advantage or reducing DC.

Default DCs: - Grant Advantage: DC 14 (describe how you help, grant ally advantage on their next check) - Reduce DC: DC 16 (exceptional help reduces ally’s DC by 2) - Enable Impossible Action: DC 18-20 (your help makes something possible that wasn’t before)

Outcomes: - Success: Ally gains advantage or DC reduction. - Partial: Ally gains benefit but you suffer cost (exposed position, resource spent, condition). - Failure: Help fails, possibly hindering ally or exposing both.

Modifiers: Cramped (hard to coordinate), Crowded (allies separated), Teamwork proficiency (advantage on help actions).

GM Guidance: Help actions enable teamwork and spotlight moments. Let players describe creative assistance and reward it. Partials might grant advantage but at cost (you draw fire, you’re Prone afterward). Help should feel heroic and tactical.

Position in Combat

Position matters. High ground, cover, range, and flanking shape combat outcomes.

Cover

Partial Cover (Half-exposed): Attackers suffer +2 DC or disadvantage on ranged attacks.

Full Cover (Fully concealed): Cannot be targeted directly. Attackers must flush target out or reposition.

Fragile Cover (Breakable): Provides cover until attacked or stressed. Partial successes or failures may destroy cover (apply Fragile tag).

GM Guidance: Cover degrades. Partials on attacks might hit cover instead of target, eroding protection. Let players create cover (flip tables, use shields) and enemies destroy it.

Example: The runner ducks behind a concrete barrier (full cover). The corporate security can’t shoot directly but can flank or use grenades to flush them out.

Elevation

High Ground: Advantage on ranged attacks against lower targets. Disadvantage on melee attacks from lower ground against higher opponents.

Low Ground: Disadvantage on attacks against elevated opponents. Hard to see or target elevated enemies (may grant cover).

GM Guidance: Elevation creates tactical choices. Players should seek high ground for ranged combat. Climbing under fire is risky—apply Slick, Fragile, or Hazardous tags to vertical surfaces.

Example: The archer climbs the tower (Elevated tag). Advantage on ranged attacks against courtyard enemies. Enemies must climb or find ranged weapons to respond effectively.

Flanking

Flanked: Target is surrounded or attacked from multiple directions. Attackers gain advantage or defenders suffer disadvantage (GM discretion based on fiction).

GM Guidance: Flanking rewards positioning and teamwork. Let players coordinate to flank and enemies do the same. Flanking isn’t automatic—it requires positioning and opportunity.

Example: Two fighters coordinate to flank the ogre. Both roll with advantage as they attack from opposite sides, splitting the ogre’s attention.

Range Bands

Close (Melee/touch range): Melee weapons, grappling, immediate interaction.

Near (Thrown weapons, short-range firearms): Reliable ranged attacks, clear communication.

Far (Bows, rifles, long-range weapons): Disadvantage without clear line of sight or appropriate weapon. Obscuring, Dim, or distance impose penalties.

GM Guidance: Range matters for targeting and tactics. Dim lighting imposes disadvantage on Far range. Obscuring helps at all ranges. Let players close distance or create space tactically.

Environmental Effects in Combat

Tags shape combat dramatically. Use them to create dynamic, memorable fights.

Cramped Spaces

Effect: Large weapons suffer disadvantage. Movement restricted. Hard to maneuver or flank.

Tactical Implications: Favor small weapons (daggers, pistols). Grappling becomes viable. Archers struggle.

Example: The tavern brawl (Cramped). Greatswords are useless, but fists, daggers, and thrown mugs excel.

Elevated Combat

Effect: High ground grants advantage on ranged attacks. Low ground suffers disadvantage against elevated foes.

Tactical Implications: Control high ground. Force enemies to climb or close distance. Archers dominate.

Example: The castle wall fight. Defenders on walls have advantage; attackers must scale walls (Slick, Fragile) under fire.

Hazardous Environments

Effect: Failures or partials inflict conditions (Poisoned, Bleeding, Weakened). Environment actively threatens combatants.

Tactical Implications: Quick resolution preferred. Prolonged fights increase casualties. Use environment against enemies.

Example: The burning building (Hazardous). Every round risks Weakened (smoke inhalation) or Bleeding (falling debris). Players must rescue hostage and escape quickly.

Obscuring Conditions

Effect: Fog, smoke, darkness reduce visibility. Advantage on Stealth, disadvantage on Perception and ranged attacks.

Tactical Implications: Melee favored. Stealth becomes viable mid-combat. Ranged weapons struggle.

Example: The foggy docks (Obscuring). The smuggler uses fog to vanish mid-fight (advantage on Stealth), forcing guards into melee or blind shots (disadvantage on ranged).

Slick Surfaces

Effect: Fast movement and Athletics checks suffer disadvantage. Risk falling Prone on failures.

Tactical Implications: Slow, careful movement preferred. Charging or fleeing is risky.

Example: The icy bridge (Slick). The duel requires careful footing. Fast attacks risk falling Prone; patient fighters have advantage.

Conditions in Combat

Conditions dramatically affect combat capability. Apply them as consequences and narrative drivers.

Prone

Combat Effect: Disadvantage on melee attacks. Ranged attacks against prone target from adjacent range have advantage. Standing requires movement/action.

Tactical Use: Knock enemies Prone (trip, explosive force) to create vulnerability. Prone characters should stand or suffer consequences.

Example: The grenade knocks the soldier Prone. Enemies have advantage on ranged attacks until soldier spends action to stand (risky under fire).

Restrained

Combat Effect: Cannot move. Disadvantage on physical actions. Attacks against restrained target may have advantage.

Tactical Use: Grapple or trap enemies to neutralize threats. Restrained characters must break free or rely on allies.

Example: The wrestler grapples the assassin (Restrained). Assassin suffers disadvantage on attacks and cannot flee until breaking free (opposed Athletics check).

Blinded

Combat Effect: Disadvantage on attacks and perception. Cannot target at range effectively. Advantage on actions where vision is irrelevant.

Tactical Use: Flash grenades, blinding spells, or sand in eyes create openings. Blinded enemies are vulnerable.

Example: The flash grenade blinds the guard (Blinded for scene). Guard fires wildly (disadvantage), enabling runner to escape.

Stunned

Combat Effect: Lose next action or act at severe disadvantage. Cannot take reactions.

Tactical Use: Stun high-priority targets to create openings or neutralize threats temporarily.

Example: The taser stuns the guard (Stunned for one round). Guard loses action, allowing team to breach door unopposed.

Frightened

Combat Effect: Disadvantage on actions opposing source of fear. May flee if overwhelmed.

Tactical Use: Intimidate or terrify enemies to break morale. Frightened enemies are easier to defeat or route.

Example: The dragon’s roar Frightens the bandits. They suffer disadvantage on attacks against it and may flee if fight goes poorly.

Bleeding, Poisoned, Weakened

Combat Effect: Disadvantage or reduced effect on physical actions. Worsen over time if untreated.

Tactical Use: Apply through traps, environmental hazards, or special attacks. Create attrition and urgency.

Example: The poisoned blade inflicts Poisoned condition. Target fights at disadvantage and worsens without antidote.

Combat Examples by Genre

Cozy: Cooking Contest

Setup: Annual baking competition. Five contestants, three rounds, escalating complexity. Stakes: village bragging rights and artisan guild recognition.

Conflict Structure: Skill challenges (Cooking, Presentation) rather than violence. Opponents are rivals, not enemies.

Tags: Bustling (crowded festival), Comforting (supportive audience), Fragile (delicate pastries), Handmade (personal recipes).

Actions: - Attack: Outperform rival (Cooking check vs their result) - Defend: Salvage mistake (prevent Distracted or Exhausted conditions) - Maneuver: Impress judges (gain advantage on next round) - Help: Assist fellow contestant (builds reputation, grants advantage)

Conditions: Distracted (rival’s flourish unsettles), Exhausted (final round fatigue).

Outcome: Success wins round. Partial impresses but falls short. Failure creates setback (burned dish, dropped plate). Final winner determined by accumulated successes and judge favor.

Example Exchange: - Round 1: Player rolls Cooking (DC 16). Success—their pie is golden perfection. Rival rolls partial—good but crust cracks. - Round 2: Bustling tag distracts player (+2 DC). Player rolls partial—dish is good but plating rushed. Rival succeeds—immaculate presentation. - Round 3: Player uses Help action to encourage nervous contestant (advantage granted). Then rolls own dish (DC 18). Success—showstopping finale. Player wins contest, earns Artisan reputation tag.

Fantasy: Dungeon Fight with Environmental Hazards

Setup: Party fights goblins in collapsing ruins. Ancient pillars crack, floor is Slick (moss and water), torches provide Dim light, ceiling is Fragile.

Combatants: 3 PCs vs 5 goblins. Goblins know terrain, use hit-and-run tactics.

Tags: Dim (low light), Slick (wet floor), Fragile (crumbling ceiling), Hazardous (collapse risk), Cramped (tight corridors).

Turn 1: - Fighter attacks goblin (DC 16, standard combat). Rolls 18 (success)—sword strike fells goblin. - Rogue uses Maneuver to gain Elevated position on crumbling stairs (DC 14 Athletics). Rolls 15 (success)—now has advantage on ranged attacks. - Wizard casts spell to collapse ceiling on goblins (DC 18 Arcana). Rolls 16 (partial)—ceiling cracks but doesn’t fall. Creates Hazardous zone; goblins must dodge or risk Bleeding. - Goblins retreat, using Dim tag for advantage on Stealth. Two vanish into shadows; three attack party from cover.

Turn 2: - Fighter charges goblins on Slick floor (DC 16 Melee + disadvantage for Slick). Rolls 14 (fail)—slips, falls Prone. Goblins gain advantage. - Rogue shoots from Elevated position (advantage). Rolls 19 (success)—arrow hits goblin, forcing it into Hazardous zone. Ceiling collapses (GM trigger), inflicting Bleeding condition on goblin. - Wizard helps Fighter stand (DC 14 Help action). Rolls 15 (success)—Fighter can act next turn without spending action to rise. - Goblins attack Prone Fighter with advantage. Roll 17 (success)—Fighter takes hit, suffers Bleeding condition.

Turn 3: - Fighter uses Defend action to block goblin attacks while Rogue and Wizard handle threats. - Rogue shoots final goblin (advantage from elevation). Rolls 20 (success)—clean kill. - Wizard stabilizes Fighter’s Bleeding (Medicine DC 14). Rolls partial—Bleeding slowed but Fighter is Weakened until rest.

Outcome: Party wins but pays cost. Fighter is Weakened, wizard used spell slot, ruins partially collapsed (escape harder). Goblins’ hit-and-run tactics and environmental knowledge made fight dangerous despite numerical inferiority.

Horror: Desperate Escape from Pursuing Threat

Setup: Investigator flees unseen horror through Dim, Obscuring fog in abandoned hospital. Goal: reach exit before horror catches them. This is chase scene, not stand-up fight.

Threat: Unseen horror (stat-less, GM-controlled narrative force). Represents terror, not tactical enemy.

Tags: Dim (emergency lights), Obscuring (fog/dust), Fragile (decayed floor), Silent (unnaturally quiet), Dread-Soaked (horror presence).

Structure: Clocks. Escape Progress (6 segments, investigator). Pursuit Progress (6 segments, horror). Investigator wins by filling Escape clock first.

Turn 1: - Investigator runs through corridors (DC 14 Athletics to advance Escape clock). Rolls 17 (success)—advances 2 segments. But Fragile floor cracks underfoot (partial consequence). Noise advances Pursuit clock by 1. - Horror advances automatically (GM narrates sounds, movement, presence growing closer).

Turn 2: - Investigator uses Stealth to hide in office (DC 16, uses Obscuring for advantage). Rolls partial—hidden but cramped, suffers Distracted condition. Pursuit clock doesn’t advance this turn (horror searches elsewhere). - Horror searches (GM describes scraping sounds, breathing, shadows moving). Tension mounts.

Turn 3: - Investigator resists Frightened condition (DC 16 Composure due to Dread-Soaked tag). Rolls fail—becomes Frightened. Disadvantage on actions opposing horror (running toward it, confronting it). - Investigator chooses to flee rather than hide (smart choice given Frightened). Rolls Athletics (DC 14 + disadvantage). Rolls 15 (partial due to disadvantage)—advances Escape clock by 1 but crashes through Fragile door. Loud noise advances Pursuit clock by 2. Horror is close.

Turn 4: - Investigator spots exit ahead. Rolls final Athletics (DC 16, Frightened disadvantage). Rolls 18 despite disadvantage (success)—bursts through exit, slams door, escapes into night. Escape clock fills. - Horror reaches door moments later. Investigator escapes but is Frightened and Exhausted. Barely survived.

Outcome: Investigator escapes but mentally scarred. Frightened and Exhausted conditions persist. Horror remains threat—undefeated, merely evaded. Scene establishes horror’s power and investigator’s vulnerability.

Modern: Urban Gunfight with Cover Mechanics

Setup: Corporate runner vs security team in parking garage. Cover abundant (cars, pillars). Range matters. Tactical positioning key.

Combatants: 1 runner vs 3 security guards. Guards have numbers, runner has skill and surprise.

Tags: Cramped (garage), Dim (emergency lighting), Crowded (parked cars = cover), Fragile (car windows), Corporate (security protocols).

Turn 1: - Runner uses surprise to attack Guard 1 from cover (DC 14, unaware target). Rolls 18 (success)—silenced pistol drops guard quietly. - Guard 2 spots body, calls alert (Alert tag applied). Raises DC for stealth to +2. - Guard 3 takes cover behind car (full cover). Cannot be targeted directly.

Turn 2: - Runner uses Maneuver to flank Guard 3 (DC 14). Rolls 16 (success)—repositions to angle on guard, negating cover. - Guard 3 fires at runner (DC 16). Rolls partial—shot hits car next to runner (Fragile tag), shattering window. Glass shards inflict minor Bleeding condition on runner. - Guard 2 advances while firing (DC 18, shooting while moving). Rolls fail—misses, wastes ammunition.

Turn 3: - Runner shoots Guard 3 now that cover is negated (DC 16). Rolls 19 (success)—guard down. - Guard 2 retreats to call reinforcements (tactical withdrawal). Runner can engage or flee before backup arrives.

Runner Choice: Flee. Goal was data theft, not firefight. Uses Crowded (cars) and Dim tags to vanish before reinforcements arrive.

Outcome: Runner completes objective (data acquired) but suffers Bleeding condition and Alert tag follows (corporate security knows intruder was present). Escape clean but not unscathed. Consequences will follow (corporate response clock starts).

Sci-Fi: Zero-G Battle Aboard Space Station

Setup: Marines board derelict station to investigate. Encounter hostile aliens in zero-gravity hangar. Microgravity changes combat dynamics.

Combatants: 2 marines vs 3 aliens. Aliens adapted to zero-g; marines are not.

Tags: Microgravity (Cramped equivalent for movement), Obscuring (venting atmosphere), Hazardous (Hard Vacuum if suit breached), Dim (emergency lighting), Fragile (hull integrity).

Turn 1: - Marine 1 fires rifle at alien (DC 16, microgravity makes aiming harder). Rolls partial—hits alien but recoil sends marine spinning. Marine suffers Distracted condition (must stabilize before next action). - Marine 2 uses mag-boots to anchor and fire (DC 14, stable platform). Rolls 18 (success)—clean hit, alien injured. - Aliens use zero-g maneuverability (advantage on movement). One flanks, two coordinate attack.

Turn 2: - Marine 1 uses thrusters to stabilize (DC 14 Athletics). Rolls 15 (success)—Distracted cleared, can act normally. - Marine 2 defends against flanking alien (opposed check). Rolls partial—blocks but loses position, drifts into Hazardous zone (exposed to venting atmosphere). - Aliens press advantage. One attacks Marine 2 in Hazardous zone (DC 16). Rolls success—breaches marine’s suit. Marine begins suffocating (Weakened condition, escalates if not sealed).

Turn 3: - Marine 1 shoots alien threatening Marine 2 (DC 16). Rolls 19 (success)—alien eliminated. - Marine 2 uses emergency sealant on suit (DC 14 Use Item under pressure). Rolls partial—suit sealed but sealant depleted. No more repairs possible. Weakened condition persists until proper repair. - Aliens retreat into station depths. Marines have won hangar but are injured and low on resources.

Outcome: Marines secure hangar but Marine 2 is Weakened and suit integrity compromised. Must decide: press on with damaged gear or retreat for repairs. Microgravity and environmental hazards made fight more dangerous than ground combat would have been. Aliens remain threat deeper in station.

GM Guidance: Running Dynamic Combat

Make environment matter: Use tags actively. Fragile cover breaks. Slick floors cause slips. Dim light hides threats. Environment should shape tactics, not just flavor.

Position is tactical: High ground, cover, flanking, and range create meaningful choices. Let players maneuver for advantage and enemies do the same.

Conditions drive consequences: Combat inflicts conditions, not just damage. Bleeding creates urgency. Frightened breaks morale. Stunned creates openings. Use conditions to make fights memorable.

Partials create drama: Partial successes hit but at cost. Shields break. Ammunition depletes. Positions are lost. Partials keep fights tense and dynamic.

Not all fights are to the death: Enemies flee when losing. Players can intimidate, negotiate, or escape. Combat is scene type, not required endpoint.

Failure forward: Failed attacks miss but create complications (alert guards, break cover, waste ammunition). Failures should drive story, not stall it.

For detailed skill mechanics, see Chapters 06-11. For comprehensive tag and condition effects, see Chapter 18. For narrative combat guidance, see the GM Guide.

Chapter 20: Character Progression Reference

Character progression makes growth tangible. As characters overcome challenges, they gain skills, advance proficiencies, and evolve narratively. This chapter provides quick reference for advancement systems, costs, and genre-specific progression pacing.

For skill details, see Chapters 06-11. For proficiency details, see Chapters 12-17. For narrative progression guidance, see the Player Guide and Storyteller Guide.

Advancement Systems

Three primary systems govern character progression. Choose the one that fits your campaign’s pacing and style, or blend them.

XP-Based Advancement

How It Works: Characters earn experience points (XP) for overcoming challenges, achieving goals, and advancing story. Accumulate XP to purchase skills, advance proficiencies, or improve attributes.

XP Awards: - Minor Success (solve small problem, overcome minor challenge): 1-2 XP - Moderate Success (complete mission, defeat significant threat): 3-5 XP - Major Success (achieve campaign milestone, overcome major antagonist): 6-10 XP

Costs: - New Skill: 5 XP (learn new skill at basic competence) - Advance Proficiency (Novice → Trained): 10 XP - Advance Proficiency (Trained → Expert): 20 XP - Advance Proficiency (Expert → Master): 30 XP - Attribute Improvement (if applicable): 15 XP per +1

GM Guidance: XP-based systems reward consistent play and granular advancement. Award XP at session end or after major scenes. Let players bank XP or spend immediately. XP creates clear progression path and player agency over growth.

When to Use: Long campaigns with regular sessions. Players who enjoy mechanical progression and optimization. Games where growth is steady and incremental.

Milestone-Based Advancement

How It Works: Characters advance when they achieve narrative milestones—complete story arcs, overcome major challenges, or reach dramatic turning points. GM determines when milestones occur.

Milestone Types: - Minor Milestone (complete session objective, resolve subplot): Gain 1 new skill OR advance 1 proficiency by 1 tier - Major Milestone (complete story arc, defeat major antagonist, reach campaign turning point): Gain 2 new skills OR advance 2 proficiencies OR mix (1 skill + 1 proficiency advance) - Campaign Milestone (complete major campaign phase, reach endgame): Significant advancement (3-5 choices from skills, proficiencies, or attributes)

GM Guidance: Milestones tie advancement directly to story. No tracking XP—growth happens at narratively appropriate moments. Communicate upcoming milestones so players can anticipate advancement. Milestones work well for episodic play or campaigns with clear arcs.

When to Use: Story-driven campaigns with clear arcs. Groups that prefer narrative progression over mechanical tracking. Irregular session schedules where XP might feel inconsistent.

Session-Based Advancement

How It Works: Characters advance at fixed intervals—every session, every 2-3 sessions, or at GM discretion. Simple, predictable, low-bookkeeping.

Standard Cadence: - Every Session: Gain 1 skill OR advance 1 proficiency tier - Every 2-3 Sessions: Gain 1 skill AND advance 1 proficiency tier - Every 5 Sessions: Major advancement (2 skills OR 2 proficiency advances OR mix + possible attribute improvement)

GM Guidance: Session-based advancement is easiest to track and most predictable. Players know when to expect growth. Works well for groups with consistent schedules. Adjust cadence to match campaign length (faster for short campaigns, slower for long ones).

When to Use: Casual groups, new players, or short campaigns. When you want minimal bookkeeping and maximum predictability.

What Advances

Skills

Gaining New Skills: Characters learn new skills through training, experience, or narrative justification. New skills represent capabilities they didn’t have before.

How to Justify: - Downtime Training: Spend time with mentor, guild, or teacher. Requires time (days to weeks) and possibly cost (tuition, materials). - Narrative Growth: Learn through experience during play (thief learns Lockpicking after watching expert, scholar learns Arcana from ancient tome). - Milestone Reward: Gain skill as reward for achieving milestone that fits narratively (completing heist earns Thievery, surviving wilderness earns Survival).

Limits: GMs may cap total skills per character (typically 5-8 skills) to maintain focus and specialization. Skills should reflect character concept and story.

Example: After apprenticing with the village healer for two weeks (downtime), the fighter gains Medicine skill. Now they can stabilize allies and treat injuries.

Proficiencies

Tier Progression: Proficiencies advance through tiers (Novice → Trained → Expert → Master). Each tier grants stronger benefits and broader applications.

How to Advance: - Downtime Training: Spend time with mentor or guild. Requires time, cost, and narrative justification. Higher tiers require expert mentors (cannot train to Expert without Expert or Master mentor). - Milestone Reward: Advance proficiency tier as reward for achievements (surviving brutal campaign earns First Aid advancement, completing diplomatic mission advances Diplomat). - XP Purchase: Spend accumulated XP to advance tier (costs scale: 10 XP Novice→Trained, 20 XP Trained→Expert, 30 XP Expert→Master).

Limits: Characters typically have 2-4 proficiencies. Quality over quantity—focused expertise is more interesting than broad shallow coverage.

Example: The Trained Ritualist completes a major consecration ritual (milestone). GM awards advancement to Expert Ritualist, granting ability to perform more complex rituals and reduced DCs.

Attributes

If Using Attributes: Some tables use attributes (Strength, Intellect, Charisma, etc.) to modify checks. If so, attributes advance slowly and represent fundamental capability growth.

How to Advance: - XP Purchase: Expensive (15-20 XP per +1). Represents significant investment. - Major Milestone: Award +1 to attribute at campaign milestones (completing major arc, reaching new campaign phase). - Narrative Transformation: Character undergoes significant change (magical enhancement, divine blessing, cybernetic upgrade). Justify mechanically with attribute increase.

Limits: Attributes should advance rarely (2-3 times per campaign). They represent baseline capability, not learned skill. Keep increases bounded (typically +0 to +5 range).

GM Guidance: Attributes are optional. Many tables run entirely on skills and proficiencies. If using attributes, keep advancement slow and meaningful.

Milestone Rewards

Milestones tie mechanical advancement to narrative beats. Here are example milestone types and appropriate rewards.

Story Arc Completion

Trigger: Complete major story arc (rescue the prince, stop the ritual, solve the murder).

Reward: - Gain 1 new skill related to arc (Investigation after solving mystery, Combat after warfare arc) - Advance 1 proficiency tier related to arc - Gain narrative reward (reputation, title, resource)

Example: After defeating the necromancer and ending the undead plague, the party earns milestone. Cleric advances Ritualist proficiency (Trained → Expert). Fighter gains Intimidation skill (reputation as undead-slayer). Both gain “Hero of the Realm” reputation tag.

Character Transformation

Trigger: Character undergoes significant personal change (overcomes fear, embraces destiny, loses innocence).

Reward: - Gain skill reflecting transformation (gain Composure after overcoming trauma, gain Leadership after accepting responsibility) - Advance proficiency that fits new identity - Remove or gain condition/tag reflecting change

Example: The coward finds courage during desperate battle. Milestone triggers transformation. Character gains Tactics skill (now leads rather than follows) and loses Shaken condition permanently (fear overcome). Player and GM narrate transformation moment.

Training and Study

Trigger: Character completes significant downtime training (weeks with master, months at academy, intensive study).

Reward: - Gain new skill or advance proficiency tier - Gain access to restricted knowledge, guild membership, or specialized equipment - Reduce future training costs in related areas (master’s endorsement)

Example: After three months at the Arcane Academy (downtime), the wizard advances Arcana skill and gains Scribe proficiency (Novice). They also gain access to academy library (resource for future research).

Survival and Endurance

Trigger: Character survives brutal ordeal (deadly combat, harsh wilderness, psychological horror).

Reward: - Gain skill reflecting survival lessons (Survival after wilderness ordeal, Composure after horror) - Advance proficiency related to survival (First Aid after treating own wounds, Urban Survival after escaping city pursuit) - Gain condition representing lasting impact (Scarred tag, Hardened condition)

Example: After barely escaping the collapsing mine (horror/survival scene), the character gains Urban Survival skill and Exhausted condition (physical toll). They also gain “Mine Survivor” tag (narrative reputation).

Downtime Training

Downtime training allows characters to advance between adventures. It requires time, resources, and narrative justification.

Training Costs and Requirements

Advancement Time Required Cost Mentor Required
New Skill 1-2 weeks 50-100 gp (or equivalent) Optional (reduces time to 1 week)
Novice → Trained 2-4 weeks 100-200 gp Trained or higher mentor
Trained → Expert 4-8 weeks 300-500 gp Expert or Master mentor
Expert → Master 12-24 weeks 1,000+ gp Master mentor (rare)

GM Guidance: Adjust costs and time to fit campaign pacing and economy. High-magic settings might have cheaper training (magic academies abundant). Low-magic settings might require rare mentors and extended time. Use training as adventure hooks—finding a Master mentor might be quest itself.

Finding Mentors

Mentors are NPCs: Guilds, academies, and settlements have experts willing to teach. Finding appropriate mentor might require investigation, social checks, or quests.

Mentor Availability by Tier: - Novice/Trained Mentors: Common in towns and cities. Guild halls, academies, professional organizations. - Expert Mentors: Rare. Found in major cities, specialized guilds, or remote locations. May require introduction or reputation. - Master Mentors: Very rare. Legendary figures, reclusive masters, or faction leaders. Require quests, favors, or extraordinary circumstances to access.

Example: The rogue wants to advance Thievery (Trained → Expert). They need Expert mentor. Investigation reveals master thief in capital city. Gaining access requires completing job for Thieves’ Guild (adventure hook). Upon completion, mentor agrees to train (4 weeks, 400 gp).

Self-Study

Without Mentor: Characters can self-study but it takes longer and may have limits.

Self-Study Modifiers: - Time: Double normal training time - Resources Required: Access to books, tools, or practice materials - Limits: Cannot advance beyond Trained without mentor (Expert and Master require direct instruction)

Example: The wizard wants to learn new skill (History) but has no mentor. They spend 4 weeks in library (double normal 2 weeks) and 50 gp on books. Successfully gains History skill through self-study.

Narrative Growth

Mechanical advancement should integrate with character arcs. Growth is not just numbers—it’s story.

Integrating Advancement with Story

Ask narrative questions when advancing: - How did you learn this? (New skill or proficiency advance) - What changed in you? (Attribute increase or transformation) - Who taught you? (Mentor-based training) - What did this cost you? (Sacrifices or consequences of growth)

Example: Fighter advances Melee Combat skill. Player narrates: “After watching my mentor die in the duel, I swore to master his techniques. Every evening, I practice his forms, hearing his voice correcting my stance. His sword is mine now, and I will honor his legacy.”

GM Guidance: Encourage players to tie advancement to story. Advancement scenes (montages, training sessions, breakthrough moments) are opportunities for character development. Let mechanical growth reflect narrative growth.

Growth Through Failure

Failure teaches: Characters can advance through failure as well as success. Failed checks, defeated battles, and lost conflicts create growth opportunities.

Failure-Based Milestones: - Learn from Defeat: After losing fight, character trains to overcome weakness (gain skill or advance combat proficiency) - Adapt to Loss: After failing mission, character develops new capability to prevent future failure - Overcome Trauma: After suffering condition or setback, character grows stronger (gain resistance, proficiency, or skill)

Example: The diplomat fails to prevent war (failed negotiation). Rather than punish player, GM offers milestone: gain Intimidation skill (learned that sometimes force is necessary) or advance Diplomat proficiency (learned from mistakes, won’t fail again).

GM Guidance: Failure forward includes advancement. Let meaningful failures drive growth. Not every failure earns advancement, but significant defeats and lessons learned should.

Examples by Genre

Progression feels different in each genre. Here’s how advancement plays out across settings.

Cozy Progression

Pacing: Slow, comfortable, tied to seasons and community events. Advancement reflects deepening roots and growing reputation.

Typical Advancement: - Session-Based: Every 2-3 sessions (reflecting seasonal changes, community projects) - Milestones: Festival completion, community crisis resolved, personal relationship milestones - Downtime: Abundant. Characters have time to train, craft, and build relationships.

Example Progression Arc: - Session 1-3: Character learns Baking skill (trains with village baker, prepares for festival) - Session 4-6: Festival arc. Milestone: advance Baking proficiency (Novice → Trained), gain Hospitality skill - Session 7-9: Character opens bakery (downtime project), trains apprentice, becomes community fixture - Session 10: Major milestone. Advance to Expert Baker, gain “Village Treasure” reputation tag

Feel: Progression is about belonging and mastery. Characters become pillars of community, recognized experts, and sources of comfort.

Fantasy Progression

Pacing: Moderate to fast. Tied to completing quests, defeating monsters, and achieving heroic deeds.

Typical Advancement: - XP-Based or Milestone: XP for quest-heavy games, milestones for story-heavy campaigns - Milestones: Quest completion, defeating major enemy, discovering powerful artifact - Downtime: Periodic. Between adventures, characters train with guilds or masters.

Example Progression Arc: - Sessions 1-5: Adventuring arc. Earn XP, accumulate 10 XP. Purchase Survival skill (preparing for wilderness quest). - Session 6: Defeat necromancer (milestone). Advance First Aid (Trained → Expert), gain “Undead Slayer” reputation tag. - Downtime: Two weeks training with weapons master. Advance Weapon Master proficiency (Novice → Trained). - Sessions 7-12: New arc. Earn XP, advance additional skills and proficiencies.

Feel: Progression is heroic and achievement-based. Characters become legendary through deeds, recognized by skills and reputation.

Horror Progression

Pacing: Slow. Survival is achievement; growth is hard-won. Advancement reflects trauma and adaptation.

Typical Advancement: - Milestone-Based: Tied to surviving encounters, uncovering truths, overcoming fear - Milestones: Survive horror encounter, solve mystery, overcome personal fear - Downtime: Limited. Horror settings are relentless; rest is brief and fraught.

Example Progression Arc: - Sessions 1-3: Investigation and survival. No advancement—characters barely survive. - Session 4: Survive encounter with monster (milestone). Gain Composure skill (learned to manage fear) and Shaken condition (lasting trauma). - Downtime: Brief rest. Shaken clears, but character is changed. - Sessions 5-8: Continued investigation. Second milestone: advance Composure, gain “Hardened” tag (fear is familiar now). - Final Session: Defeat horror (major milestone). Advance Investigation skill, gain Occult Lore proficiency, but suffer lasting condition (Scarred, Haunted).

Feel: Progression is survival. Characters become tougher, warier, and scarred. Growth is bittersweet—capabilities increase but innocence is lost.

Modern Progression

Pacing: Moderate. Tied to completing jobs, building reputation, and acquiring resources.

Typical Advancement: - XP or Session-Based: XP for gig-based games (shadowrunners, freelancers), session-based for episodic campaigns - Milestones: Complete job, avoid capture, build network - Downtime: Regular. Between jobs, characters train, network, and upgrade equipment.

Example Progression Arc: - Sessions 1-3: Complete heist (milestone). Gain Hacking skill, advance Stealth proficiency (Novice → Trained). - Downtime: Purchase new cyberdeck (equipment upgrade, paid from heist proceeds). Train with fixer contact (gain Streetwise skill). - Sessions 4-6: Corporate espionage arc. Earn XP, advance proficiencies. - Session 7: Betrayed by client (failure milestone). Gain Intimidation skill (won’t be double-crossed again) and “Burned by Corps” reputation tag.

Feel: Progression is professional. Characters become specialists, networked operators, and survivors in ruthless world.

Sci-Fi Progression

Pacing: Moderate to fast. Tied to missions, discoveries, and technological advancement.

Typical Advancement: - XP or Milestone-Based: XP for exploration/episodic games, milestones for campaign arcs - Milestones: Complete mission, make first contact, survive space hazard - Downtime: Variable. Shipboard life provides training time; crises limit rest.

Example Progression Arc: - Sessions 1-4: Exploration mission. Earn XP, purchase Pilot Spacecraft skill (training during long voyage). - Session 5: First contact with alien species (milestone). Gain Xenobiology skill, advance Diplomacy proficiency. - Downtime: Ship repair and crew training. Advance System Engineering proficiency (Trained → Expert). - Sessions 6-10: Crisis arc (alien threat). Rapid advancement due to extreme circumstances. Gain Zero-G Combat skill, advance multiple proficiencies. - Final Session: Save colony (major milestone). Gain “Hero of the Frontier” reputation, advance to Master tier in key proficiency.

Feel: Progression is discovery and adaptation. Characters become frontier experts, explorers, and survivors of the unknown.

GM Guidance: Pacing Advancement

Match pacing to campaign length: - Short campaigns (6-12 sessions): Fast advancement. Milestones every 2-3 sessions or XP awards generous. Let characters feel powerful. - Medium campaigns (12-30 sessions): Moderate advancement. Balance mechanical growth with story development. - Long campaigns (30+ sessions): Slow advancement. Let characters savor each new capability. Growth is earned and meaningful.

Communicate advancement expectations: Tell players how fast advancement will be. Set expectations about milestone frequency or XP awards. Avoid surprises.

Tie advancement to story: Mechanical growth should reflect narrative growth. Advancement scenes (training montages, breakthrough moments, mentorship) are roleplay opportunities.

Reward different playstyles: Some players optimize mechanics; others focus on story. Offer both mechanical rewards (skills, proficiencies) and narrative rewards (reputation, relationships, resources).

Let failure teach: Meaningful failures can trigger advancement. Learn from defeats, adapt to setbacks, overcome trauma. Growth through adversity is powerful storytelling.

For detailed skill mechanics, see Chapters 06-11. For proficiency mechanics, see Chapters 12-17. For narrative progression guidance, see the Player Guide and Storyteller Guide.

Advantage/Disadvantage Flowchart

Visual guide for when to use advantage and disadvantage.

Quick Decision Process

START: Player wants to make a check
    ↓
Are there FAVORABLE circumstances?
    ↓
YES → Consider Advantage
NO  → Continue
    ↓
Are there UNFAVORABLE circumstances?
    ↓
YES → Consider Disadvantage
NO  → Straight roll
    ↓
Do both Advantage and Disadvantage apply?
    ↓
YES → They cancel, straight roll
NO  → Apply whichever one exists
    ↓
ROLL

Advantage: When to Grant

Category 1: Preparation and Planning

☑ Thorough preparation - Scouted location beforehand - Researched target/situation - Practiced specific skill - Created detailed plan

☑ Perfect tools/resources - Ideal equipment for task - Custom-made tools - Professional-grade gear - Magical enhancement

☑ Expert guidance - Mentor advising - Expert consultation - Detailed instructions - Training specifically for this

Category 2: Tactical Advantage

☑ Superior position - High ground in combat - Hidden from target - Flanking position (if using flanking rules) - Ambush position

☑ Opponent disadvantaged - Target distracted - Enemy wounded/impaired - Target doesn’t see you coming - Enemy fighting multiple threats

☑ Environmental benefit - Cover while they have none - Light while they’re in darkness - Stable ground while they’re unstable - Favorable weather/conditions

Category 3: Ally Assistance

☑ Direct help - Ally actively assisting - Teamwork on task - Boosted by ally ability - Working together

☑ Setup/enabling - Ally created opportunity - Previous action enables yours - Combination move - Support from teammate

Category 4: Circumstantial

☑ Target is vulnerable - Friendly and trusting - Weakened or compromised - Wants to cooperate - Predisposed to help

☑ Situation favors you - Cultural/social advantage - Reputation precedes you - Authority in situation - Right place/right time

Category 5: Proficiency Excellence

☑ Proficiency particularly applicable - Using exact specialty - Perfect match for training - Elite-level expertise - Master demonstrating skill

Disadvantage: When to Impose

Category 1: Improvisation and Lack

☐ Unprepared - No planning - First time trying - Winging it - No intel or knowledge

☐ Wrong/missing tools - Improvised tools - Broken equipment - No tools at all - Inadequate resources

☐ No expertise - Completely untrained - Outside area of knowledge - Never done before - No guidance

Category 2: Tactical Disadvantage

☐ Inferior position - Low ground - Exposed with no cover - Surrounded - Trapped or cornered

☐ Self at disadvantage - Fighting while prone - Moving through difficult terrain - Poor visibility - Hostile environment

☐ Opponent advantaged - Enemy has high ground - Enemy in cover - Enemy prepared - Enemy has backup

Category 3: Impairment

☐ Physical impairment - Wounded - Exhausted - Poisoned - Encumbered

☐ Mental impairment - Shaken or frightened - Confused or dazed - Distracted - Overwhelmed

☐ Environmental impairment - Darkness - Loud noise - Extreme weather - Hazardous conditions

Category 4: Active Opposition

☐ Someone working against you - Guard actively watching - Opponent countering moves - Expert lie detector - Aware of your attempt

☐ Complications - Time pressure (rushed) - High stakes (nervous) - Multiple obstacles - Hostile crowd

Category 5: Inherent Difficulty

☐ Task is harder than normal - Complex variation - Missing key information - Requires multiple skills - Conditional success needed

Advantage/Disadvantage Interaction

Single Source vs Multiple

1 source of Advantage = Advantage
Multiple sources of Advantage = Still just Advantage
(Same for Disadvantage)

Advantage + Disadvantage

Any Advantage + Any Disadvantage = CANCEL
Result: Straight roll (2d6, no modification)

Even if:
- 3 sources of Advantage + 1 source of Disadvantage = Straight roll
- 1 source of Advantage + 5 sources of Disadvantage = Straight roll

Common Scenarios

Combat Advantage

  • ✓ High ground
  • ✓ Flanking (optional rule)
  • ✓ Hidden from enemy
  • ✓ Ally creates opening
  • ✓ Enemy prone/restrained
  • ✓ Target surprised

Combat Disadvantage

  • ✗ You are prone
  • ✗ You are restrained
  • ✗ You are blinded
  • ✗ Fighting in darkness
  • ✗ Long range shot
  • ✗ You are wounded

Social Advantage

  • ✓ Target is friendly
  • ✓ Perfect timing
  • ✓ Leverage or information
  • ✓ Cultural/social position
  • ✓ Ally vouches for you
  • ✓ Target wants deal

Social Disadvantage

  • ✗ Target is hostile
  • ✗ You’re outsider
  • ✗ Bad timing
  • ✗ No credibility
  • ✗ Bad reputation
  • ✗ Target suspicious

Physical Advantage

  • ✓ Perfect tools
  • ✓ Ideal conditions
  • ✓ Ample time
  • ✓ Expert help
  • ✓ Practiced this before
  • ✓ Favorable environment

Physical Disadvantage

  • ✗ Improvised tools
  • ✗ Harsh conditions
  • ✗ Rushed/time pressure
  • ✗ Injured/exhausted
  • ✗ Unfamiliar task
  • ✗ Hostile environment

Investigation Advantage

  • ✓ Good information
  • ✓ Expert analysis tools
  • ✓ Time to be thorough
  • ✓ Additional clues
  • ✓ Cooperation from witnesses
  • ✓ Clear trail

Investigation Disadvantage

  • ✗ Old/degraded evidence
  • ✗ Crime scene contaminated
  • ✗ Time pressure
  • ✗ Misleading information
  • ✗ Uncooperative witnesses
  • ✗ Obscured trail

Stealth Advantage

  • ✓ Darkness/shadows
  • ✓ Loud distractions
  • ✓ Guards distracted
  • ✓ Know patrol patterns
  • ✓ Crowd to blend with
  • ✓ Excellent cover

Stealth Disadvantage

  • ✗ Bright light
  • ✗ Quiet environment
  • ✗ Alert guards
  • ✗ Unknown territory
  • ✗ Open ground
  • ✗ Noisy equipment

Decision Matrix

When Circumstance is…

Situation Mechanical Effect
Exceptionally favorable Advantage
Somewhat favorable +2 bonus (no advantage)
Standard No modifier
Somewhat unfavorable -2 penalty (no disadvantage)
Exceptionally unfavorable Disadvantage

Advantage vs Bonus

Use Advantage When

  • Circumstantial benefit
  • Represents opportunity, not skill
  • Situational, not permanent
  • Multiple factors combine

Use Bonus When

  • Permanent character ability
  • Specific numerical benefit
  • Stacks with other effects
  • Represents training/gear

Can Use Both

Example: Expert lockpicking
- Thievery skill: +2 (bonus)
- Lockpicking proficiency: +1 (bonus)  
- Quality tools: +2 (bonus)
- Guards distracted: Advantage
Total: 2d6+5 with advantage

Edge Cases

Do I grant Advantage or lower DC?

Advantage: Circumstantial, temporary benefit Lower DC: Task itself is easier

Example: Climbing
- Rope ladder = Lower DC (task is easier)
- Ally belaying you = Advantage (help, but still same difficulty)

Multiple reasons for same Advantage?

Still just one Advantage

Example: Three reasons for combat advantage
- High ground
- Enemy distracted
- Ally flanking
Result: Just roll with advantage once (not 3x)

Advantage on already easy task?

Advantage still applies

Even with advantage, can still roll poorly
Advantage on DC 6 task is not auto-success

Disadvantage on already hard task?

Disadvantage still applies

Makes hard task even less likely to succeed
May become effectively impossible

Probability Reference

Success Rates (with +2 skill)

DC Normal With Advantage With Disadvantage
6 95% 99%+ 83%
8 85% 95% 64%
10 70% 87% 44%
12 50% 72% 25%
14 30% 51% 11%
16 15% 28% 3%

Key Insight: - Advantage roughly equivalent to +3 bonus - Disadvantage roughly equivalent to -3 penalty - Impact is greatest at moderate DCs (10-12)

Quick Reference Flowchart

CHECK REQUIRED
     ↓
Multiple favorable factors? → YES → Advantage
     ↓ NO
Single favorable factor? → YES → +2 bonus
     ↓ NO
Normal conditions? → YES → Straight roll
     ↓ NO
Single unfavorable factor? → YES → -2 penalty
     ↓ NO
Multiple unfavorable factors? → YES → Disadvantage
     ↓
Any Advantage AND any Disadvantage? → YES → Cancel to straight roll
     ↓ NO
ROLL WITH DETERMINED MODIFIER

Common Questions

Q: Can I have double advantage? A: No. Multiple sources still = one advantage.

Q: Does +2 bonus count as advantage? A: No. Bonuses and advantage are separate. You can have both.

Q: If I have advantage, can I reroll? A: No. Advantage means roll 2d6 once, take higher. Not reroll.

Q: Can I choose between advantage and +2? A: No. GM determines which applies based on situation.

Q: Do I need to track all sources of advantage? A: Only need to note that advantage applies. Sources don’t stack.

Flowchart Version: 1.0.0

Clock & Progress Templates

Ready-to-use templates for tracking ongoing situations and threats.

What Are Clocks?

Clocks track progress toward outcomes - both PC goals and threats.

Use when: - Something gets worse over time - Progress toward goal needs tracking - Pressure and urgency needed - Offscreen events happening

Don’t use when: - Single check resolves situation - Time pressure not relevant - Would feel arbitrary

Clock Sizes

4-Clock ☐☐☐☐

Use for: - Fast-moving situations - Single session timers - Immediate threats - Quick progress tracking

Examples: - Alarm spreading through building - Bleeding out - Chase scene - Quick research task

6-Clock ☐☐☐☐☐☐

Use for: - Standard progress tracking - Multi-session projects - Medium-term threats - Default clock size

Examples: - Villain’s plan unfolds - PC project (build item, research) - Faction relationships - Investigation progress

8-Clock ☐☐☐☐☐☐☐☐

Use for: - Long-term threats - Campaign-level tracking - Major projects - Slow-burn tension

Examples: - War approaching - Prophecy unfolding - Grand construction - Empire’s decline

10-12-Clock ☐☐☐☐☐☐☐☐☐☐

Use for: - Epic scope - Multiple arcs - Very long-term - Rarely used

Threat Clocks

Template: Villain’s Plan

6-Clock: ☐☐☐☐☐☐

Segments: 1. Plan begins, PCs unaware 2. First signs appear 3. PCs discover threat 4. Escalation, stakes clear 5. Final preparations 6. Plan succeeds (unless PCs stop it)

Advance when: - Session passes (automatic) - PCs ignore clues - Villain acts successfully

Template: Approaching Danger

4-Clock: ☐☐☐☐

Segments: 1. Distant threat detected 2. Threat getting closer 3. Threat nearly here 4. Threat arrives

Advance when: - Time passes - Trigger event occurs - PCs delay

Template: Deteriorating Situation

6-Clock: ☐☐☐☐☐☐

Segments: 1. Situation stable but concerning 2. First cracks appear 3. Problem worsens 4. Crisis point 5. Critical failure imminent 6. Complete breakdown

Advance when: - Problem not addressed - Complications arise - Resources depleted

Template: Pursuit/Escape

4-Clock: ☐☐☐☐

PCs Trying to Escape: 1. Pursuers close behind 2. Gaining/losing ground 3. Final stretch 4. Caught (or escaped, if PCs succeed first)

Advance when: - Pursuers make progress - PCs fail checks - Time passes

Reduce when: - PCs succeed on checks - PCs create obstacles - PCs gain advantages

Progress Clocks

Template: Investigation

6-Clock: ☐☐☐☐☐☐

Segments: 1. First clues gathered 2. Pattern emerges 3. Key insight gained 4. Major breakthrough 5. Nearly solved 6. Mystery solved

Advance when: - PCs find clues - PCs make connections - NPCs provide info

Template: Building/Crafting

6-Clock: ☐☐☐☐☐☐

Segments: 1. Design and planning 2. Gather materials 3. Foundation/structure 4. Detail work 5. Finishing touches 6. Complete

Advance when: - Work session completed - Resources acquired - Skill checks succeed

Template: Relationship Building

6-Clock: ☐☐☐☐☐☐

Segments: 1. First meeting 2. Positive interaction 3. Trust building 4. Friendship forming 5. Deep trust 6. Loyal ally

Advance when: - Help NPC - Share secrets - Demonstrate loyalty - Spend time together

Template: Reputation Change

6-Clock: ☐☐☐☐☐☐

Segments: 1. Unknown 2. Noticed 3. Recognized 4. Respected 5. Renowned 6. Legendary

Advance when: - Complete missions - Heroic acts - Word spreads - Major victories

Competing Clocks

Template: Race

Two 6-clocks side by side:

PC Progress: ☐☐☐☐☐☐ Rival Progress: ☐☐☐☐☐☐

Use for: - Racing to goal - Competing parties - Resource gathering competition

First to fill wins

Template: Tug of War

Single clock, fill from middle:

☐☐☐ [Center] ☐☐☐ PC ← → Enemy

Use for: - Battle of wills - Territory control - Influence struggle

Advances left or right based on actions

Combined Clocks

Template: Heist

Multiple interconnected clocks:

Security Alert: ☐☐☐☐ (threat) Vault Access: ☐☐☐☐☐☐ (progress) Escape Route: ☐☐☐☐ (progress)

If Security fills: Heist becomes much harder Need Vault + Escape both full for clean success

Template: War

Campaign-scale tracking:

Our Army Morale: ☐☐☐☐☐☐ Enemy Advance: ☐☐☐☐☐☐ Civilian Support: ☐☐☐☐☐☐ Supply Lines: ☐☐☐☐☐☐

Each clock affects others

Advancement Triggers

Automatic (Time-Based)

  • Each session: +1 segment
  • Each downtime: +1 segment
  • Each week/month: +1 segment

Action-Based

  • PCs ignore threat: +1 segment
  • Villain acts: +1-2 segments
  • Major event: +2-3 segments

Check-Based (Progress Clocks)

  • Success (10+): +2 segments
  • Partial (7-9): +1 segment
  • Failure (6-): +0 (or threat advances)

Fantasy Clocks

Prophecy Unfolding: ☐☐☐☐☐☐☐☐ 1. First sign appears 2. Second omen 3. Hero revealed 4. Trials begin 5. Sacrifice made 6. Darkness rises 7. Final battle 8. Prophecy fulfilled

The Truth Emerges: ☐☐☐☐☐☐ 1. Something is wrong 2. Clues point somewhere dark 3. Horrible suspicion 4. Undeniable evidence 5. Full horror revealed 6. Must act or succumb

Modern Clocks

Police Investigation: ☐☐☐☐☐☐ 1. Crime discovered 2. Suspects identified 3. Evidence gathered 4. Warrant issued 5. Closing in 6. Arrested

Deadline: ☐☐☐☐ 1. Days away 2. Tomorrow 3. Hours 4. Time’s up

Sci-Fi Clocks

Ship Systems Failing: ☐☐☐☐☐☐ 1. Minor malfunction 2. System degrading 3. Critical warning 4. Cascade failure 5. Life support threatened 6. Total failure

First Contact Protocol: ☐☐☐☐☐☐ 1. Detection 2. Communication attempt 3. Basic understanding 4. Exchange begins 5. Trust building 6. Relations established

Countdown Clocks

Template: Bomb Timer

4-Clock (countdown): 4 ☑ → 3 ☑ → 2 ☑ → 1 ☑ → BOOM

Start full, remove segments

Use for: - Literal timers - Degrading resources - Countdown situations

Clock Decision Tree

START: Need to track something over time? ↓ YES: Is it urgent/immediate? ↓ YES → Use 4-clock NO → Continue ↓ Is it campaign-scale/long-term? ↓ YES → Use 8-clock NO → Use 6-clock (default) ↓ What advances it? - Time → Automatic each session - Actions → When triggered - Checks → On success/failure ↓ What happens when full? - Threat → Bad thing occurs - Progress → Goal achieved - Race → Winner determined ↓ Create clock and start tracking

Using Clocks in Play

Reveal to Players?

Yes (transparent): When… - Progress clocks (their goals) - Creating urgency - Clear time pressure - Tension is the point

No (hidden): When… - Threat clocks (surprises) - Atmosphere over mechanics - Metagaming concern - Reveal at dramatic moment

Partial (hints): When… - Signs of progress - Clues to threat - Mystery unfolding - Building tension

Advancing Clocks

During Session: - Mark immediately when triggered - Announce if transparent - Describe what it means fictionally

Between Sessions: - Automatic advances - Offscreen actions - Time passage - World keeps moving

Modifying Clocks

Can you change clock mid-use? Yes: - Add segments if scope increases - Remove segments if accelerating - Change triggers if makes sense - Adjust based on PC actions

Clocks are tools, not rigid rules

Common Clock Mistakes

Don’t

  • Create too many clocks (max 3-4 active)
  • Advance without fictional cause
  • Use when single check works
  • Make advancement arbitrary
  • Forget about clocks you started

Do

  • Tie to fiction
  • Give players information
  • Advance consistently
  • Complete or retire clocks
  • Let PCs affect advancement

Quick Clock Templates

Immediate Threat (4-clock)

Use for: Single session urgency Advance: Each major scene or check Result: Crisis occurs

Standard Project (6-clock)

Use for: 2-3 session goals Advance: Each successful step Result: Goal achieved

Campaign Threat (8-clock)

Use for: Arc-level danger Advance: Each session or major event Result: Climactic showdown

Combat Actions Quick Reference

Complete reference for all combat actions and rules.

Action Economy

Each turn, you get 2 actions. Choose from: - Move - Attack - Defend - Use Item - Help - Special Action

Plus unlimited reactions (but each costs future action)

Standard Actions

Move

Cost: 1 action Effect: Change position, range, or tactical situation Examples: - Close to melee range - Retreat to cover - Flank enemy - Reach high ground - Create distance

No roll required unless moving through difficult terrain or under fire

Attack

Cost: 1 action Roll: Relevant combat skill + 2d6 vs. DC DC: 8 (standard), +2 if defended/in cover, -2 if exposed/vulnerable

Results: - 10+: Deal damage, no cost to you - 7-9: Deal damage BUT: - Take damage in return (traded blows) - Expend resource (ammunition, spell slot) - Create opening for enemy - Put yourself in danger - 6-: Miss, enemy acts or situation worsens

Damage: 1d6 + weapon bonus + skill modifiers (GM discretion)

Defend

Cost: 1 action Effect: Protect yourself or ally Options: - Take Cover: Enemies have disadvantage to hit you - Brace: +2 to resist being moved or knocked down - Guard Ally: Redirect attack to yourself - Raise Shield: Block next incoming attack

Duration: Until your next turn or until you move

Use Item

Cost: 1 action Examples: - Drink healing potion - Activate magic item - Apply bandage - Use environmental object - Deploy gadget

Roll if uncertain: Appropriate skill (Medicine, Technology, etc.)

Help

Cost: 1 action Effect: Grant advantage to ally’s next action Requirements: Must be able to meaningfully assist Examples: - Create opening in combat - Provide covering fire - Distract enemy - Set up combo attack - Share tactical information

No roll required, but describe how you help

Special Action

Cost: 1 action Examples: - Use proficiency ability - Activate special power - Exploit environmental feature - Attempt creative tactic - Cast spell

Roll: Depends on action, GM sets DC and determines effect

Reactions (Interrupt Enemy Actions)

Cost: Your next action (lose 1 action next turn)

Dodge

Trigger: When targeted by attack Effect: Attacker has disadvantage on attack roll Timing: Declare before attack roll

Counter

Trigger: When hit by attack Effect: Immediately attack back Roll: Standard attack roll Cost: Lose next action entirely

Protect

Trigger: When ally would be hit Effect: Redirect attack to yourself Requirement: Must be in range Cost: Automatically hit, lose next action

Combat Modifiers

Advantage (Roll 3d6, keep highest 2)

Granted by: - Higher ground - Flanking enemy (ally on opposite side) - Enemy unaware or surprised - Target restrained or helpless - Ideal conditions for your approach

Disadvantage (Roll 3d6, keep lowest 2)

Imposed by: - Fighting in darkness/poor visibility - Difficult terrain - Wounded condition - Outnumbered (3+ enemies vs. 1) - Poor tactical position

Range & Movement

Range Bands

Melee: Adjacent, within reach Close: Same room/area, 10-30 feet Near: Shouting distance, 30-60 feet Far: Visible but distant, 60-120 feet Extreme: Edge of perception, 120+ feet

Movement During Combat

One Move action gets you: - Melee → Close - Close → Near - Near → Far - Or significant repositioning within same range band

Difficult Terrain: Requires roll (Athletics, Acrobatics) or costs 2 actions

Cover & Concealment

Light Cover

Examples: Low wall, furniture, partial obstruction Effect: +2 to defense, enemies have disadvantage on ranged attacks

Heavy Cover

Examples: Stone wall, vehicle, solid barrier Effect: Cannot be targeted by ranged attacks unless exposed

Concealment (vs. Cover)

Examples: Darkness, smoke, fog, camouflage Effect: Hard to target (disadvantage), but doesn’t stop attacks

Common Combat Situations

Flanking

Setup: You and ally on opposite sides of enemy Effect: Both gain advantage on attacks

Outnumbered

Setup: 3+ enemies surrounding 1 target Effect: Target has disadvantage on all actions

High Ground

Setup: Elevated position above enemy Effect: Advantage on attacks, +1 to defense

Prone

Setup: Knocked down or lying flat Effect: Disadvantage on attacks, enemies gain advantage (melee) or disadvantage (ranged) Recovery: Costs 1 action to stand

Grappled/Restrained

Setup: Held by enemy or environmental effect Effect: Cannot move, disadvantage on attacks, attackers gain advantage Escape: Athletics or Acrobatics vs. DC 10

Damage & Conditions

Taking Damage

Health tracks harm: - Full HP: No penalty - Half HP: “Bloodied”, describe injuries - Quarter HP: Wounded condition (disadvantage on physical actions) - 0 HP: Incapacitated, risk of death

Healing

Short Rest (scene, 15-30 min): Regain 1d6 HP Long Rest (session end): Regain half max HP Medicine/Magic: Varies by ability, typically 1d6 to 2d6 HP

Combat Conditions

Wounded: -2 to physical actions or disadvantage Stunned: Lose next action Blinded: Disadvantage on attacks, enemies gain advantage Deafened: Cannot hear warnings, -2 to Perception Frightened: Disadvantage while source of fear visible

Special Combat Maneuvers

Called Shot

Effect: Target specific body part or object Mechanic: +2 to DC, enhanced effect on success Examples: - Disarm (remove weapon) - Disable (reduce mobility) - Blind (target eyes) - Destroy object (shatter shield)

All-Out Attack

Effect: Deal extra damage, expose yourself Mechanic: +1d6 damage, but enemy gains advantage against you until your next turn

Defensive Fighting

Effect: Reduce damage taken, sacrifice offense Mechanic: -1d6 damage dealt, gain +2 to defense

Fighting Retreat

Effect: Move while defending Mechanic: Use both actions (Defend + Move), cannot attack

Initiative & Turn Order

Quick Initiative (Default)

PCs act first, resolve simultaneously, then enemies act

Slow Initiative (Dangerous Situation)

Enemies act first, then PCs respond

Negotiated Initiative (Complex Scene)

GM and players discuss who acts when based on fiction

Changing Initiative

Spend action to “Hold”: Delay your turn, act later (interrupt enemy or wait for opening)

Ending Combat

Combat ends when: - All enemies defeated, fled, or surrendered - PCs retreat or escape - Objectives achieved (rescue, retrieve item, etc.) - Narrative resolution (negotiation, third party intervenes)

Don’t fight to zero HP - allow surrender, retreat, or dramatic conclusions

Quick Combat Checklist

Start of Combat: 1. ☐ Establish fiction (positions, terrain, goals) 2. ☐ Determine initiative 3. ☐ Clarify stakes (what happens if PCs lose)

During Each Turn: 1. ☐ Describe action in fiction first 2. ☐ GM determines mechanic (roll? which skill? DC?) 3. ☐ Resolve roll and describe outcome 4. ☐ Apply damage/conditions 5. ☐ Update tactical situation

End of Combat: 1. ☐ Describe outcome and consequences 2. ☐ Allow short rest if appropriate 3. ☐ Loot/rewards/information 4. ☐ Transition to next scene

Quick Reference Version: 1.0.0 Part of: Mechanics Reference (3-Book Structure) See Also: - Chapter 19 (Combat Reference) - Full combat rules - Chapter 03 (Check Resolution) - Core dice mechanics - Chapter 04 (Tags & Conditions) - Condition effects - Player’s Handbook Chapter 08 (Combat Basics) - Player-facing combat guide - GM’s Toolkit Chapter 11 (Combat for GMs) - Running combat scenes

DC Guidelines Table

Comprehensive reference for setting Difficulty Classes.

Core DC Scale

DC Difficulty When to Use Success Rate*
6 Easy Routine task, favorable conditions, trained ~95%
8 Moderate Standard challenge for competent character ~85%
10 Challenging Difficult but achievable, requires skill ~70%
12 Hard Requires expertise, unfavorable conditions ~50%
14 Very Hard Exceptional skill needed, serious obstacles ~30%
16 Extreme Near impossible for most, only masters succeed ~15%
18+ Legendary Should only be possible for tier 4 characters <5%

*Success rate assumes +2 skill bonus (competent character)

Setting DCs by Situation

By Character Capability

Novice (Tier 1, +0 to +2): - Routine: DC 6 - Challenge: DC 8-10 - Hard limit: DC 12

Competent (Tier 2, +2 to +3): - Routine: DC 8 - Challenge: DC 10-12 - Hard limit: DC 14

Expert (Tier 3, +3 to +4): - Routine: DC 10 - Challenge: DC 12-14 - Hard limit: DC 16

Master (Tier 4, +4 to +5): - Routine: DC 12 - Challenge: DC 14-16 - Hard limit: DC 18+

Contextual Modifiers

Increase DC (+2 to +4)

+2 when: - Time pressure or rushed - Poor tools or missing equipment - Distractions or interruptions - Unfamiliar environment

+4 when: - Active opposition - Extreme conditions (storm, darkness, etc.) - Severe injury or exhaustion - Critical stakes create pressure

Decrease DC (-2 to -4)

-2 when: - Ample time and preparation - Perfect tools and resources - Ideal conditions - Expert assistance available

-4 when: - Previous practice or rehearsal - Custom tools designed for task - Multiple experts assisting - Perfect circumstances align

Advantage/Disadvantage Instead

Grant Advantage rather than lowering DC when: - Situational benefit that doesn’t change task difficulty - Clever approach or good planning - Ally assistance - Using relevant proficiency creatively

Impose Disadvantage rather than raising DC when: - Situational hindrance - Improvising or lacking preparation - Injured or impaired - Environmental distraction

DCs by Task Type

Physical Tasks

Climbing: - DC 6: Ladder, rope with knots, easy tree - DC 8: Rough wall with handholds, sturdy tree - DC 10: Smooth wall with cracks, difficult climb - DC 12: Nearly sheer surface, minimal handholds - DC 14+: Perfectly smooth, overhang, extreme

Jumping: - DC 6: 5-foot gap, 3-foot high obstacle - DC 8: 10-foot gap, 5-foot high obstacle - DC 10: 15-foot gap, 7-foot high obstacle - DC 12: 20-foot gap, 10-foot high obstacle - DC 14+: 25+ foot gap, 12+ foot high obstacle

Breaking Objects: - DC 6: Glass, thin wood, rope - DC 8: Door, wooden crate, simple lock - DC 10: Reinforced door, iron bars, quality lock - DC 12: Stone wall, steel door, master lock - DC 14+: Reinforced steel, magical barrier

Social Tasks

Persuasion: - DC 6: Friendly target, simple request - DC 8: Neutral target, reasonable request - DC 10: Wary target, difficult request - DC 12: Hostile target, unreasonable request - DC 14+: Enemy, impossible request

Deception: - DC 6: Trusting target, small lie - DC 8: Neutral target, believable lie - DC 10: Cautious target, significant lie - DC 12: Suspicious target, major deception - DC 14+: Expert lie detector, absurd claim

Intimidation: - DC 6: Already frightened target - DC 8: Vulnerable target, clear threat - DC 10: Equal target, serious threat - DC 12: Confident target, difficult to scare - DC 14+: Fearless target, immune to threats

Knowledge and Investigation

Recall Information: - DC 6: Common knowledge - DC 8: Specialized but well-known - DC 10: Obscure but documented - DC 12: Rare, requires research - DC 14+: Secret, lost, or forbidden

Find Hidden Object: - DC 6: Poorly concealed, obvious - DC 8: Basic concealment - DC 10: Well hidden, requires search - DC 12: Expert concealment - DC 14+: Nearly impossible to find

Deduce Connection: - DC 6: Obvious pattern - DC 8: Clear with attention - DC 10: Requires analysis - DC 12: Subtle, easy to miss - DC 14+: Obscure, genius-level deduction

Technical and Crafting

Repair: - DC 6: Simple fix, obvious problem - DC 8: Standard repair, clear damage - DC 10: Complex repair, diagnosis needed - DC 12: Extensive damage, skilled work - DC 14+: Nearly destroyed, master craftsman

Create Item: - DC 6: Simple, common item - DC 8: Standard quality item - DC 10: Fine quality item - DC 12: Masterwork item - DC 14+: Legendary quality

Operate Unfamiliar Device: - DC 6: Simple, intuitive interface - DC 8: Standard device, clear controls - DC 10: Complex device, learning curve - DC 12: Advanced system, training needed - DC 14+: Alien/magical, incomprehensible

Genre-Specific DCs

Cozy Genre

Lower stakes = lower base DCs: - Most tasks: DC 6-8 - Challenges: DC 8-10 - Significant challenges: DC 10-12 - Rarely above DC 12

Focus: Success with cost, not outright failure

Fantasy Genre

Traditional scaling: - Use standard DC table - Magic may modify DCs - Legendary tasks common at high tier

Horror/Mystery Genre

Information gathering easier, action harder: - Clues and investigation: DC 6-8 - Physical challenges: DC 10-12 - Escape/survival: DC 12-14

Focus: Players should find information; acting on it is dangerous

Modern Genre

Realistic difficulty: - Use standard DC table - Technology can lower DCs - Firearms and modern threats are deadly

Sci-Fi Genre

Technology shifts baseline: - Basic tasks easier with tech: -2 DC - Advanced tasks possible - Alien tech raises DCs significantly

Competitive Checks

PC vs PC

Both roll, higher result wins Tie: Status quo or both partially succeed

PC vs NPC

PC rolls vs NPC’s skill as DC: - NPC passive skill: 7 + skill bonus - NPC active opposition: NPC rolls, becomes DC

Example: - Stealth vs Perception - Deception vs Insight - Sleight of Hand vs Perception

Group Checks

Everyone Must Succeed

Each PC rolls individually: - All must meet DC - Used when everyone faces same challenge - Example: Climbing a wall

Majority Must Succeed

Half or more must succeed: - Group succeeds if majority does - Used for team efforts - Example: Sneaking past guard

Assist Checks

One PC rolls with advantage: - Others provide help - Only one check made - Used when one person leads

Automatic Success/Failure

Automatic Success

Don’t roll when: - Task is trivial for character’s skill level - No time pressure - Failure wouldn’t be interesting - DC would be 4 or lower

Example: Expert climber with rope and time doesn’t roll for easy climb

Automatic Failure

Don’t roll when: - Task is impossible - No chance even with perfect roll - Success wouldn’t be interesting

Example: Can’t persuade someone to commit suicide

Success with Consequences (7-9 Result)

Common Consequences

Cost: - Use resource (HP, item, time, favor) - Expend limited ability - Pay price in some way

Complication: - New problem arises - Situation becomes more complex - Future challenges created

Compromise: - Get less than wanted - Partial information - Incomplete solution

Attention: - Success but noticed - Alert enemies - Lose stealth/surprise

Risk: - Exposed to danger - Vulnerable position - Create opening for enemy

Interpreting Results

By Roll Total

10+: Full success, no downside - Achieve goal completely - No complications - May get bonus on exceptional roll (13+)

7-9: Success with cost - Achieve goal but… - Choose from consequence list - GM offers hard bargain

6 or less: Failure but… - Story moves forward - Learn something - New opportunity emerges - Not dead end

Quick DC Decision Tree

Step 1: What tier are the PCs? - Tier 1: Base DC 8-10 - Tier 2: Base DC 10-12 - Tier 3: Base DC 12-14 - Tier 4: Base DC 14-16

Step 2: How difficult for that tier? - Easy: -2 - Standard: +0 - Hard: +2 - Very hard: +4

Step 3: Situational modifiers? - Good conditions: -2 - Bad conditions: +2 - Active opposition: +2 to +4

Step 4: Result - Final DC set - Roll and resolve

Common Pitfalls

Don’t

  • Set DC too high for tier
  • Require roll for trivial task
  • Use DC when opposed check better
  • Forget about advantage/disadvantage
  • Make critical path impossible

Do

  • Match DC to party tier
  • Use auto-success for mundane
  • Offer multiple paths to goal
  • Consider advantage over DC changes
  • Make failure interesting

DC by Frequency

Often (Every Session)

  • DC 8: Standard challenges
  • DC 10: Meaningful challenges
  • Players should succeed most of time

Sometimes (Every 2-3 Sessions)

  • DC 12: Hard challenges
  • DC 14: Very hard challenges
  • Should feel significant when overcome

Rarely (Once per arc)

  • DC 16: Extreme challenges
  • DC 18: Legendary challenges
  • Epic moments, major milestones

Quick Selection by Archetype

Combat-Focused Characters

Pick 2-3 from: Relevant combat skills + Athletics + Perception Example (Fantasy): Melee Combat +2, Ranged Combat +1, Athletics +1, Perception +1

Social/Diplomatic Characters

Pick 2-3 from: Persuasion/Diplomacy + Insight/Reading People + Knowledge Example (Modern): Negotiation +2, Psychology +1, Investigation +1

Technical/Crafting Characters

Pick 2-3 from: Primary craft + Related knowledge + Perception Example (Sci-Fi): Engineering +2, Robotics +1, Analysis +1

Stealth/Infiltration Characters

Pick 2-3 from: Stealth + Social deception + Investigation Example (Horror): Stealth +2, Deception +1, Investigation +1

Support/Healing Characters

Pick 2-3 from: Medicine/Healing + Empathy + Knowledge Example (Cozy): Medicine +2, Empathy +1, Herbalism +1

Investigation Characters

Pick 2-3 from: Investigation/Perception + Knowledge + Social Example (Modern): Investigation +2, Forensics +1, Interrogation +1

Skill Ratings

Starting Characters (Tier 1): - Distribute 5 points among 3-5 skills - No skill above +2 at start - Common distribution: +2, +1, +1, +1

Progression: - Tier 1: Skills at +0 to +2 - Tier 2: Skills at +1 to +3 - Tier 3: Skills at +2 to +4 - Tier 4+: Skills at +3 to +5

Combining Skills

When tasks require multiple areas of expertise, combine skills:

Example Combinations: - Combat + Acrobatics: Flashy, mobile fighting - Stealth + Perception: Careful infiltration - Knowledge + Social: Teaching or convincing with expertise - Technical + Investigation: Analyzing complex systems - Survival + Medicine: Field medical care

Mechanic: Use higher skill, gain advantage if both relevant

Porting Skills Between Genres

To convert character to different genre:

  1. Identify skill function (combat, social, knowledge, etc.)
  2. Find equivalent in new genre (use table above)
  3. Keep same ratings
  4. Adjust flavor to fit new setting

Example: Fantasy Ranger → Modern Survival Expert - Survival (Fantasy) → Survival (Modern) - Tracking (Fantasy) → Tracking (Modern) - Ranged Combat (Fantasy) → Firearms (Modern) - Nature Lore (Fantasy) → Wilderness Knowledge (Modern)
